Legal Philosophy and Theory
beginner
8 min
From Custom to Codification: The Evolution of Positive Law
Expansion probe on the evolution of positive law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the process by which positive laws develop from customs to codified statutes, and contrast this with the divine origin of Islamic Sharia.

Core Idea
Positive law evolves from custom to codification; Islamic Sharia is divinely revealed but accommodates custom within its framework.

This lesson examines the first section of al-Zarqa's introduction, which traces the evolution of human-made laws. Understanding this process helps us appreciate the unique nature of Sharia as divine law and the role of custom (urf) in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stages
Read the passage and note the three stages: custom as law, codification, and legislation with general principles.
The passage uses the term 'شرائع وضعية' for positive laws.
2
Understand the role of custom
Before codification, custom was the law itself. After codification, custom becomes an assistant to the law, filling details.
In Hanafi fiqh, custom (urf) is a source for rulings when no clear text exists.
3
Contrast with Sharia
Islamic Sharia is revealed by Allah, not evolved from custom. However, Urf is recognized as a secondary source in the Hanafi school.
Sharia's primary sources are Quran and Sunnah.
4
Apply to Hanafi fiqh
Consider how the Hanafi school uses custom in areas like transactions (muamalat) where local customs may determine contractual terms.
Example: the permissibility of certain sales based on common practice.

Source Text

الفصل الأول تمهيد
نظرة عامة إلى الشريعة الإسلامية بين يدي هذه السلسلة الفقهية
1 - ضوء على الشرائع الوضعية بوجه عام.
2 - ضوء على الشرائع الإلهية.
- الإطار العام للشريعة الإسلامية أو نظام الإسلام.
4 - النظام القانوني في الشريعة الإسلامية.
أهم المبادىء الأساسية في هذا النظام القانوني من الشريعة الإسلامية.
أولاً - قسم الحقوق الخاصة:
-
في حقوق الأسرة (الأحوال الشخصية).
- في الزواج وانحلاله وآثارهما .
- الولاية والوصاية .
- الميراث .
ب - في الحقوق المدنية (المعاملات).
ج - في الحقوق الجنائية (الجرائم والعقوبات).
ثانياً - قسم الحقوق العامة:
1
ا - في الحقوق الداخلية .
في الناحية الدستورية
- في الناحية الإدارية.
في الناحية المالية العامة.
ب - في الحقوق الخارجية (الدولية العامة).

الباب الأول: تمهيد وتعريف وتقسيم
ضوء على الشرائع الوضعية بوجه عام
١/١ - إنَّ الشرائع الوضعية، أو القوانين في المجتمعات البشرية البدائية تتكون تدريجياً في صورة عادات وأعراف. ثم إذا ارتقت حياة الأمة ومداركها ومعارفها وأصبحت لها سلطة حاكمة وسيادة، تلجأ إلى تقنين Codification تلك العادات وتجعل منها نظاماً آمراً في أعمال الناس ومعاملاتهم وعلائقهم فيحل القانون محل تلك العادات وينسخ اعتبارها، فيلغي منها ما يرى غير صالح ويثبت ما يرى صالحاً، وتصبح العبرة لنصوص القانون وروحه ومقاصد شارعه .
ثم يرتقي التشريع فيضع الأسس الحقوقية والقواعد القانونية العامة تاركاً للأعراف والعادات تخطيط الحدود الفاصلة للحقوق والالتزامات المتقابلة في التفاصيل الجزئية التي يعسر على الشارع استقصاؤها بالنصوص وليس من المستحسن فيها ذلك الاستقصاء.
يعود
وهكذا بارتقاء التشريع ودخوله في طور تقعيد القواعد العامة للعرف والعادات اعتبار في نطاق تقوم فيه بوظيفة المساعد للقانون، غير ميدانها الأول الذي كانت فيه هي القانون.
۲/۱ - وأهم مراحل الرقي التشريعي هي المرحلة التي تجسد فيها الإرادات الحقوقية المسماة بالعقود، وتنظم وتحترم نتائجها، وتطلق فيها حرية التعاقد والمشارطات في كل ما لا يخل بالنظام العام والآداب، ومقاصد الشارع الأساسية.
ثم في الأمم ذات التراث التشريعي الراقي يوجد إلى جانب نصوص القوانين فقه للعلماء المتشرّعين أي نظريات حقوقية وشروح)، واجتهاد للقضاة في فهم النصوص وتطبيقها والقياس عليها بصورة توسع استيعاب النصوص المحدودة إلى آفاق غير محدودة بمراعاة العلل التي راعاها الشارع، وتحكيم دلائل نصوصه.
Chapter One: Introduction
A general overview of Islamic Sharia before this jurisprudential series.
1 - Light on positive laws in general.
2 - Light on divine laws.
- The general framework of Islamic Sharia or the system of Islam.
4 - The legal system in Islamic Sharia.
The most important basic principles in this legal system from Islamic Sharia.
First: Section of private rights:
-
In family rights (personal status).
- In marriage, its dissolution and effects.
- Guardianship and trusteeship.
- Inheritance.
B - In civil rights (transactions).
C - In penal rights (crimes and punishments).
Second: Section of public rights:
1
A - In internal rights.
In the constitutional aspect.
- In the administrative aspect.
In the public financial aspect.
B - In external rights (public international).

Chapter One: Introduction, Definition, and Division
Light on positive laws in general.
1/1 - Indeed, positive laws, or laws in primitive human societies, gradually take shape in the form of customs and traditions. Then, when the life of the nation, its intellect, and its knowledge advance, and it has a ruling authority and sovereignty, it resorts to the codification of those customs and makes them an imperative system in the actions, transactions, and relations of people. So law replaces those customs and abrogates their consideration, canceling what it deems unsound and affirming what it deems sound, and the criterion becomes the texts of the law, its spirit, and the objectives of its legislator.
Then legislation rises, laying down the legal foundations and general legal rules, leaving to customs and traditions the demarcation of the boundaries of rights and reciprocal obligations in the partial details that are difficult for the legislator to cover with texts and for which such coverage is not advisable.
Thus, with the rise of legislation and its entry into the phase of codifying general rules, custom and tradition gain consideration within a scope where they perform the function of an assistant to the law, unlike their initial field where they themselves were the law.
2/1 - And the most important stages of legislative advancement are the stage in which legal wills called contracts are embodied, their results are organized and respected, and freedom of contract and stipulations is unleashed in everything that does not violate public order, morals, and the basic objectives of the legislator.
Then, in nations with a high legislative heritage, alongside the texts of laws, there exists jurisprudence of legist scholars (i.e., legal theories and commentaries), and ijtihad of judges in understanding texts, applying them, and reasoning by analogy upon them in a way that expands the comprehension of limited texts to unlimited horizons by observing the ratio legis that the legislator observed, and giving weight to the evidences of His texts.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is an introductory overview from the book 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Am' by Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It sets the stage for the study of Islamic fiqh by first discussing the nature of positive (man-made) law and its evolution from custom to codification. The author highlights that in primitive societies, law emerged as customs, which later become codified by a ruling authority. He then notes that advanced legislation establishes general legal principles, leaving details to custom. In the Hanafi tradition, custom (urf) plays a significant role in legal rulings, especially in transactions. The passage then lists the main divisions of Islamic Sharia: private rights (family, civil, penal) and public rights (constitutional, administrative, financial, international). This classification reflects the comprehensive nature of Sharia. The author also emphasizes the importance of legal theory (fiqh) and judicial ijtihad in expanding the application of texts through analogy (qiyas) and consideration of legal causes (illal). This aligns with Hanafi usul al-fiqh, which extensively uses qiyas and istihsan. The passage is a preparatory overview, not a specific ruling, so no authority weight is assigned.

Key Terms
الشرائع الوضعية (ash-sharā'iʿ al-waḍʿiyyah): Laws that are created by human societies (positive law), as opposed to divine revelation.
التشريع (at-tashrīʿ): The process of legislation, including the formulation and codification of laws.
العرف (al-urf): Custom or tradition, considered a subsidiary source of law in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first stage of development of positive laws?
A) Codification of laws
B) Existence as customs and traditions
C) Judicial ijtihad
D) Legislative assemblies

Key Takeaway
Positive law evolves from custom to codification, while Sharia is divine but incorporates custom as a subsidiary source in the Hanafi school.

The Comprehensive Structure of Islamic Sharia
Expansion probe on the structure of Sharia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the main divisions of Islamic Sharia as private and public rights, and give examples of each from the Hanafi fiqh perspective.

Core Idea
Islamic Sharia comprehensively covers both private and public rights, regulating all aspects of life.

This lesson focuses on the latter part of al-Zarqa's introduction, where he outlines the structure of Sharia. Understanding this classification helps a mufti locate the relevant fiqh rules for any given issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions
According to the passage, Sharia is divided into private rights (huquq khassah) and public rights (huquq ammah).
This classification is common in modern Islamic legal studies.
2
Break down private rights
Private rights include family law (marriage, divorce, guardianship, inheritance), civil law (transactions), and penal law (crimes and punishments).
In Hanafi fiqh, these are found in books like al-Hidayah.
3
Break down public rights
Public rights include constitutional, administrative, financial, and international aspects.
These correspond to modern categories like constitutional law and public finance.
4
Relate to Hanafi fiqh
Hanafi fiqh texts traditionally cover these areas, with detailed rulings on each.
For example, the chapter on siyar covers international law.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
beginner
8 min
Evolution of Positive Laws: From Custom to Codification
Overview of the development of man-made legal systems

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the three stages of legal development—custom, codification, and the subsidiary role of custom—and explain how each stage relates to the authority of the legislator.

Core Idea
Man-made laws begin as customs, then become codified; after codification, custom serves only as a subsidiary source.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's introduction provides a comparative framework for understanding how all legal systems, including Islamic Sharia, develop. However, the author emphasizes that Islamic Sharia's universal principles set it apart. In this lesson, we focus on the first part: the natural evolution of positive laws from customs to codified legislation. You will learn the typical trajectory of human legal systems and the role of juristic reasoning (fiqh and ijtihad) once legislation matures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial source of law
The text states that laws begin as customs and traditions in primitive societies. Recognize that there is no formal legislation at this stage.
2
Trace the transition to codification
As a society advances, it codifies its customs into imperative legal texts. Note that this process involves selecting which customs to retain and which to discard.
3
Distinguish the new role of custom
Once codified, custom does not disappear but becomes a subsidiary source, filling in details that the legislator cannot cover with texts.
4
Recognize the emergence of fiqh and ijtihad
In advanced legal systems, alongside texts, scholars develop jurisprudence (fiqh) and judges practice ijtihad, expanding the law through reasoning and analogy. This includes the recognition of contracts and freedom of contract within public order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الأول: تمهيد وتعريف وتقسيم
ضوء على الشرائع الوضعية بوجه عام
١/١ - إنَّ الشرائع الوضعية، أو القوانين في المجتمعات البشرية البدائية تتكون تدريجياً في صورة عادات وأعراف. ثم إذا ارتقت حياة الأمة ومداركها ومعارفها وأصبحت لها سلطة حاكمة وسيادة، تلجأ إلى تقنين Codification تلك العادات وتجعل منها نظاماً آمراً في أعمال الناس ومعاملاتهم وعلائقهم فيحل القانون محل تلك العادات وينسخ اعتبارها، فيلغي منها ما يرى غير صالح ويثبت ما يرى صالحاً، وتصبح العبرة لنصوص القانون وروحه ومقاصد شارعه .

وهكذا بارتقاء التشريع ودخوله في طور تقعيد القواعد العامة للعرف والعادات اعتبار في نطاق تقوم فيه بوظيفة المساعد للقانون، غير ميدانها الأول الذي كانت فيه هي القانون.

ثم في الأمم ذات التراث التشريعي الراقي يوجد إلى جانب نصوص القوانين فقه للعلماء المتشرّعين أي نظريات حقوقية وشروح)، واجتهاد للقضاة في فهم النصوص وتطبيقها والقياس عليها بصورة توسع استيعاب النصوص المحدودة إلى آفاق غير محدودة بمراعاة العلل التي راعاها الشارع، وتحكيم دلائل نصوصه.

الفصل (۱) نظرة عامة إلى الشريعة الإسلامية
٣/١ - فالشرع بوجه عام في أمة من الأمم ليس إلا صورة صحيحة لحياة اجتماعية واقعية وهدفه العام فيها إقامة العدل وحفظ التوازن في الحقوق والالتزامات وصيانة حقوق الناس الفردية، ومصالح المجتمع بقواعد قانونية وهذه القواعد تكون وقتية غير صالحة للخلود إذا كانت تعبر عن أوضاع خاصة عرفية إقليمية، وتكون صالحة للخلود إذا كانت تعبر عن مفاهيم وحقائق مُسَلَّمة ثابتة عالمية كقاعدة منع الضرر وإيجاب التعويض عنه، وقاعدة عدم سريان حكم العقد على غير عاقديه، وعدم تأثيره في حقوق الغير .
فالتشريع واقع اجتماعي واقتصادي في الأمة، ويعبر عما وصلت إليه الحياة فيها من التطور والإدراك الاجتماعي. وبقدر ما فيه من قواعد ذات مفاهيم تشريعية عامة عالمية الاعتبار، وبقدر ما فيه من توجيه للأمة نحو الإصلاح المستمر، تكون درجة رقي ذلك الشرع وصلاحية مبادئه للخلود.
وليس اختلاف الشرائع بين الأمم إلا تعبيراً عن الاختلاف في الحياة الاجتماعية والاقتصادية فيما بينها ؛ وفي الأهداف التي تتجه نحوها هذه الحياة، وفي المثل العليا Ideals التي تستلهمها الأمة وتستوحيها من عقيدتها. فعندما تبلغ الأمة ذروة الرقي الفكري تبلغ تلك القواعد التشريعية لديها منتهاها من الرقي والقوة وتتمتع بالخلود. فالأمة الرومانية مثلاً لما كانت تعيش حياة منعزلة عن العالم منكمشة على عادات خاصة بها كانت قوانينها مجرد عادات ثم شرعاً موقتاً لحياة محدودة ولما خرجت من موطنها الأصلي واندفعت نحو الشرق، وحكمت عناصر أخرى من الناس، اضطرت للخروج من نطاق شرعها الضيق، فتطوّر وتوسع ولكنه بقي موقوتاً غير صالح للخلود إذ كان لهم شرعان أحدهما يتمتع به الرومان الأصليون، والآخر الأجانب. حتى إذا بلغوا مستوى عالياً في الرقي المدني والفكري بعد القرن الثالث للميلاد، وثبتوا قدمهم الثانية في الشرق الأوسط مهد الحضارات القديمة
Chapter One: Introduction, Definition, and Classification
Light on Positive Laws in General
1/1 - Indeed, positive laws, or laws in primitive human societies, gradually form in the form of customs and traditions. Then, when the life of the nation and its perceptions and knowledge advance and it has a governing authority and sovereignty, it resorts to codification of those customs and makes them an imperative system in people's works, transactions, and relations. Thus, law replaces those customs and abrogates their consideration, canceling what is deemed unsuitable and affirming what is deemed suitable, and the criterion becomes the texts of the law, its spirit, and the intentions of its legislator.
Then legislation advances and lays down the legal foundations and general legal principles, leaving to customs and traditions the delineation of the boundaries separating rights and reciprocal obligations in the partial details that the legislator finds difficult to cover with texts and for which such coverage is not advisable.
Thus, with the advancement of legislation and its entering into the stage of establishing general rules, custom and tradition gain consideration within a scope where they perform the function of assistant to the law, not their original field where they were the law.
2/1 - And the most important stages of legislative advancement are the stage in which legal wills called contracts are embodied, their results are organized and respected, and freedom of contract and stipulations is released in everything that does not violate public order, morals, and the basic intentions of the legislator.
Then, in nations with a high legislative heritage, alongside the legal texts, there is fiqh (jurisprudence) of the legal scholars (i.e., legal theories and commentaries), and ijtihad of judges in understanding the texts, applying them, and analogizing from them in a manner that expands the limited texts' capacity to unlimited horizons by considering the causes (illal) that the legislator considered, and implementing the evidences of his texts.

Chapter (1) General View of Islamic Sharia
3/1 - So, law in general in any nation is but a true picture of a real social life, and its general goal therein is to establish justice, maintain balance in rights and obligations, and protect individual rights and societal interests through legal rules. These rules are temporary and not fit for eternity if they express specific regional customary conditions, and they are fit for eternity if they express universal, settled concepts and facts, such as the rule of preventing harm and obligating compensation for it, and the rule that a contract's ruling does not extend to non-parties and does not affect the rights of others.
Thus, legislation is a social and economic reality in the nation, and it expresses the development and social awareness that life has reached. The degree of advancement of that law and the suitability of its principles for eternity is proportional to the extent of general universal legislative concepts it contains and the extent of its guidance of the nation towards continuous reform.
And the difference in laws among nations is but an expression of the difference in social and economic life among them; in the goals towards which this life is directed, and in the ideals that the nation draws inspiration from its creed. So when a nation reaches the peak of intellectual advancement, those legislative rules reach their peak of advancement and strength and enjoy eternity. For example, the Roman nation, when it lived an isolated life from the world, confined to its own customs, its laws were mere customs, then a temporary law for a limited life. When it left its original homeland and pushed towards the east, and ruled other elements of people, it was forced to exit the narrow scope of its law, so it developed and expanded, but it remained temporary, not fit for eternity, as they had two laws: one enjoyed by the original Romans, and the other for foreigners. Until after the third century AD, when they reached a high level of civil and intellectual advancement, and established their second foothold in the Middle East, the cradle of ancient civilizations.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical introduction comparing the development of positive (man-made) law with the characteristics of Islamic Sharia. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by describing how in primitive societies, law emerges gradually as customs and traditions. As a nation advances and develops a governing authority, these customs are codified into formal legal codes, replacing the original customs. Over time, legislation matures: it lays down general principles and leaves detailed boundaries to custom, which now plays a supportive role. The author highlights that the highest stage of legislative development is when contracts are recognized and freedom of contract is permitted within public order and the legislator's basic objectives. In advanced legal systems, alongside legal texts there exists fiqh (jurisprudence) from scholars and ijtihad from judges, expanding the law through reasoning and analogy by considering underlying causes (illal).

The author then turns to a general view of Islamic Sharia (chapter 1). He states that any legal system reflects the real social life of a nation, aiming at justice, balance of rights and obligations, and protection of individual and societal interests. Rules that are merely regional and customary are temporary and not eternal. Eternal rules are those based on universal settled concepts, such as the prohibition of harm and the requirement of compensation, and the principle that a contract only binds its parties and does not affect third parties. The advancement of a legal system is measured by the universality of its principles and its orientation toward continuous reform. Differences among legal systems stem from differences in social and economic life, goals, and ideals derived from each nation's creed. The author uses the example of Roman law: initially isolated and custom-based, it became temporary and dual (one for Romans, one for foreigners). Only after the third century AD, when Rome reached high intellectual civilization and expanded into the Middle East, did its law develop, but it still lacked eternity because it remained tied to particular circumstances. In contrast, Islamic Sharia, though not explicitly mentioned in this passage, is implied to contain such universal principles that fit for all times and places. This sets the stage for the detailed study of Islamic legal principles.

Key Terms
الشرائع الوضعية (al-sharā'i‘ al-waḍ‘iyyah): Positive laws; man-made laws that develop gradually from customs and are codified by a governing authority.
تقنين (taqnīn): Codification; the process of transforming customs into formal, imperative legal texts.
فقه (fiqh): Jurisprudence; the body of legal theories and commentaries developed by scholars alongside legal texts.
اجتهاد (ijtihād): Independent legal reasoning by judges or scholars to interpret texts and apply analogies.
عرف (‘urf): Custom; established practice that may become a source of law, especially when not overridden by codified texts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to customs after they are codified into law?
A) They are completely replaced and have no further role.
B) They become subsidiary sources assisting the law.
C) They remain primary but are modified.
D) They are abolished.

Key Takeaway
Positive laws evolve from custom to codification, and custom then serves as a subsidiary source; fiqh and ijtihad expand the law beyond its texts.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
The Universal Principles of Islamic Sharia: Eternal Rules Beyond Time and Place
Comparative view of temporary man-made laws and eternal principles in Islamic Sharia

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between temporary regional rules and eternal universal rules, and identify the examples of universal principles given in the passage.

Core Idea
Legal rules are eternal if they express universal concepts like preventing harm; regional customs are temporary.

In this lesson, we examine the final part of the passage where Mustafa al-Zarqa discusses what makes a legal system eternal. He contrasts temporary positive laws, which are tied to specific regional customs, with universal principles that transcend time and place. Islamic Sharia, he implies, contains such universal principles. We will focus on the criteria for eternal rules and the examples provided.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of law in any society
The text says law aims to establish justice, maintain balance, and protect individual and societal interests. This is a universal goal.
2
Distinguish temporary from eternal rules
Temporary rules express specific regional customs; eternal rules express universal settled concepts and facts. The passage gives examples.
3
List the examples of universal principles
The passage explicitly mentions: (1) the rule of preventing harm and obligating compensation, and (2) the rule that a contract does not bind non-parties and does not affect third-party rights.
4
Explain why these principles are eternal
These principles are based on universal rational truths, not on particular circumstances of a specific time and place. They are applicable in any society.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الأول: تمهيد وتعريف وتقسيم
ضوء على الشرائع الوضعية بوجه عام
١/١ - إنَّ الشرائع الوضعية، أو القوانين في المجتمعات البشرية البدائية تتكون تدريجياً في صورة عادات وأعراف. ثم إذا ارتقت حياة الأمة ومداركها ومعارفها وأصبحت لها سلطة حاكمة وسيادة، تلجأ إلى تقنين Codification تلك العادات وتجعل منها نظاماً آمراً في أعمال الناس ومعاملاتهم وعلائقهم فيحل القانون محل تلك العادات وينسخ اعتبارها، فيلغي منها ما يرى غير صالح ويثبت ما يرى صالحاً، وتصبح العبرة لنصوص القانون وروحه ومقاصد شارعه .
ثم يرتقي التشريع فيضع الأسس الحقوقية والقواعد القانونية العامة تاركاً للأعراف والعادات تخطيط الحدود الفاصلة للحقوق والالتزامات المتقابلة في التفاصيل الجزئية التي يعسر على الشارع استقصاؤها بالنصوص وليس من المستحسن فيها ذلك الاستقصاء.
وهكذا بارتقاء التشريع ودخوله في طور تقعيد القواعد العامة للعرف والعادات اعتبار في نطاق تقوم فيه بوظيفة المساعد للقانون، غير ميدانها الأول الذي كانت فيه هي القانون.
۲/۱ - وأهم مراحل الرقي التشريعي هي المرحلة التي تجسد فيها الإرادات الحقوقية المسماة بالعقود، وتنظم وتحترم نتائجها، وتطلق فيها حرية التعاقد والمشارطات في كل ما لا يخل بالنظام العام والآداب، ومقاصد الشارع الأساسية.
ثم في الأمم ذات التراث التشريعي الراقي يوجد إلى جانب نصوص القوانين فقه للعلماء المتشرّعين أي نظريات حقوقية وشروح)، واجتهاد للقضاة في فهم النصوص وتطبيقها والقياس عليها بصورة توسع استيعاب النصوص المحدودة إلى آفاق غير محدودة بمراعاة العلل التي راعاها الشارع، وتحكيم دلائل نصوصه.

الفصل (۱) نظرة عامة إلى الشريعة الإسلامية
٣/١ - فالشرع بوجه عام في أمة من الأمم ليس إلا صورة صحيحة لحياة اجتماعية واقعية وهدفه العام فيها إقامة العدل وحفظ التوازن في الحقوق والالتزامات وصيانة حقوق الناس الفردية، ومصالح المجتمع بقواعد قانونية وهذه القواعد تكون وقتية غير صالحة للخلود إذا كانت تعبر عن أوضاع خاصة عرفية إقليمية، وتكون صالحة للخلود إذا كانت تعبر عن مفاهيم وحقائق مُسَلَّمة ثابتة عالمية كقاعدة منع الضرر وإيجاب التعويض عنه، وقاعدة عدم سريان حكم العقد على غير عاقديه، وعدم تأثيره في حقوق الغير .

According to the passage, one example of a universal legal principle that makes a rule eternal is:
A) Codification by a strong state.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
beginner
8 min
Law as a Reflection of Society: Eternal vs Temporary Rules
Philosophical analysis of law's nature and universality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between eternal and temporary legal rules based on their source and universality, and apply this distinction to identify the nature of Islamic Sharia.

Core Idea
Legal rules based on universal truths are eternal; those based on local custom are temporary.

In this passage, the author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, presents a philosophical framework for understanding law in any nation. He argues that law reflects the real social life of a people, and its rules can be either temporary (if based on particular customs) or eternal (if based on universal concepts like preventing harm). This lesson explores that distinction and its implications for Islamic Sharia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim about law and society
Read the first paragraph: law is a correct picture of social life, aiming at justice. Then note the distinction between temporary and eternal rules.
Focus on the phrases 'وقتية غير صالحة للخلود' and 'صالحة للخلود'.
2
Examine the examples given for eternal rules
The author mentions two examples: the rule of preventing harm and imposing compensation, and the rule that a contract does not affect non-parties. Recognize these as universal principles.
These are recognized in Hanafi fiqh as qawa'id kulliyyah (e.g., لا ضرر ولا ضرار).
3
Contrast with the Roman law example
The author explains that Roman law evolved from local customs to a universal code under Justinian, showing how law develops with a nation's intellectual growth. This illustrates the temporary nature of localized laws.
Notice the three stages: isolated customs, dual legal system, universal code.
4
Apply the distinction to Islamic Sharia
Reflect on how Islamic Sharia, being divine, is based on eternal principles (e.g., prohibitions of riba, gharar) that are universally applicable.
Hanafi scholars consider these rules as part of the thubut (firmly established) and qat'i (definitive).

الفصل (۱) نظرة عامة إلى الشريعة الإسلامية
٣/۱ - فالشرع بوجه عام في أمة من الأمم ليس إلا صورة صحيحة لحياة اجتماعية واقعية وهدفه العام فيها إقامة العدل وحفظ التوازن في الحقوق والالتزامات وصيانة حقوق الناس الفردية، ومصالح المجتمع بقواعد قانونية وهذه القواعد تكون وقتية غير صالحة للخلود إذا كانت تعبر عن أوضاع خاصة عرفية إقليمية، وتكون صالحة للخلود إذا كانت تعبر عن مفاهيم وحقائق مُسَلَّمة ثابتة عالمية كقاعدة منع الضرر وإيجاب التعويض عنه، وقاعدة عدم سريان حكم العقد على غير عاقديه، وعدم تأثيره في حقوق الغير .
·
الأمة
فالتشريع واقع اجتماعي واقتصادي في الأمة، ويعبر عما وصلت إليه الحياة فيها من التطور والإدراك الاجتماعي. وبقدر ما فيه من قواعد ذات مفاهيم تشريعية عامة عالمية الاعتبار، وبقدر ما فيه من توجيه للأمة نحو الإصلاح المستمر، تكون درجة رقي ذلك الشرع وصلاحية مبادئه للخلود.
دائماً في هو كالأدب فيها كلاهما يعطي صورة عن
٤/١ - وليس اختلاف الشرائع بين الأمم إلا تعبيراً عن الاختلاف في الحياة الاجتماعية والاقتصادية فيما بينها ؛ وفي الأهداف التي تتجه نحوها هذه الحياة، وفي المثل العليا Ideals التي تستلهمها الأمة وتستوحيها من عقيدتها. فعندما تبلغ الأمة ذروة الرقي الفكري تبلغ تلك القواعد التشريعية لديها منتهاها من الرقي والقوة وتتمتع بالخلود. فالأمة الرومانية مثلاً لما كانت تعيش حياة منعزلة عن العالم منكمشة على عادات خاصة بها كانت قوانينها مجرد ،عادات ثم شرعاً موقتاً لحياة محدودة ولما خرجت من موطنها الأصلي واندفعت نحو الشرق، وحكمت عناصر أخرى من الناس، اضطرت للخروج من نطاق شرعها الضيق، فتطوّر وتوسع ولكنه بقي موقوتاً غير صالح للخلود إذ كان لهم شرعان أحدهما يتمتع به الرومان الأصليون، والآخر الأجانب. حتى إذا بلغوا مستوى عالياً في الرقي المدني والفكري بعد القرن الثالث للميلاد، وثبتوا قدمهم الثانية في الشرق الأوسط مهد الحضارات القديمة والشرائع والأديان، بعد أن هذبتهم المبادىء الإنسانية العامة التي خلفتها تعاليم السيد المسيح عليه السلام بلغ عندئذ تفكيرهم التشريعي منتهى رقيه، فقام الامبراطور جوستنيان في القرن الخامس الميلادي بتقنين الشرع الروماني وتحريره من جديد، فأصبح أساساً لمعظم الشرائع الأوربية إلى هذا اليوم. ٥/١ - ننتهي من هذه النظرة الخاطفة في القانون الوضعي بوجه عام إلى الحقيقة التالية : إن التشريع بوجه عام له ثلاث وظائف كبرى في الأمة: العلاج والوقاية، والتوجيه :
- فهو علاج للعلل الاجتماعية والمشكلات الاقتصادية الواقعة.
- وهو وقاية من العلل والمشكلات المتوقعة.
- وهو توجيه وتمهيد لاستمرار التكامل حتى يبلغ تنظيم الحقوق والالتزامات والمصالح مستواه الأكمل، كما يبلغ صاعد الجبل ذروته تدريجياً إذا استمر في اتجاهه إليها.
أن يكون
ولكي تتحقق هذه الوظائف الثلاث في التشريع يجب إلزامياً Obligatoire وإلا كان من قبيل المواعظ والإرشادات الأخلاقية. ثم لكي تضمن له الطاعة في العمل والتطبيق يحتاج الشرع الأصلي إلى نوع إضافي من الأحكام التشريعية يسمى : المُؤيدات Sanctions وهي : إما زواجر مدنية كبطلان العقود المخالفة للنظام، وإما عقوبات تأديبية كالسجن والغرامات على الجرائم العدوانية لكي يضطر المكلف إلى التزام حدود الطاعة.
على أنه إذا كان الشرع يفترق عن المبادىء الأخلاقية من ناحية القوة الإلزامية، فإنه دائماً يفتقر في التطبيق إلى الأخلاق التي يفترق عنها في المفهوم. ذلك لأن باب الاحتيال على القانون لا يمكن سده في وجه الأذكياء من الناس الذين يقف القانون حائلاً دون منافعهم ومطامعهم غير المشروعة إلا إذا توافرت الأخلاق الفاضلة لديهم، حتى تصبح أحكام القانون محترمة مقدسة في نفوسهم، فلا يستبيحوا هضم حقوق الغير أو
Chapter (1) General Overview of Islamic Sharia
3/1 - Law in general in any nation is nothing but a correct picture of a real social life, and its general objective is to establish justice, maintain balance in rights and obligations, and protect individual rights and community interests through legal rules. These rules are temporary and unfit for eternity if they express particular customary regional conditions, and they are fit for eternity if they express universal, constant, established truths and concepts, such as the rule of preventing harm and imposing compensation for it, and the rule that a contract's ruling does not extend to non-contracting parties and does not affect the rights of others.
·
The Nation
Thus legislation is a social and economic reality in a nation, and it expresses the level of development and social awareness that life has reached. The degree of advancement of that law and the suitability of its principles for eternity is proportional to the extent of its rules with universal legislative concepts and its guidance of the nation towards continuous reform.
It is always like literature; both give a picture of...
4/1 - Differences among the laws of nations are only an expression of differences in social and economic life among them, and in the goals that this life pursues, and in the ideals that the nation draws from its creed. When a nation reaches the peak of intellectual advancement, its legislative rules reach their peak of advancement and strength and enjoy eternity. For example, the Roman nation, when it lived an isolated life withdrawn into its own customs, its laws were mere customs, then a temporary law for a limited life. When it left its original homeland and pushed towards the East, and ruled over other elements of people, it was forced to exit the scope of its narrow law, so it developed and expanded, but it remained temporary and unfit for eternity, as they had two laws: one enjoyed by the original Romans, and the other for foreigners. Until they reached a high level of civil and intellectual advancement after the third century AD, and established their second foothold in the Middle East, the cradle of ancient civilizations, laws, and religions, after having been refined by the universal human principles left by the teachings of the Lord Christ (peace be upon him). At that time, their legislative thinking reached its peak, so Emperor Justinian in the fifth century AD codified the Roman law and reformulated it anew, making it the basis for most European laws to this day. 
5/1 - We conclude from this cursory look at positive law in general to the following truth: Legislation in general has three major functions in the nation: treatment, prevention, and guidance:
- It treats social ailments and economic problems that occur.
- It prevents expected ailments and problems.
- It guides and paves the way for continuous perfection until the organization of rights, obligations, and interests reaches its complete level, just as the mountain climber gradually reaches the summit if he continues in its direction.
For these three functions to be realized in legislation, it must be obligatory; otherwise, it would be of the type of moral sermons and advice. Then, to ensure obedience in practice and application, the original law needs an additional type of legislative provisions called: sanctions. They are either civil deterrents, such as nullity of contracts that violate the system, or disciplinary punishments, such as imprisonment and fines for aggressive crimes, to compel the accountable person to adhere to the limits of obedience.
However, if law differs from ethical principles in terms of obligatory force, it always lacks in application the ethics from which it differs in concept. This is because the door of legal loopholes cannot be closed against intelligent people for whom the law stands as an obstacle to their illegitimate benefits and ambitions unless virtuous ethics are present in them, so that the provisions of the law become respected and sacred in their hearts, and they do not allow themselves to usurp the rights of others or...
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical introduction to the nature of Islamic Sharia as a legislative system, compared with positive law, and an outline of the functions of legislation. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, a prominent Hanafi jurist, begins by stating that law (shar) in any nation reflects the reality of its social life, aiming at justice and balance. Rules based on universal concepts like preventing harm (a key Hanafi qawida: لا ضرر ولا ضرار) and contractual privity are eternal, while those based on particular customs are temporary. He then discusses how legislation evolves with a nation's intellectual development, citing Roman law as an example: it progressed from local customs to a universal code under Justinian, influenced by Christian ethics. This mirrors the Hanafi understanding that Sharia, being divine, embodies universal principles that are eternally valid. The author then identifies three essential functions of legislation: treatment (of existing problems), prevention (of future problems), and guidance (towards continuous improvement). For these functions to be effective, law must be obligatory (not merely advisory) and backed by sanctions—civil (e.g., nullity of contracts) or penal (e.g., imprisonment). Finally, he emphasizes that despite its coercive force, law relies on ethics to be effective, as ethical individuals are less likely to exploit legal loopholes. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on the integration of law and morality, recognizing that legal rules alone cannot ensure justice without a virtuous society.

Key Terms
الشريعة الإسلامية (al-shari'ah al-islamiyyah): The divine law revealed to the Prophet Muhammad, encompassing all aspects of life, including worship, transactions, and ethics.
التشريع (al-tashri'): Legislation; the body of legal rules governing a society, whether divine (shari'ah) or man-made (qanun).
المؤيدات (al-mu'ayyidat): Sanctions; enforcement mechanisms that ensure compliance with legal rules, including civil nullity and penal punishments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of an 'eternal' legal rule?
A) A local custom regulating trade in a particular region.
B) The Roman law before Justinian's codification.
C) The rule that harm must be compensated.
D) The tax laws of a modern state.

Key Takeaway
Legal rules based on universal truths are eternal; those based on local custom are temporary – a key distinction for understanding Islamic Sharia's immutable principles.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
beginner
8 min
The Three Functions of Legislation and the Role of Sanctions
Pragmatic analysis of legislation's functions and enforcement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three functions of legislation (treatment, prevention, guidance) and justify the necessity of sanctions and ethics for effective law.

Core Idea
Law must be obligatory, backed by sanctions, and supplemented by ethics to achieve its functions.

The second half of the passage shifts to a pragmatic discussion of what legislation must do. The author identifies three essential functions: treating existing problems, preventing future ones, and guiding society toward continuous improvement. He then argues that law must be obligatory and enforced by sanctions, but ultimately relies on ethics for voluntary compliance. This lesson breaks down these ideas and links them to Hanafi legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three functions of legislation
From the passage: treatment (علاج), prevention (وقاية), guidance (توجيه). Underline each and note what they target.
These are three distinct but interconnected roles.
2
Explain why law must be obligatory
The author states that without obligatory force, law becomes mere moral advice. Find the phrase 'يجب أن يكون إلزامياً' and note its significance.
This contrasts with purely ethical systems.
3
Categorize the types of sanctions
The passage mentions two types: civil sanctions (bطلان العقود) and penal sanctions (سجن وغرامات). List them and their purpose.
Hanafi fiqh similarly divides sanctions into financial and corporal.
4
Analyze the relationship between law and ethics
The author concludes that even with sanctions, law needs ethics to prevent loopholes. Identify the sentence about 'باب الاحتيال على القانون'.
This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on taqwa and inner morality.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
10 min
Functions of Legislation and the Role of Sanctions
Explains the three functions of positive law and the necessity of sanctions and ethics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three functions of legislation and explain why sanctions and ethical virtue are necessary for legal enforcement according to Mustafa al-Zarqa's analysis.

Core Idea
Legislation requires binding force and ethical backing to fulfill its functions of treatment, prevention, and guidance.

Al-Zarqa presents a foundational discussion on the nature of positive law. He identifies three key functions that any legislation must serve to be effective: curing existing problems, preventing future ones, and steering society toward continuous improvement. But he also warns that law without ethical commitment is vulnerable to clever circumvention. This lesson unpacks these ideas, linking them to the mufti's need to consider both legal obligation and moral context when issuing fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three functions of law
Read the passage and list the three functions al-Zarqa attributes to legislation: treatment (علاج), prevention (وقاية), and guidance (توجيه). Note how each function addresses a different temporal dimension: past, present, and future.
These functions are not mutually exclusive; they work together.
2
Understand the need for binding force (ilzam)
Al-Zarqa states that legislation must be obligatory (إلزامياً) to be distinguished from mere moral advice. Reflect on why a non-binding rule cannot achieve the three functions effectively.
This relates to the Hanafi principle that legal rulings (ahkam) are inherently binding when derived from authoritative sources.
3
Distinguish types of sanctions (mu'ayyidat)
Find the two categories of sanctions mentioned: civil (e.g., nullification of contracts) and penal (e.g., imprisonment, fines). How do these enforce compliance?
Sanctions are an additional layer beyond the original legal command.
4
Analyze the relationship between law and ethics
Al-Zarqa argues that law, despite its binding force, requires ethical virtue to prevent 'legal circumvention' (الاحتيال على القانون). Identify the reason: intelligent people may find loopholes if they lack moral scruples.
This echoes the Hanafi legal maxim that 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are judged by intentions) – intention and ethics are central to legal compliance.
5
Apply the lesson to fatwa practice
Consider a mufti issuing a fatwa on a financial transaction. The legal ruling might be valid, but if the parties are ethically compromised, the fatwa should address the ethical dimension or advise caution. Discuss how al-Zarqa's framework informs such a situation.
The mufti must weigh both the letter of the law (ilzam) and the spirit of ethical integrity.

Chapter One: Introduction, Definition, and Classification. And the laws and religions, after the general humane principles left by the teachings of the Messiah (peace be upon him) had refined them, their legislative thinking reached its peak of development. Emperor Justinian in the fifth century AD codified and revised Roman law, so it became the basis for most European laws to this day. 5/1 - We conclude from this brief overview of positive law in general to the following truth: Legislation in general has three major functions in the nation: treatment, prevention, and guidance. - It is a treatment for social ills and economic problems that occur. - It is prevention from expected ills and problems. - It is guidance and paving the way for continued integration until the organization of rights, obligations, and interests reaches its complete level, just as the mountain climber gradually reaches its peak if he continues in his direction towards it. For these three functions to be realized in legislation, it must be obligatory; otherwise it would be of the type of moral exhortations and guidance. Then, to ensure obedience in practice and application, the original law needs an additional type of legislative provisions called: sanctions. These are either civil penalties such as nullification of contracts contrary to the system, or disciplinary punishments such as imprisonment and fines for aggressive crimes, in order to compel the obligated person to adhere to the limits of obedience. However, if law differs from moral principles in terms of binding force, it is always in need in application of ethics from which it differs in concept. This is because the door of circumventing the law cannot be closed in the faces of intelligent people for whom the law stands as a barrier to their illicit interests and greed, unless they possess virtuous ethics, so that the provisions of law become respected and sacred in their souls, and they do not permit themselves to encroach on the rights of others or the rights of the state even if they can reach their greed through avenues far from judicial oversight, safe from its power and authority. From here the extent of the close connection and mutual support between legislation on one hand and intellectual, social, and ethical education on the other hand becomes clear.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is an introductory discussion of the nature and functions of positive law. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by briefly noting the development of Roman law and its influence. He then identifies three core functions of legislation: treatment (curing existing social and economic ills), prevention (avoiding anticipated problems), and guidance (steering continuous improvement). For legislation to fulfill these functions, it must be obligatory and backed by sanctions—either civil (like nullification of contracts) or penal (imprisonment, fines). However, the author emphasizes that law alone is insufficient; ethical virtue is essential to prevent circumvention by clever individuals. The passage underscores the interdependence of law and ethics, a principle deeply relevant in Hanafi usul al-fiqh where the moral and legal dimensions are intertwined.

Key Terms
التشريع (al-tashri'): Legislation, the process of making laws; here used for positive law (al-qanun al-wad'i).
الزامياً (ilzamiyan): Obligatory, binding; a quality of law that distinguishes it from moral advice.
المُؤيدات (al-mu'ayyidat): Sanctions; mechanisms to enforce law, either civil or penal.

Key Takeaway
For a law to be effective, it must be obligatory, backed by sanctions, and supported by ethical virtue to prevent circumvention.

Classification of Divine Laws and the Comprehensiveness of Islamic Sharia
Classifies divine laws and highlights Islamic Sharia's comprehensive nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify divine laws into three types and identify the features that make Islamic Sharia comprehensive and adaptable.

Core Idea
Islamic Sharia uniquely combines fixed principles with dynamic rulings tied to custom and necessity.

In this passage, Mustafa al-Zarqa transitions from discussing positive law to divine law, categorizing divine laws into three types: purely moral, particular legal, and universal comprehensive. He then elaborates on the unique structure of Islamic Sharia, which includes fixed principles, temporary measures, exceptional rulings, and custom-based rulings that allow it to remain relevant across all times and places.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish positive law from divine law
Understand the shift in topic from general legal philosophy to the specific category of divinely revealed laws.
2
Identify the three types of divine laws
Read the classification: (1) moral guidance without legal system (Christianity), (2) particular legal system for a specific people (Jewish law), (3) universal comprehensive legal system (Islamic Sharia).
Focus on the basis of each type.
3
Characterize the third type
Note the four features of Islamic Sharia: fixed principles, temporary measures, exceptional rulings for necessities, and rulings connected to customs.
These features ensure adaptability.
4
Relate to Hanafi legal theory
The concept of rulings tied to customs (ahkam marbuta bi'l-a'raf) is particularly important in the Hanafi school, where custom (urf) is a recognized source of law.
Consider how this impacts fatwa.
5
Summarize the unique position of Islamic Sharia
Conclude that Islamic law's comprehensiveness and flexibility stem from its balanced combination of immutable principles and changeable provisions.
This distinguishes it from other divine laws.

After this quick overview of law in general, its elements and perfective qualities, we move to talk about divine laws, then the Islamic Sharia to see its position regarding the elements of legislative perfection and its share thereof. - 2 - Light on Divine Laws. 6/1 - Alongside positive laws there exist divine laws through messengers who carry the revelation of Allah Almighty to humanity, guiding, reforming, and correcting the deviant path of human conduct. These divine laws are of three types: - A type that comes for moral reform, purification of souls, and support of virtue, and does not include a legal system akin to positive laws, such as Christianity. And a type that includes a legal system specific to an environment or people, like the Jewish law brought by our master Moses (peace be upon him) to the Children of Israel, which included a legal system and practical judicial rulings suitable for the temporal and spatial circumstances they were in. And a type that includes a legal system founded on the basis of comprehensiveness and suitability for all environments and circumstances, because it contains fixed principles, temporary measures, exceptional rulings for situations of necessity, and rulings connected to customs and specific circumstances that change and develop according to the change of those customs and the development of circumstances, while preserving the fundamental idea in it, which is justice and equity.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage transitions from positive law to divine law. Al-Zarqa classifies divine laws into three categories: (1) purely moral guidance without a legal system (e.g., Christianity), (2) a legal system tailored to a specific people and time (e.g., Mosaic law for the Israelites), and (3) a comprehensive, adaptable legal system suitable for all times and places—this is Islamic Sharia. He highlights that Islamic Sharia incorporates fixed principles (mabadi' thabita), temporary measures (tadabir mawquta), exceptional rulings for necessities (ahkam istithna'iyya li-ahwal al-darurat), and rulings tied to customs (ahkam marbuta bi'l-a'raf) that evolve while maintaining the core values of justice and equity. This aligns with the Hanafi view of Sharia as dynamic and pragmatic, capable of accommodating changing circumstances without losing its foundational ethos.

Key Terms
الشرائع الإلهية (al-shara'i' al-ilahiyya): Divine laws, revealed through prophets to guide humanity.
المبادىء الثابتة (al-mabadi' al-thabita): Fixed principles, unchanging fundamentals of Islamic law.
الأحكام المربوطة بالأعراف (al-ahkam al-marbuta bi'l-a'raf): Rulings connected to customs, which can change as customs change.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Types of Divine Laws and the Unique Framework of Islamic Shari'ah
Describing types of divine laws and the framework of Islamic Shari'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three types of divine laws and identify the unique characteristics of Islamic Shari'ah that enable its comprehensiveness and flexibility.

Core Idea
Islamic Shari'ah is the final divine law: fixed in principles, flexible in application, and open to ijtihad.

This lesson introduces the classification of divine laws and highlights what makes Islamic Shari'ah distinct. Mustafa al-Zarqa explains that before Islamic law, there were divine laws focusing on moral reform or limited to a specific people. Islamic Shari'ah combines fixed principles with adaptability to all times and places, setting the stage for its role as the ultimate legal framework.

Guided Path
1

2

For each type, note its scope: type 1 is moral only, type 2 is particular, type 3 is universal. Also note the presence or absence of a legal system.
3

This definition appears in section 7/1.

The passage outlines three objectives: (a) freeing the mind from imitation and superstition through tawhid and rational proof, (b) reforming the individual through worship and moral cultivation, and (c) (implied) societal reform.

Chapter (1) General Overview of Islamic Shari'ah
The rights of the state... even if they could reach their desires through avenues far from judicial oversight, safe from its penalty and authority. From here, the extent of the close connection and mutual support between legislation on one hand and intellectual, social, and moral education on the other becomes clear. After this brief general look at law in general, its elements and perfective qualities, we move to speak about divine laws, then Islamic Shari'ah to see its position regarding the elements of legislative perfection and its share thereof.
- 2 -
Light on Divine Laws
6/1 - Alongside positive laws, there are divine laws through the messengers who carry the revelation of Allah Almighty to humanity, guiding, reforming, and correcting the deviant human path. These divine laws are of three types:
- A type that comes for moral rectification, purification of souls, and support of virtue, and does not include a legal system on the model of positive laws, such as Christianity.
- A type that includes a legal system specific to an environment or people, such as the Jewish law brought by our master Moses (peace be upon him) to the Children of Israel, containing a legal system and practical judicial rulings commensurate with the temporal and spatial circumstances they were in.
- A type that includes a legal system founded on the basis of comprehensiveness and suitability for all environments and circumstances, because it contains fixed principles, temporary measures, exceptional rulings for situations of necessity, and rulings linked to customs and specific circumstances that change and evolve according to changes in those customs and circumstances, while preserving the underlying idea of justice and fairness. This third type is the Islamic Shari'ah, which came after its predecessors as a call to all humanity when the intellectual development and readiness of humans reached a sufficient degree to receive such final rulings in the form of fixed principles and universal rules. Alongside those principles and universals, it left a wide field for ijtihad in detail and a great share for reason in application, and it linked rulings to their causes and reasons so that the obligated individuals might understand them and be able to analogize from them with their minds, according to the objectives of Shari'ah in justice, benevolence, bringing benefits, and averting harms.
- 3 -
The General Framework of Islamic Shari'ah or the System of Islam
7/1 - Islamic Shari'ah is the set of commands and rulings, both creedal and practical, that Islam requires to be implemented to achieve its reformist objectives in society. Islam has three basic reformist objectives, each arranged as the result of the previous and the basis for the next:
a) - Freeing the human mind from the bondage of imitation and superstitions, through creed and faith in Allah alone, directing the mind towards evidence, proof, and free scientific thinking. Hence Islam combated polytheism in all its forms because it is intellectual degradation and blindness of insight, and it established creed in Allah Almighty, His necessary attributes, and the Last Day on the basis of simple rational evidence from the signs and proofs of the universe. Thus the Islamic creed came as logical, clear, without complexity or arbitrariness, containing nothing that contradicts reason, although it contains matters of the unseen and the will of Allah whose wisdom may be hidden, which no divine law can be without.
b) - Reforming the individual psychologically and morally, directing him towards goodness, benevolence, and duty, so that his desires and ambitions do not overwhelm his reason and responsibilities. This is achieved through the individual's practice of prescribed worship which reminds him of his Creator, and through the belief in reward.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is an introductory exposition on divine laws and the unique characteristics of Islamic Shari'ah. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by establishing the close relationship between legislation and intellectual, social, and moral education. He then transitions to discussing divine laws, distinguishing three types: (1) those focused solely on moral reform without a legal system (like Christianity), (2) those with a legal system limited to a specific people and time (like Judaism), and (3) the comprehensive and perpetually suitable divine law—Islamic Shari'ah. The key features of Islamic Shari'ah highlighted are: fixed principles (qawa'id kulliyya), temporary measures, exceptional rules for necessities, flexibility with customs, and a wide scope for ijtihad and rational reasoning ('aql). The passage then outlines the general framework of Islam, defining Shari'ah as the total set of creedal and practical rulings. It identifies three foundational reformist objectives: first, freeing the mind from blind imitation and superstition through rational belief in tawhid; second, reforming the individual through worship and moral cultivation; and third (implied, though the OCR cuts off) likely societal reform. The author emphasizes that Islamic creed is rational and does not contradict reason, while acknowledging that some unseen matters may be beyond human wisdom. This passage sets the stage for understanding the Hanafi usul al-Iftaa framework, which relies on a balance of textual sources, qawa'id, 'aql, and ijtihad.

Key Terms
الشريعة الإسلامية (al-shari'a al-islamiyya): The set of commands and rulings, both creedal and practical, required by Islam to achieve its reformist objectives.
الشرائع الإلهية (al-shara'i' al-ilahiyya): Divine laws revealed through prophets, comprising three types: purely moral, temporally/culturally specific legal, and comprehensive/eternal legal.
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Independent legal reasoning to derive rulings from the sources, particularly in areas not explicitly detailed in the Quran and Sunnah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a characteristic of Islamic Shari'ah?
A) Fixity in principles
B) Flexibility in application
C) Limited to a specific people
D) Reliance on ijtihad

Key Takeaway
Islamic Shari'ah's unique blend of fixed universal principles and adaptable mechanisms ensures its relevance for all times and places.

The Three Reform Goals of Islamic Sharia
Defining Sharia's framework and three reform goals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three reform goals of Islamic Sharia and explain how they form the pillars of the legal system according to the Hanafi juristic framework.

Core Idea
Islamic Sharia rests on three pillars: rational creed, spiritual worship, and a comprehensive legal system.

Islamic Sharia is more than a set of legal rules—it is a holistic system designed to reform humanity. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, presents a clear framework: freeing the intellect through creed, reforming the individual through worship, and reforming society through law. Understanding this structure helps a mufti see how every ruling serves a higher purpose within the Sharia's objectives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three reform goals
Read the passage and list the three goals: freeing the intellect, reforming the individual, and reforming society. Note their sequential relationship.
The goals are ordered: each builds on the previous.
2
Distinguish the means for each goal
For each goal, identify the specific means mentioned: rational creed (aqidah), prescribed worship (ibadah), and a judicial legal system (nizam qanuni).
The text explicitly links each goal to a corresponding pillar.
3
Explain the role of the legal system
Focus on the third pillar: the legal system covers private and public rights, including constitutional, administrative, financial, and international law. This is the focus of the book series.
The author distinguishes between the ideal system and its historical application.
4
Analyze the distinction between system and application
The passage warns that historical misapplications do not invalidate the Sharia system itself. As a mufti, you must separate the ideal from the flawed human implementation.
This is crucial for responding to criticisms of Islamic law.
5
Apply the framework to a modern case
Consider a modern societal issue (e.g., cybercrime). Identify which goal it relates to (social reform) and how the legal system pillar provides principles for legislation.
This shows the relevance of the framework for fatwa derivation.

الباب الأول : تمهيد وتعريف وتقسيم
وهذا النوع الثالث هو الشريعة الإسلامية التي جاءت بعد سوابقها دعوة للبشر كافة حينما وصل التطور والاستعداد الفكري عند البشر إلى درجة كافية لتلقي مثل هذه الأحكام النهائية في صورة مبادىء وقواعد كلية ثابتة، وتركت إلى جانب تلك المبادىء والكليات مجالاً واسعاً للاجتهاد في التفصيل ونصيباً كبيراً للعقل في التطبيق؛ وَقَرنت الأحكام بعللها وأسبابها كي يفهمها المكلفون ويتمكنوا من القياس عليها بعقولهم، وفقاً لمقاصد الشريعة في العدل والإحسان وجلب المصالح ودرء المفاسد.
- ٣ -
الإطار العام للشريعة الإسلامية، أو نظام الإسلام ۷/۱ - الشريعة الإسلامية هي مجموعة الأوامر والأحكام الاعتقادية والعملية التي يوجب الإسلام تطبيقها لتحقيق أهدافه الإصلاحية في المجتمع .
فللإسلام ثلاثة أهداف إصلاحية أساسية مرتبة كل منها نتيجة لما قبله وأساس لما بعده:
أ) - تحرير العقل البشري من رقّ التقليد والخرافات، وذلك عن طريق العقيدة والإيمان بالله وحده، وتوجيه العقل نحو الدليل والبرهان والتفكير العلمي الحرة ولذا كافح الإسلام الوثنية في شتى صورها الأنها انحطاط في العقل وعماوة في البصيرة، وأسس العقيدة في الله تعالى وصفاته الواجبة وفي اليوم الآخر على أساس الدليل العقلي البسيط من: شواهد الكون ودلائله حتى جاءت عقيدة الإسلام منطقية واضحة لا تعقيد فيها ولا تحكم، ليس فيها ما يناقض العقل، وإن كان فيها ما تخفى حكمته من أمور الغيب وإرادة الله تعالى مما لا يمكن أن تخلو منه شريعة إلهية ..
ب - إصلاح الفرد نفسياً وخلقياً، وتوجيهه نحو الخير والإحسان والواجب كي لا تطغى شهواته ومطامعه على عقله وواجباته. وذلك بممارسة الفرد للعبادة المشروعة التي تذكره بخالقه، وبعقيدة الثواب والعقاب في الآخرة لكي يكون المؤمن في مراقبة دائمة لأعماله، حريصاً على عدم التقصير في واجباته.
ج) - إصلاح المجتمع أي الحياة الاجتماعية بصورة يسود فيها النظام والأمن العام والعدل بين الناس وصيانة الحريات المعقولة والكرامة الإنسانية .
ولتحقيق هذا الهدف الاجتماعي الأخير جاء الإسلام بنظام مدني يتضمن تشريعاً شاملاً الأسس القانونية اللازمة لإقامة حياة اجتماعية جميع في دولة وتنظيم علائق الناس بعضهم مع بعض، وعلائقهم بالسلطة الحاكمة، وصيانة الحقوق الخاصة للأفراد والحقوق العامة للجماعة .
من هذه الأهداف الثلاثة في الإسلام يتحدّد معنى الشريعة الإسلامية ويتضح أنها تقوم على ثلاث دعائم : عقيدة عقلية وعبادة روحية، ونظام قانوني قضائي. وهذا هو المعنى المراد عندما يقال: إن الإسلام دين ودولة .
هذا ويجب التفريق بين النظام والتطبيق؛ إذ لا ينكر أنه في الواقع العملي والتاريخي كثيراً مايساء فهم الحقيقة الإسلامية في بعض هذه النواحي الثلاثة، أو يساء تطبيقها، فتظهر صورة مشوهة. إنَّ هذه الناحية الثالثة وهي النظام القانوني من الشريعة الإسلامية، هي وحدها المقصودة هنا في هذه السلسلة الفقهية سلسلة (الفقه الإسلامي في ثوبه الجديد).
النظام القانوني في الشريعة الإسلامية
٨/١ - إن النظام القانوني القضائي في الشريعة تضمن قواعد وأحكاماً أساسية في كلا الميدانين ميدان الحقوق الخاصة بفرعيها المدني والجنائي، وميدان الحقوق العامة بفرعيها الداخلي والخارجي، أي الدستوري، ،والإداري ،والمالي والدولي
Chapter One: Introduction, Definition, and Division
And this third type is the Islamic Sharia which came after its predecessors as a call to all of humanity when human intellectual development and readiness reached a sufficient degree to receive such final rulings in the form of fixed universal principles and rules, and alongside those principles and universals, it left a wide field for ijtihad in detail and a large share for the intellect in application; and it linked rulings to their causes and reasons so that the obligated may understand them and be able to analogize upon them with their intellects, in accordance with the objectives of the Sharia in justice, benevolence, bringing benefits, and repelling harms.
- 3 -
The General Framework of Islamic Sharia, or the System of Islam 7/1 - The Islamic Sharia is the set of doctrinal and practical commands and rulings that Islam requires to be applied to achieve its reformist objectives in society.
For Islam has three basic reformist objectives, each ordered as a result of what comes before and as a foundation for what comes after:
a) Freeing the human intellect from the bondage of blind imitation and superstitions, through creed and faith in God alone, and directing the intellect towards evidence, proof, and free scientific thought. Hence Islam combated polytheism in all its forms because it is a degradation of the intellect and blindness of insight, and it established creed in God Almighty, His necessary attributes, and the Hereafter on the basis of simple rational evidence from the signs and proofs of the universe, until the Islamic creed came as logical, clear, with no complexity or arbitrariness, containing nothing that contradicts the intellect, although it contains matters of the unseen and the will of God whose wisdom may be hidden, which any divine law cannot be devoid of.
b) Reforming the individual psychologically and morally, and directing him towards goodness, benevolence, and duty so that his desires and ambitions do not overwhelm his intellect and duties. This is through the individual's practice of prescribed worship that reminds him of his Creator, and through the belief in reward and punishment in the Hereafter, so that the believer is in constant watch over his deeds, keen not to fall short in his duties.
c) Reforming society i.e., social life so that order, public security, justice among people, and the safeguarding of reasonable freedoms and human dignity prevail.
To achieve this final social goal, Islam brought a civil system that includes comprehensive legislation encompassing the legal foundations necessary to establish a social life for all in a state, and to regulate relations among people with each other, their relations with the ruling authority, and the protection of private rights of individuals and public rights of the community.
From these three objectives in Islam, the meaning of Islamic Sharia is determined and it becomes clear that it stands on three pillars: a rational creed, a spiritual worship, and a judicial legal system. This is the intended meaning when it is said: Islam is religion and state.
And one must distinguish between the system and its application; for it cannot be denied that in practical and historical reality, the Islamic truth is often misunderstood in some of these three aspects, or misapplied, thus a distorted picture appears. Indeed, this third aspect, which is the legal system of Islamic Sharia, is alone intended here in this jurisprudential series, the series 'Islamic Jurisprudence in Its New Garb'.
The Legal System in Islamic Sharia
8/1 - Indeed, the judicial legal system in the Sharia included basic rules and rulings in both fields: the field of private rights with its civil and criminal branches, and the field of public rights with its internal and external branches, i.e., constitutional, administrative, financial, and international.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is an introductory chapter defining the general framework of Islamic Sharia and its reform goals. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by describing the third type of law (Islamic Sharia) as a final and universal law that came when humanity was ready, characterized by fixed principles and room for ijtihad. He then outlines three fundamental reform objectives: first, freeing the intellect through rational creed; second, reforming the individual through worship and accountability; third, reforming society through a comprehensive legal system. These three objectives correspond to the three pillars of Sharia: creed, worship, and legal system. The author emphasizes that the legal system is the focus of this book series, and he distinguishes between the system itself and its historical application, which may be distorted. Finally, he notes that this legal system covers both private and public rights, including constitutional, administrative, financial, and international law.

Key Terms
الشريعة الإسلامية (al-Shari‘ah al-Islamiyyah): مجموعة الأوامر والأحكام الاعتقادية والعملية التي يوجب الإسلام تطبيقها لتحقيق أهدافه الإصلاحية في المجتمع
النظام القانوني (al-Nizam al-Qanuni): الجانب الثالث من الشريعة الإسلامية الذي يتضمن قواعد وأحكاماً في الحقوق الخاصة والعامة
العبادة الروحية (al-‘Ibadah al-Ruhiyyah): ممارسة الفرد للعبادة المشروعة التي تذكره بخالقه وتؤدي إلى مراقبة الأعمال

Key Takeaway
Islamic Sharia is a comprehensive system with three interdependent reform goals—freeing the intellect, reforming the individual, and reforming society—corresponding to its three pillars: creed, worship, and legal system.

The Foundational Character of Sharia: Principles and Ijtihad
Overview of Sharia principles and the need for ijtihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that Sharia provides fundamental principles and identify that detailed rulings, except for a few, are derived through ijtihad.

Core Idea
Sharia gives principles, not pre-packaged rulings — ijtihad fills the details.

This lesson explores the opening of al-Zarqa's chapter, where he establishes that the Quran and Sunnah came with basic principles, leaving the vast majority of legal details to the ijtihad of qualified scholars. This foundational understanding is crucial for any mufti: you are not simply applying fixed rules but interpreting principles in light of changing circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish principles from details
Identify that Sharia texts provide broad principles (mabadi' asasiyyah) and only occasionally detailed rulings (e.g., inheritance).
2
Recognize the role of ijtihad
Understand that the elaboration of details is left to ijtihad, which takes into account temporal interests and spatial possibilities.
Ijtihad is a duty for those qualified (mujtahidun).
3
Note the exceptions
The text mentions a few detailed rulings like inheritance and some punishments. These are exceptions to the general principle of leaving details to ijtihad.
4
Reflect on the mufti's task
A mufti must appreciate that many rulings in the Hanafi school are the product of ijtihad on general principles, not direct textual commands.
This helps in understanding the methodology of tarjih (preferring one opinion over another).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الأول: تمهيد وتعريف وتقسيم
هذه الزمر قد أتت نصوص الشريعة الإسلامية الأصلية في وفي جميع القرآن والسنة النبوية بمبادىء أساسية، وتركت التفصيلات للاجتهاد في التطبيق بحسب المصالح الزمنية والإمكانيات المكانية، إلا قليلاً من الأحكام تناولتها بالتفصيل كأحكام الميراث وبعض العقوبات.
وحول تلك المبادىء القانونية في ميادينها المختلفة، ونتيجة للتطبيق في البلاد الإسلامية التي واجه فيها المسلمون آثار مدنيات قديمة، وبنتيجة تطور الظروف الاقتصادية المختلفة، نشأ فقه تفسيري وتفصيلي عظيم حول النصوص الأصلية في الشريعة الكتاب والسنة النبوية باجتهاد الفقهاء الشراح، والقضاة الحاكمين. وكان هذا الفقه الإسلامي أعظم وأوسع فقه قانوني عرف إلى اليوم في تاريخ الشرائع. وقد نشأت فيه مذاهب فقهية قانونية كثيرة، أشهرها المذاهب الأربعة الحيَّة إلى اليوم وهي الحنفي والمالكي والشافعي والحنبلي. بین هذه المذاهب ليس اختلافاً دينياً، بل هو اختلاف قانوني قضائي فالاختلاف نشأ منه ثروة تشريعية عظمى في نظريات الفقه الإسلامي.
Chapter One: Introduction, Definition, and Division. This group of original texts of Islamic Sharia, in all of the Quran and Prophetic Sunnah, came with basic principles, and left details to ijtihad in application according to temporal interests and spatial possibilities, except for a few rulings that were detailed, such as inheritance rulings and some punishments. Around those legal principles in their various fields, and as a result of application in Islamic lands where Muslims encountered the effects of ancient civilizations, and as a result of the development of different economic circumstances, a great explanatory and detailed fiqh arose around the original texts of Sharia (the Book and Prophetic Sunnah) through the ijtihad of jurist commentators and ruling judges. This Islamic fiqh was the greatest and most comprehensive legal fiqh known to date in the history of laws. Many legal fiqh schools arose within it, the most famous of which are the four living schools today: Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, and Hanbali. Among these schools there is no religious difference, but rather a legal/judicial difference. From this difference arose a great legislative wealth in the theories of Islamic fiqh.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This is an introductory passage on the nature of Islamic Sharia and the development of fiqh. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that the primary sources (Quran and Sunnah) provide fundamental principles, leaving the detailed elaboration to qualified jurists through ijtihad, taking into account changing circumstances. This led to the emergence of a vast interpretive fiqh corpus. Over time, distinct legal schools (madhahib) formed, the four most prominent being Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, and Hanbali. Emphasizing that differences among them are not religious but legal and judicial, this diversity is seen as a legislative treasure, enriching the overall fiqh theory. This understanding is crucial for a mufti: the existence of multiple valid opinions within the Hanafi school itself is part of this broader legislative wealth.

Key Terms
مبادىء أساسية (mabādiʼ asāsiyyah): The fundamental principles provided by the Quran and Sunnah that are general and leave room for ijtihad.
اجتهاد (ijtihād): The process of applying fundamental principles to derive specific rulings according to temporal and spatial circumstances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the original texts of Islamic Sharia primarily provide?
A) Detailed rulings for every situation
B) Basic principles, leaving details to ijtihad
C) Only inheritance and punishment laws
D) A complete legal code without room for interpretation

Key Takeaway
Sharia came with fundamental principles; ijtihad is essential to derive detailed rulings.

Understanding Ikhtilaf: The Wealth of Legal Schools
Explanation of madhahib and the nature of ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain that differences among the four Sunni schools are legal and judicial, not religious, and appreciate this diversity as a legislative wealth.

Core Idea
Madhhab differences are legislative wealth, not religious division.

Al-Zarqa moves to the historical outcome of ijtihad: the formation of legal schools (madhahib). He stresses that the differences among them are not religious but legal and judicial. For a Hanafi mufti, this means your school's ruling is one valid interpretation among others, and this recognition enriches the legal system.

Guided Path
1
List the four famous schools
Recall the four surviving schools: Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali.
There were many others, but these are the most widespread.
2
Identify the nature of differences
The text states that differences are 'qanuni qadai' (legal/judicial), not 'dini' (religious). This means they arise from methodological variations in interpreting sources.
No school accuses another of heresy due to fiqh differences.
3
Recognize the benefit of ikhtilaf
Al-Zarqa calls this difference a 'great legislative wealth' because it provides flexibility and a range of valid opinions.
This is the basis for talfiq and tatabbu' al-rukhas in some contexts.
4
Apply as a mufti
When giving fatwa within the Hanafi school, a mufti acknowledges that other schools have valid positions, and in cases of hardship, may refer to them (under strict conditions).
This is part of the mufti's adab.

Source Text

Among these schools there is no religious difference, but rather a legal/judicial difference. From this difference arose a great legislative wealth in the theories of Islamic fiqh.

Commentary

The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that the primary sources (Quran and Sunnah) provide fundamental principles, leaving the detailed elaboration to qualified jurists through ijtihad, taking into account changing circumstances. Key Terms
مذاهب فقهية (madhāhib fiqhiyyah): Legal schools of thought within Islam, such as the four well-known ones, whose differences are of a legal nature, not religious.
اختلاف (ikhtilāf): Difference of opinion among scholars or schools, which is considered a source of legislative wealth in Islamic jurisprudence.
ثروة تشريعية (tharwah tashri'iyyah): Legislative wealth; the cumulative benefit derived from the multiplicity of valid opinions in fiqh.

Foundations of Family Law: Women's Rights in Sharia
Exposition of Sharia's foundational family law reforms and women's legal status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key reforms of Islamic family law regarding women’s status and explain how these maqasid inform contemporary fatwa in personal status matters.

Core Idea
Islamic Sharia revolutionized women's status by granting them legal autonomy and inheritance while preserving family leadership for men.

This lesson presents the foundational legal principles that underpin Islamic family law. Understanding these maqasid is essential for a mufti when issuing fatwas on contemporary personal status issues, as they reflect the spirit of the Sharia in promoting justice and women's dignity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pre-Islamic context
Recognize the degraded status of women before Islam: lack of right to life, inheritance, and autonomy. This sets the stage for the revolutionary reforms.
This historical context is crucial for appreciating the Sharia's intent.
2
Enumerate the specific reforms
List the rights granted to women: prohibition of infanticide, inheritance, freedom in marriage, guardianship, and full financial authority without male control.
These are specific legal capacities (ahliyyat huquqiyyah).
3
Analyze the balance of authority
Note that the Sharia retained for men the supreme authority in managing family affairs, but without infringing on the wife's private rights. This is a balanced approach.
The principle is not absolute patriarchy but functional leadership.
4
Outline the three stages of family law
Marriage and dissolution, guardianship of minors, and inheritance form the core of personal status law.
These three areas are the framework for all family-related rulings.
5
Apply the principle of religious pluralism
Non-Muslims are allowed to follow their own personal status laws, reflecting respect for religious freedom.
This is a key principle for fatwa in plural societies.
