أهم المبادىء الأساسية
في هذا النظام القانوني من الشريعة الإسلامية المطلب الأول
قسم الحقوق الخاصة
أ - في حقوق الأسرة (الأحوال الشخصية)
أتت الشريعة الإسلامية في القسم القانوني منها بنظام شامل لتنظيم جميع أوضاع الأسرة وعلائقها المسماة اليوم بالأحوال الشخصية فبدأت أولاً بإنقاذ المرأة من الوضع المهضوم الذي كانت عليه في أسرتها، إذ كانت المرأة لا تتمتع بحق الحياة فضلاً عن غيره من الحقوق. فحرَّم القرآن قتل البنات المعتاد في جاهلية العرب، ونَدَّد القرآن به أشد تنديد. وهذا، كما يقول الأستاذ مونته عميد جامعة جنيف في مقدمة ترجمته للقرآن كاف لأن يضمن لمحمد اسماً لا ينسى بين المصلحين بالنسبة إلى عصره.
ولم تكتف الشريعة بهذا المقدار، بل اعتبرت للمرأة الأهليات الحقوقية التي للرجل، فقررت لها حق الإرث الذي كانت محرومة منه، كما قررت لها الحرية في الزواج واختيار الزوج وأهلية الوصاية على أولادها أو غيرهم، وحق إدارة أموالها واستثمارها وسائر التصرفات دون أي سيطرة عليها في شيء من ذلك للرجل من قريب أو زوج.
وقد احتفظت الشريعة للرجل بالسلطة العليا في تدبير شؤون الأسرة لأنه أقدر على ذلك دون مساس بالحقوق الخاصة للزوجة.
ثم إنَّ الشريعة نظمت سائر حقوق الأسرة تنظيماً جديداً دون تقيد بشيء من الحقوق العرفية التي كانت عليها الأسرة قبل الإسلام. وشمل هذا التنظيم حقوق الأسرة بمراحلها الطبيعية الثلاث:
١ - الزواج وانحلاله ونتائجهما الواسعة النطاق.
٢ - الولاية والوصاية على القاصرين بسبب الصغر أو الخلل العقلي.
٣ - الميراث.
وسنشير بإيجاز إلى بعض الملاحظات والأسس الهامة في كل من الأحوال الثلاث.
ونشير الآن سلفاً إلى أن الشريعة الإسلامية تحتفظ لرعايا الدولة الإسلامية من غير المسلمين بأن يطبقوا فيما بينهم أحكام ديانتهم في أحوالهم الشخصية نظراً لاتصال هذه الأحوال بالديانة التي تصون الشريعة لكل طائفة حريتها فيها.
The Most Important Basic Principles in this Legal System of Islamic Sharia - First Requirement - Section of Private Rights - A. Family Rights (Personal Status). Islamic Sharia, in its legal section, came with a comprehensive system to regulate all family conditions and relationships, today called personal status. It began first by rescuing women from the degraded position they were in within their families, as women did not enjoy the right to life, let alone other rights. So the Quran prohibited the customary killing of girls in Arab Jahiliyyah, and the Quran condemned it most severely. This, as Professor Montet, Dean of the University of Geneva, says in the introduction to his translation of the Quran, is sufficient to guarantee Muhammad an unforgettable name among reformers relative to his era. And Sharia did not stop at this amount, but rather considered for women the legal capacities that men have, so it established for them the right of inheritance which they were deprived of, and also established for them freedom in marriage and choice of husband, eligibility for guardianship over their children or others, and the right to manage their wealth, invest it, and all other dispositions without any control over them in any of that by a relative or husband. And Sharia retained for men the supreme authority in managing family affairs, because he is more capable of that without infringing on the special rights of the wife. Then Sharia organized all other family rights in a new organization, without being bound by any of the customary rights that the family had before Islam. This organization included family rights in their three natural stages: 1. Marriage and its dissolution and their far-reaching consequences. 2. Guardianship and custody over minors due to minority or mental deficiency. 3. Inheritance. We will briefly indicate some important observations and foundations in each of the three conditions. And we now point out beforehand that Islamic Sharia preserves for the subjects of the Islamic state from non-Muslims that they apply among themselves the rulings of their own religion in their personal affairs, because these affairs are connected to religion, and Sharia safeguards for each sect its freedom in them.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage outlines the fundamental principles of Islamic family law, emphasizing the revolutionary changes introduced by Sharia for women. It begins by highlighting the prohibition of female infanticide, a practice in pre-Islamic Arabia, and notes the endorsement of a Western scholar. Then it enumerates the legal capacities granted to women: inheritance, freedom in marriage, guardianship rights, and full financial autonomy without male interference. It also clarifies that men retain ultimate authority in managing family affairs, but without violating the wife's rights. The passage then summarizes the three key areas of family law: marriage and its dissolution, guardianship/custody of minors, and inheritance. Finally, it notes the tolerance for non-Muslims to follow their own personal status laws. For a mufti, this establishes the overarching objectives of family law: justice for women, preservation of family structure, and respect for religious diversity.

Key Terms
أهليات حقوقية (ahliyyāt ḥuqūqiyyah): Legal capacities granted to women, equivalent to those of men, such as inheritance, freedom of marriage, and financial management.
الولاية والوصاية (al-wilāyah wa al-wiṣāyah): Guardianship and custody over minors due to age or mental incapacity, part of family law regulation.
أحوال شخصية (aḥwāl shakhṣiyyah): Personal status matters, including marriage, divorce, custody, and inheritance, regulated by Sharia.

A) The right to lead prayer
B) The right to choose their husband
C) The right to serve as judges
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The Sharia's family law reforms established women's legal autonomy and inheritance, setting a foundation for justice that muftis must uphold.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate

Marriage as a Civil Contract in Islamic Law
Explaining women's legal capacity and the civil nature of marriage in Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key elements that make marriage a civil contract in Hanafi law and apply this understanding to evaluate the validity of marriage ceremonies.

Core Idea
Marriage in Islam is a civil contract (عقد مدني), not a sacrament; it requires only mutual consent and two witnesses.

This lesson explores the foundational Islamic principle that marriage is a civil contract, not a religious ceremony requiring clergy. Mustafa al-Zarqa provides a clear exposition, linking this to women's rights and the absence of mediation in Islam. Understanding this distinction is crucial for muftis dealing with contemporary issues like interfaith marriage or the role of imams in weddings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Capacities of Women
List the rights granted by Islamic law: inheritance, freedom in marriage, guardianship, property management. Note that these set the context for the civil nature of marriage.
Women's rights are a precursor to the argument that marriage does not require religious authority.
2
Recognize the Absence of Clergy
Understand that Islam has no priestly class with exclusive religious authority. The Prophet's role is limited to conveying revelation (تبليغ) and temporal implementation.
The Quranic verse 'فإنما عليك البلاغ' supports this.
3
Analyze the Prophetic Proof
Examine the hadith where the Prophet tells Fatima he cannot intercede for her. This proves no one has authority over a person's relationship with God.
The hadith is cited as evidence that marriage does not require religious mediation.
4
Derive the Ruling on Marriage
Conclude that marriage is purely civil: it is concluded by agreement of wills in the presence of two witnesses, with no need for a religious official.
This is the Hanafi position and the basis for considering Islamic law as the first to establish civil marriage.
5
Apply to Modern Contexts
Consider how this principle resolves questions about the validity of marriages performed without an imam or in courts. The mufta bihi ruling is that such marriages are valid.
Common misconception: confusion between presence of imam as a custom versus a legal condition.

Part One: Introduction, Definition, and Classification
1 - On Marriage, Its Dissolution, and Their Effects
Conclusion of Marriage: 1/10 - The law made marriage a purely civil contract like other contracts, so it is concluded, completed, and produces all its results simply by the agreement of the two wills in the presence of two witnesses without depending on the intervention of a religious authority, because there is no clergy in Islam who have religious authority not possessed by others. In Islamic law, there is no mediation of anyone between God and people, even the Prophet himself, who brought the law, his religious mission is limited to conveying, and his temporal authority to implementation, as the Quran says to him: 'Indeed, upon you is only [the duty of] notification; indeed, you are a warner, and for every people there is a guide.'
So the Prophet himself does not have religious authority to judge the fate of a person with his Lord. Rather, every person enters the religion by his faith and leaves by his denial. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, says to his daughter: 'O Fatima, daughter of Muhammad, ask me for whatever you wish of my wealth, I cannot avail you against Allah at all.' (1) For in Islam, people are distinguished in religious consideration by the merit of knowledge and action. So the most knowledgeable of the rulings of the law (which are established, written, announced, not secret, whoever is most knowledgeable is most entitled to speak about them, and his opinion is subject to criticism and weighing by the scale of the established legal texts.)
Thus, under this situation, it is inconceivable that marriage in Islam is religious in the known sense of religious marriage today (which is the dependence of its conclusion and its results, in the view of religion, on the intervention of someone of religious authority in its procedures). And thus Islamic law is the first founder of civil marriage, which all modern positive law states have adopted.
(1) Narrated by al-Bukhari in his Sahih (4771) in the Book of Tafsir, and Muslim (206) in Iman, and al-Tirmidhi (3184) in Tafsir, and al-Nasa'i (3648) in Wasaya.

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa is an introductory exposition on the legal philosophy of Islamic family law. It begins by highlighting the revolutionary rights granted to women by Islamic law, such as inheritance, freedom in marriage, and full legal capacity to manage property independently. The author emphasizes that while men retain overall authority in family management, this does not undermine the wife's specific rights. The passage then outlines the three stages of family regulation: marriage and its dissolution, guardianship over minors, and inheritance. A significant point is that Islamic law allows non-Muslims to follow their own religious laws in personal matters. The second part focuses on the nature of marriage as a purely civil contract, arguing that it requires no religious official for validity, only mutual consent and two witnesses. The author supports this with Quranic verses and a hadith where the Prophet declares he cannot intercede for his daughter. The conclusion is that Islamic law pioneered the concept of civil marriage, later adopted by modern secular states.

Key Terms
زواج مدني (zawāj madanī): A marriage contract that is purely civil, concluded by mutual consent and witnesses, without any religious ritual or intervention of clergy.
عقد مدني ('aqd madanī): A contract that is worldly and legal, not requiring religious sanction for its validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Marriage as a Civil Contract: The Hanafi Evidence
Legal-philosophical argument with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the evidence that Islamic marriage is a civil contract and explain why no religious authority is required.

Core Idea
Islamic marriage requires no clergy — it is a civil contract completed by mutual consent and witnesses.

This lesson explores a foundational principle of Islamic family law: that marriage is a purely civil contract, not a religious sacrament. The passage argues that the Shari'ah made marriage like any other contract, valid solely by offer and acceptance before witnesses, without any need for a religious figure. This is because Islam has no clergy with exclusive authority; even the Prophet's role is limited to conveying revelation. Understanding this helps clarify why Islamic marriage is considered the prototype of modern civil marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Central Claim
Read the passage and locate the main ruling: marriage is concluded without religious intervention. Note the phrase 'civil contract'.
The claim is that marriage is purely civil, like other contracts.
2
Locate the Proofs
The author cites two proofs: a Quranic verse and a hadith. Identify each and note the key phrases.
Quran: 'For you is only the conveyance…' Hadith: 'O Fatima, I cannot avail you against God.'
3
Explain How Each Proof Supports
For the Quran: it limits the Prophet's role to conveying, implying no intercession. For the hadith: even the Prophet cannot intercede, so no clergy can either.
4
Consider the Implication for Modern Civil Marriage
The author concludes that Islamic law founded civil marriage. Think about how this contrasts with religious marriage in other traditions.
This principle remains operative today: a valid Islamic marriage does not require an imam or mosque.

(۱) رواه البخاري في صحيحه (٤٧٧١) في كتاب التفسير، ومسلم (۲۰٦) في الإيمان، والترمذي (٣١٨٤) في التفسير، والنسائي (٣٦٤٨) في الوصايا .
The Contracting of Marriage: 1/10 - The Shari'ah made marriage a purely civil contract like other contracts, so it is concluded, completed, and produces all its effects merely by the agreement of the two wills in the presence of two witnesses, without being contingent on the intervention of a religious authority, because there is no class of clergy in Islam who have a religious authority that others do not. For in the Shari'ah of Islam, there is no intercession between God and people; even the Prophet himself, who brought the Shari'ah, his religious task is confined to conveying, and his temporal authority to implementation, as the Qur'an says to him: 'For you is only the conveyance, you are only a warner, and for every people there is a guide.' So the Prophet himself has no religious authority by which to decide a person's fate with his Lord. Rather, every person enters religion by his faith and leaves it by his denial. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, said to his daughter: 'O Fatima, daughter of Muhammad, ask me whatever you wish of my wealth; I cannot avail you against God in anything.' So in Islam, people are distinguished in religious matters only by the measure of knowledge and action. The most knowledgeable of the rulings of the Shari'ah (which are established, written, public, not secret rulings, whoever is the most knowledgeable of them is the most entitled to speak about them, and his opinion is subject to criticism and weighing by the balance of established legal texts. So in light of this situation, it is unimaginable that marriage in Islam is religious in the sense known today as religious marriage (i.e., its conclusion and consequences in the view of religion depend on the intervention of someone with religious authority in its procedures). Thus, Islamic Shari'ah is the first founder of civil marriage, which all modern positive law states have adopted. (1) Narrated by al-Bukhari in his Sahih (4771) in the book of Tafsir, Muslim (206) in Iman, al-Tirmidhi (3184) in Tafsir, and al-Nasa'i (3648) in al-Wasaya.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical argument establishing that Islamic marriage is a civil contract, not a religious sacrament. The author begins by stating the ruling: marriage is concluded solely by offer and acceptance before two witnesses, without requiring a religious figure. The proof is that Islam has no clergy with exclusive religious authority; even the Prophet's role is limited to conveying revelation and implementing law, as shown by Quranic verses and a hadith where he tells his daughter he cannot intercede for her. The author concludes that early Islamic marriage was the prototype of modern civil marriage, later adopted by secular states. The argument relies on the absence of intercession and the equality of believers before God.

Key Terms
عقد مدني (ʿaqd madanī): A contract governed by civil law, not requiring religious authority for its validity.
مرجع ديني (marjiʿ dīnī): A religious authority or clergy whose intervention is necessary for a legal act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does Islamic marriage not require a religious authority?
A) Because the Prophet himself performed marriages without clergy.
B) Because Islam has no class of clergy with exclusive religious authority.
C) Because all contracts require only witnesses.
D) Because the Quran commands marriage without intermediaries.

Key Takeaway
Islamic marriage is a civil contract valid by mutual consent and two witnesses, without any need for a religious figure, because Islam has no clergy.

Prohibited Marriage Kinship and Polygamy
Overview of marital prohibitions and polygamy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify prohibited kinship categories in marriage and explain the Hanafi conditional permission of polygamy.

Core Idea
Marriage is forbidden with ascendants, descendants, siblings, and their children; cousins are allowed. Polygamy is permitted with conditions.

This lesson covers the essential Islamic rulings on who one may and may not marry due to blood ties. It also addresses polygamy, illustrating how Islamic law balances permissibility with restrictions to maintain family harmony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sets of prohibited relatives
The passage distinguishes between the person's own relatives and the relatives of the spouse. Understand each set.
For the person himself: ascendants, descendants, descendants of parents (siblings and their children), and first layer of descendants of grandparents (e.g., cousins).
2
Apply the prohibitions to specific examples
Test with examples: why a niece is prohibited but a cousin is permitted.
A niece is a descendant of a sibling, which falls under 'descendants of parents'. A cousin is a descendant of a grandparent's child, which is allowed.
3
Analyze the rationale for polygamy restrictions
The passage states a middle position between absolute permission and prohibition. Identify the conditions (justice, necessity, reality).
Polygamy is not unrestricted; it is tied to justice and social considerations.

الفصل (۱) نظرة عامة إلى الشريعة الإسلامية

القرابة المانعة للزواج : ۱۱/۱ - أما القرابة المانعة للزواج فبالنسبة إلى أقارب الشخص نفسه يمتنع زواجه بأحد أصوله أو فروعه أو فروع أبويه (أي الإخوة وأولادهم) مطلقا، وبالطبقة الأولى من فرع أجداده فبنت العم وبنت الخال يجوز تزوجهما .
وبالنسبة إلى أقارب أحد الزوجين يمتنع على كل منهما التزوج بأحد أصول الآخر أو فروعه.
•
وهنا تأتي مسألة تعدد الزوجات وهي مسألة معقدة الارتباطات والاعتبارات. وقد اختار الشرع الإسلامي فيها موقفاً وسطاً بين الإباحة المطلقة والمنع المطلق، فأباحه ضمن حدود وشرائط ناظراً إلى الأمر الواقع والحقائق الاجتماعية والضرورات الملجئة المتوقعة .

نتائج الزواج :
۱/ ۱۲ - ينتج الزواج في حكم الشريعة وجائب مالية وغير مالية : أ) - فمن حيث الوجيبة المالية، يكلف الرجل بنفقة الزوجة والأولاد بالقدر المتعارف في مستوى معيشة أمثال الزوج. ولا ينتج الزواج أي وجيبة مالية على الزوجة، كي تتفرغ لإدارة المنزل وتدبير الحياة الداخلية وتربية الأولاد.
ب) - ومن الناحية غير المالية توجب الشريعة على المرأة طاعة الرجل ضمن الحدود الشرعية، كما توجب عليها متابعته والسكنى معه. وتوجب الشريعة على كل منهما معاشرة الآخر بالحسنى والمودة. أما نسب الأولاد فكما يترتب على الزواج الصحيح يثبت أيضاً في الزواج غير الصحيح صيانة لحق الأولاد. وقد منعت الشريعة البنوّة الوهمية بطريق التبني : Adoptation حضرت البنوة وحقوقها بطريق التناسل الحقيقي.

انحلال الزواج :
١٣/١ - سوغت الشريعة انحلال الزواج بين الزوجين بالطلاق اجتناباً لمحاذير إلزامهما باستمرار الزوجية عند التنافر. ولكن الشريعة حفت الطلاق بالزواجر الدينية لأجل عدم استعماله إلا عند الضرورة الموجبة. وقد وصف النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الطلاق بأنه «أبغض الحلال إلى الله» (۱) .
وقد جعلت الشريعة الطلاق مبدئياً بيد الرجل لأنه بذلك يكون أقل ممارسة من جعله بيد كل منهما، ولا سيما أنَّ الزواج في الإسلام يكلف الرجل مالاً يدفعه للمرأة يدفعه للمرأة يسمى : مَهْراً. ولكن الشريعة فسحت للمرأة مجالاً للتخلص من الزوجية بطريق القضاء في حالات عديدة، منها ما إذا أساء الرجل معاملتها .

نتائج انحلال الزواج :
١٤/١ - رتبت الشريعة على انحلال الزواج بالموت أو بالطلاق تربص مدة معينة لا يجوز خلالها للمرأة أن تتزوج، كي يتحقق فراغها من الحمل، منعاً لاختلاط الأنساب وضياعها وهذه المدة تسمى: العدة وهي في حال الطلاق لها ارتباط بالحيض ولا تتجاوز عادة ثلاثة أشهر. وفي حال وفاة الزوج أربعة أشهر وعشرة أيام فإذا ظهر حمل لدى المرأة لا يجوز زواجها إلا بعد أن تضع حملها.

۲ - الولاية والوصاية
١٥/١ - ميّزت الشريعة بين الولاية والوصاية. فالولاية سلطة على شخص القاصر لتنشئته وتعليمه وسائر التصرفات المتعلقة بشخصه. وهذه قررت لها الشريعة نظاماً خاصاً يرجح فيه للولاية الأقرب فالأقرب إلى القاصر بشرط أن يكون الولي ذا أهلية وحَسَنَ التصرف. فالأب مقدم على الجد، والأخ يقدم على العم، وهكذا..
أما الوصاية فهي سلطة إدارية على مال القاصر لحفظه وإدارته
(1) أخرجه أبو داود (۲۱۷۸)، وابن ماجه (۲۰۱۸)، والحاكم ١٩٦/٢، والبيهقي ٣٢٢/٧ من حديث محارب بن دثار عن ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما قال الحافظ ابن حجر في التلخيص ٢٠٥/٣ : ورواه أبو داود والبيهقي مرسلاً ليس فيه ابن عمر، ورجح أبو خاتم والدارقطني في «العلل والبيهقي : المرسل.
Chapter 1: General Overview of Islamic Law
11/1 - Marital prohibitions due to kinship: As for the relatives of the person himself, he is absolutely prohibited from marrying any of his ascendants or descendants or the descendants of his parents (i.e., siblings and their children), and from the first layer of the descendants of his grandparents, so the daughter of an uncle and daughter of a maternal uncle may be married.
As for the relatives of one of the spouses, each is prohibited from marrying any ascendants or descendants of the other.
•
Here comes the issue of polygamy, which is a complex matter of ties and considerations. Islamic law chose a middle position between absolute permission and absolute prohibition, permitting it within limits and conditions, looking at reality, social facts, and anticipated compelling necessities.

Results of marriage:
12/1 - Marriage results, in the ruling of Islamic law, in financial and non-financial obligations: a) From the financial obligation side, the man is charged with providing for the wife and children according to the customary amount at the level of living of his peers. Marriage does not produce any financial obligation on the wife, so that she may devote herself to managing the household, directing internal life, and raising children.
b) From the non-financial side, Islamic law obliges the woman to obey the man within legal limits, and obliges her to follow him and reside with him. Islamic law obliges each to treat the other kindly and with affection. As for the lineage of children, just as it is established from a valid marriage, it is also established from an invalid marriage to protect the rights of children. Islamic law prohibited fictitious lineage through adoption; lineage is restricted to real procreation.

Dissolution of marriage:
13/1 - Islamic law permitted the dissolution of marriage between spouses by divorce to avoid the disadvantages of forcing them to continue marriage when there is discord. But Islamic law surrounded divorce with religious deterrents so that it would not be used except when necessary. The Prophet (peace be upon him) described divorce as 'the most hated lawful thing to Allah' (1).
Islamic law made divorce initially in the hand of the man, because it would be practiced less than if it were in the hand of each, especially since marriage in Islam costs the man money he pays to the woman called a dowry. But Islamic law provided the woman scope to free herself from marriage through the court in many cases, including if the man mistreats her.

Results of dissolution of marriage:
14/1 - Islamic law prescribed upon dissolution of marriage by death or divorce a waiting period of a specified time during which the woman may not marry, to ensure she is free from pregnancy, preventing mixing of lineages and their loss. This period is called 'iddah. In case of divorce, it is connected to menstruation and usually does not exceed three months. In case of the husband's death, it is four months and ten days. If pregnancy appears, she may not marry until she delivers.

2 - Guardianship and executorship
15/1 - Islamic law distinguished between guardianship (wilayah) and executorship (wisayah). Guardianship is authority over the person of a minor to raise him, educate him, and all other acts related to his person. For this, Islamic law established a special system giving precedence to the nearest relative to the minor, provided the guardian is qualified and good in conduct. The father is preferred over the grandfather, the brother over the uncle, and so on.
As for executorship, it is administrative authority over the minor's wealth to preserve and manage it.
(1) Narrated by Abu Dawud (2178), Ibn Majah (2018), al-Hakim 2/196, al-Bayhaqi 7/322 from the hadith of Muharib ibn Dithar from Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with them). Al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar said in al-Talkhis 3/205: Abu Dawud and al-Bayhaqi narrated it as mursal without Ibn Umar, and Abu Hatim and al-Daraqutni in al-'Ilal and al-Bayhaqi preferred the mursal version.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is an introductory overview of Islamic family law from a modern jurisprudential perspective. It covers essential rulings on marriage, divorce, and guardianship while explaining the underlying rationales. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, presents the Hanafi position as part of general Islamic law. The text begins by outlining prohibited marriage relationships based on blood kinship and affinity, noting that polygamy is permitted with conditions. It then describes the financial and non-financial effects of marriage: the husband bears financial responsibility while the wife is not obligated; the wife must obey and live with her husband; mutual kindness is required; lineage is protected even in invalid marriages, and adoption does not create legal parentage. Next, it explains that divorce is permitted but discouraged, with deterrents against its misuse. The husband initially holds the right to divorce, but wives can seek judicial dissolution. Following divorce or death, a waiting period ('iddah) is prescribed to establish non-pregnancy. Finally, the distinction between guardianship (wilayah over the person) and executorship (wisayah over property) is explained, with a priority system for guardians. The hadith at the end shows the evidentiary basis for the dislike of divorce. These rules reflect the maqasid (objectives) of preserving lineage, family stability, and financial justice.

Key Terms
القرابة المانعة للزواج (al-qarabah al-mani'ah li al-zawaj): Prohibited degrees of relationship that prevent marriage
تعدد الزوجات (ta'addud al-zawjat): Polygamy; marriage to more than one wife, up to four

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following marriages is absolutely prohibited?
A) Marriage to a first cousin (daughter of paternal uncle)
B) Marriage to a brother's daughter (niece)
C) Marriage to a maternal uncle's daughter
D) Marriage to a distant relative from the third layer

Key Takeaway
Marriage is prohibited with ascendants, descendants, siblings, and their children; polygamy is conditionally permitted.

Financial and Non-Financial Obligations of Marriage
Effects of marriage: financial and non-financial obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the financial obligations of the husband and the non-financial duties of the wife, and explain the rationale behind the division.

Core Idea
The husband must provide financially; the wife is not financially obligated. She must obey and reside with him, and both must treat each other kindly.

Marriage in Islam creates a set of rights and obligations designed to ensure family stability. This lesson examines the financial and non-financial consequences of a valid marriage, highlighting the complementary roles of husband and wife.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish financial from non-financial obligations
The passage clearly separates the two categories. Identify each.
Financial: husband's duty to provide for wife and children. Non-financial: wife's obedience, mutual kindness, and living together.
2
Explain why the wife has no financial obligation
The reason given is to allow her to manage the household and raise children. Understand the maqasid.
This is a central feature of Islamic family law: the wife's property is her own, and she is not required to spend on the family.
3
Analyze the concept of 'obedience' within legal limits
The wife must obey the husband in lawful matters. Identify what 'legal limits' means.
Obedience is not absolute; it is bounded by shariah. For example, she must not obey if he commands something haram.

Source Text

Dissolution of Marriage and Divorce
Dissolution of marriage: divorce and judicial remedy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why divorce is permitted but discouraged, and identify the wife's recourse through judicial dissolution.

Core Idea
Divorce is the most hated lawful thing; it is permitted only when necessary. The husband initially holds the right, but wives can seek judicial divorce.

Marriage is meant to be permanent, but Islam acknowledges that sometimes it cannot continue. This lesson explores the Islamic approach to divorce: it is permitted as a last resort, surrounded by deterrents to avoid misuse. The balance between husband's right and wife's recourse is examined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on divorce
The passage states divorce is permitted but has deterrents. Understand the hadith: 'the most hated lawful thing'.
This shows divorce is halal but makruh (disliked) when not necessary.
2
Analyze why the right is given to the husband initially
The text argues that it leads to less practice than if it were given to both. Also, the husband pays mahr.
This is a talil (reasoning) about practical effects.
3
Explain the wife's ability to seek dissolution through court
The passage mentions cases like mistreatment. Understand that this is a safeguard.
Wife can apply for khul' or judicial divorce under certain circumstances.

Source Text

الفصل (۱) نظرة عامة إلى الشريعة الإسلامية

القرابة المانعة للزواج : ۱۱/۱ - أما القرابة المانعة للزواج فبالنسبة إلى أقارب الشخص نفسه يمتنع زواجه بأحد أصوله أو فروعه أو فروع أبويه (أي الإخوة وأولادهم) مطلقا، وبالطبقة الأولى من فرع أجداده فبنت العم وبنت الخال يجوز تزوجهما .
وبالنسبة إلى أقارب أحد الزوجين يمتنع على كل منهما التزوج بأحد أصول الآخر أو فروعه.
•
وهنا تأتي مسألة تعدد الزوجات وهي مسألة معقدة الارتباطات والاعتبارات. وقد اختار الشرع الإسلامي فيها موقفاً وسطاً بين الإباحة المطلقة والمنع المطلق، فأباحه ضمن حدود وشرائط ناظراً إلى الأمر الواقع والحقائق الاجتماعية والضرورات الملجئة المتوقعة .

نتائج الزواج :
۱/ ۱۲ - ينتج الزواج في حكم الشريعة وجائب مالية وغير مالية : أ) - فمن حيث الوجيبة المالية، يكلف الرجل بنفقة الزوجة والأولاد بالقدر المتعارف في مستوى معيشة أمثال الزوج. ولا ينتج الزواج أي وجيبة مالية على الزوجة، كي تتفرغ لإدارة المنزل وتدبير الحياة الداخلية وتربية الأولاد.
ب) - ومن الناحية غير المالية توجب الشريعة على المرأة طاعة الرجل ضمن الحدود الشرعية، كما توجب عليها متابعته والسكنى معه. وتوجب الشريعة على كل منهما معاشرة الآخر بالحسنى والمودة. أما نسب الأولاد فكما يترتب على الزواج الصحيح يثبت أيضاً في الزواج غير الصحيح صيانة لحق الأولاد. وقد منعت الشريعة البنوّة الوهمية بطريق التبني : Adoptation حضرت البنوة وحقوقها بطريق التناسل الحقيقي.

انحلال الزواج :
١٣/١ - سوغت الشريعة انحلال الزواج بين الزوجين بالطلاق اجتناباً لمحاذير إلزامهما باستمرار الزوجية عند التنافر. ولكن الشريعة حفت الطلاق بالزواجر الدينية لأجل عدم استعماله إلا عند الضرورة الموجبة. وقد وصف النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الطلاق بأنه «أبغض الحلال إلى الله» (۱) .
وقد جعلت الشريعة الطلاق مبدئياً بيد الرجل لأنه بذلك يكون أقل ممارسة من جعله بيد كل منهما، ولا سيما أنَّ الزواج في الإسلام يكلف الرجل مالاً يدفعه للمرأة يدفعه للمرأة يسمى : مَهْراً. ولكن الشريعة فسحت للمرأة مجالاً للتخلص من الزوجية بطريق القضاء في حالات عديدة، منها ما إذا أساء الرجل معاملتها .

نتائج انحلال الزواج :
١٤/١ - رتبت الشريعة على انحلال الزواج بالموت أو بالطلاق تربص مدة معينة لا يجوز خلالها للمرأة أن تتزوج، كي يتحقق فراغها من الحمل، منعاً لاختلاط الأنساب وضياعها وهذه المدة تسمى: العدة وهي في حال الطلاق لها ارتباط بالحيض ولا تتجاوز عادة ثلاثة أشهر. وفي حال وفاة الزوج أربعة أشهر وعشرة أيام فإذا ظهر حمل لدى المرأة لا يجوز زواجها إلا بعد أن تضع حملها.

۲ - الولاية والوصاية
١٥/١ - ميّزت الشريعة بين الولاية والوصاية. فالولاية سلطة على شخص القاصر لتنشئته وتعليمه وسائر التصرفات المتعلقة بشخصه. وهذه قررت لها الشريعة نظاماً خاصاً يرجح فيه للولاية الأقرب فالأقرب إلى القاصر بشرط أن يكون الولي ذا أهلية وحَسَنَ التصرف. فالأب مقدم على الجد، والأخ يقدم على العم، وهكذا..
أما الوصاية فهي سلطة إدارية على مال القاصر لحفظه وإدارته
(1) أخرجه أبو داود (۲۱۷۸)، وابن ماجه (۲۰۱۸)، والحاكم ١٩٦/٢، والبيهقي ٣٢٢/٧ من حديث محارب بن دثار عن ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما قال الحافظ ابن حجر في التلخيص ٢٠٥/٣ : ورواه أبو داود والبيهقي مرسلاً ليس فيه ابن عمر، ورجح أبو خاتم والدارقطني في «العلل والبيهقي : المرسل.
Chapter 1: General Overview of Islamic Law
11/1 - Marital prohibitions due to kinship: As for the relatives of the person himself, he is absolutely prohibited from marrying any of his ascendants or descendants or the descendants of his parents (i.e., siblings and their children), and from the first layer of the descendants of his grandparents, so the daughter of an uncle and daughter of a maternal uncle may be married.
As for the relatives of one of the spouses, each is prohibited from marrying any ascendants or descendants of the other.
•
Here comes the issue of polygamy, which is a complex matter of ties and considerations. Islamic law chose a middle position between absolute permission and absolute prohibition, permitting it within limits and conditions, looking at reality, social facts, and anticipated compelling necessities.

Results of marriage:
12/1 - Marriage results, in the ruling of Islamic law, in financial and non-financial obligations: a) From the financial obligation side, the man is charged with providing for the wife and children according to the customary amount at the level of living of his peers. Marriage does not produce any financial obligation on the wife, so that she may devote herself to managing the household, directing internal life, and raising children.
b) From the non-financial side, Islamic law obliges the woman to obey the man within legal limits, and obliges her to follow him and reside with him. Islamic law obliges each to treat the other kindly and with affection. As for the lineage of children, just as it is established from a valid marriage, it is also established from an invalid marriage to protect the rights of children. Islamic law prohibited fictitious lineage through adoption; lineage is restricted to real procreation.

Dissolution of marriage:
13/1 - Islamic law permitted the dissolution of marriage between spouses by divorce to avoid the disadvantages of forcing them to continue marriage when there is discord. But Islamic law surrounded divorce with religious deterrents so that it would not be used except when necessary. The Prophet (peace be upon him) described divorce as 'the most hated lawful thing to Allah' (1).
Islamic law made divorce initially in the hand of the man, because it would be practiced less than if it were in the hand of each, especially since marriage in Islam costs the man money he pays to the woman called a dowry. But Islamic law provided the woman scope to free herself from marriage through the court in many cases, including if the man mistreats her.

Results of dissolution of marriage:
14/1 - Islamic law prescribed upon dissolution of marriage by death or divorce a waiting period of a specified time during which the woman may not marry, to ensure she is free from pregnancy, preventing mixing of lineages and their loss. This period is called 'iddah. In case of divorce, it is connected to menstruation and usually does not exceed three months. In case of the husband's death, it is four months and ten days. If pregnancy appears, she may not marry until she delivers.

2 - Guardianship and executorship
15/1 - Islamic law distinguished between guardianship (wilayah) and executorship (wisayah). Guardianship is authority over the person of a minor to raise him, educate him, and all other acts related to his person. For this, Islamic law established a special system giving precedence to the nearest relative to the minor, provided the guardian is qualified and good in conduct. The father is preferred over the grandfather, the brother over the uncle, and so on.
As for executorship, it is administrative authority over the minor's wealth to preserve and manage it.
(1) Narrated by Abu Dawud (2178), Ibn Majah (2018), al-Hakim 2/196, al-Bayhaqi 7/322 from the hadith of Muharib ibn Dithar from Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with them). Al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar said in al-Talkhis 3/205: Abu Dawud and al-Bayhaqi narrated it as mursal without Ibn Umar, and Abu Hatim and al-Daraqutni in al-'Ilal and al-Bayhaqi preferred the mursal version.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is an introductory overview of Islamic family law from a modern jurisprudential perspective. It covers essential rulings on marriage, divorce, and guardianship while explaining the underlying rationales. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, presents the Hanafi position as part of general Islamic law. The text begins by outlining prohibited marriage relationships based on blood kinship and affinity, noting that polygamy is permitted with conditions. It then describes the financial and non-financial effects of marriage: the husband bears financial responsibility while the wife is not obligated; the wife must obey and live with her husband; mutual kindness is required; lineage is protected even in invalid marriages, and adoption does not create legal parentage. Next, it explains that divorce is permitted but discouraged, with deterrents against its misuse. The husband initially holds the right to divorce, but wives can seek judicial dissolution. Following divorce or death, a waiting period ('iddah) is prescribed to establish non-pregnancy. Finally, the distinction between guardianship (wilayah over the person) and executorship (wisayah over property) is explained, with a priority system for guardians. The hadith at the end shows the evidentiary basis for the dislike of divorce. These rules reflect the maqasid (objectives) of preserving lineage, family stability, and financial justice.

Key Terms
الطلاق (al-talaq): Divorce; dissolution of marriage by the husband's pronouncement.
التطليق القضائي (al-tatliq al-qada'i): Judicial dissolution of marriage granted by a qadi upon the wife's request due to a valid ground.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on divorce according to the hadith cited in the passage?
A) It is haram.
B) It is the most hated lawful thing.
C) It is recommended.
D) It is an optional act with no moral value.

Key Takeaway
Divorce is permitted but strongly discouraged; wives can seek judicial dissolution when harmed.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
beginner
6 min
The Waiting Period ('Iddah) After Dissolution
Results of dissolution: waiting period ('iddah)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the purpose and duration of 'iddah after divorce and death, and apply the rules to different scenarios.

Core Idea
The 'iddah is a waiting period to confirm the womb is free; it is three months for divorce and four months ten days for widowhood, or until delivery if pregnant.

After a marriage dissolves, whether by divorce or death, Islamic law prescribes a waiting period called 'iddah. This lesson explains its purpose—to prevent confusion of lineage—and the specific durations for different situations. The rulings reflect a concern for clarity of parentage and respect for the deceased.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of 'iddah
The passage states it is to ensure the woman is free from pregnancy, preventing mixing of lineages.
This is a maqsad (objective) of family law: preserving lineage.
2
Distinguish the duration for divorce and death
For divorce: related to menstruation, usually three months. For death: four months and ten days. If pregnant, until delivery.
The death 'iddah is longer out of respect for the deceased and to observe mourning.
3
Apply to a specific case
Given a scenario, calculate the 'iddah period.
E.g., if a woman is divorced during pregnancy, her 'iddah ends upon delivery, even if before three months.

Source Text

Legal Philosophy and Theory
beginner
6 min
Guardianship (Wilayah) and Executorship (Wisayah) Over Minors
Guardianship vs. executorship over minors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between wilayah (guardianship over the person) and wisayah (executorship over property), and apply the Hanafi priority system for guardians.

Core Idea
Wilayah governs upbringing; wisayah manages property. The nearest qualified relative has priority for wilayah.

Islamic law carefully protects the interests of minors by assigning responsibilities for their personal and financial affairs. This lesson distinguishes between two concepts: guardianship (wilayah) for personal development and executorship (wisayah) for property management. The Hanafi priority system ensures the most suitable relative is entrusted with the child's welfare.

Guided Path
1
Differentiate wilayah and wisayah
Wilayah is authority over the person (upbringing, education). Wisayah is authority over property (management, preservation).
One person may hold both, but they are conceptually separate.
2
Apply the priority order for wilayah
The passage states: nearest relative first, with conditions of qualification and good conduct. Father before grandfather, brother before uncle, etc.
This is a standard Hanafi hierarchy.
3
Explain the conditions for a guardian
The guardian must have legal capacity (ahliyyah) and be of good conduct (hasan al-tasarruf).
These conditions ensure the minor's best interests.

Source Text

الفصل (۱) نظرة عامة إلى الشريعة الإسلامية

القرابة المانعة للزواج : ۱۱/۱ - أما القرابة المانعة للزواج فبالنسبة إلى أقارب الشخص نفسه يمتنع زواجه بأحد أصوله أو فروعه أو فروع أبويه (أي الإخوة وأولادهم) مطلقا، وبالطبقة الأولى من فرع أجداده فبنت العم وبنت الخال يجوز تزوجهما .
وبالنسبة إلى أقارب أحد الزوجين يمتنع على كل منهما التزوج بأحد أصول الآخر أو فروعه.
•
وهنا تأتي مسألة تعدد الزوجات وهي مسألة معقدة الارتباطات والاعتبارات. وقد اختار الشرع الإسلامي فيها موقفاً وسطاً بين الإباحة المطلقة والمنع المطلق، فأباحه ضمن حدود وشرائط ناظراً إلى الأمر الواقع والحقائق الاجتماعية والضرورات الملجئة المتوقعة .

نتائج الزواج :
۱/ ۱۲ - ينتج الزواج في حكم الشريعة وجائب مالية وغير مالية : أ) - فمن حيث الوجيبة المالية، يكلف الرجل بنفقة الزوجة والأولاد بالقدر المتعارف في مستوى معيشة أمثال الزوج. ولا ينتج الزواج أي وجيبة مالية على الزوجة، كي تتفرغ لإدارة المنزل وتدبير الحياة الداخلية وتربية الأولاد.
ب) - ومن الناحية غير المالية توجب الشريعة على المرأة طاعة الرجل ضمن الحدود الشرعية، كما توجب عليها متابعته والسكنى معه. وتوجب الشريعة على كل منهما معاشرة الآخر بالحسنى والمودة. أما نسب الأولاد فكما يترتب على الزواج الصحيح يثبت أيضاً في الزواج غير الصحيح صيانة لحق الأولاد. وقد منعت الشريعة البنوّة الوهمية بطريق التبني : Adoptation حضرت البنوة وحقوقها بطريق التناسل الحقيقي.

انحلال الزواج :
١٣/١ - سوغت الشريعة انحلال الزواج بين الزوجين بالطلاق اجتناباً لمحاذير إلزامهما باستمرار الزوجية عند التنافر. ولكن الشريعة حفت الطلاق بالزواجر الدينية لأجل عدم استعماله إلا عند الضرورة الموجبة. وقد وصف النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الطلاق بأنه «أبغض الحلال إلى الله» (۱) .
وقد جعلت الشريعة الطلاق مبدئياً بيد الرجل لأنه بذلك يكون أقل ممارسة من جعله بيد كل منهما، ولا سيما أنَّ الزواج في الإسلام يكلف الرجل مالاً يدفعه للمرأة يدفعه للمرأة يسمى : مَهْراً. ولكن الشريعة فسحت للمرأة مجالاً للتخلص من الزوجية بطريق القضاء في حالات عديدة، منها ما إذا أساء الرجل معاملتها .

نتائج انحلال الزواج :
١٤/١ - رتبت الشريعة على انحلال الزواج بالموت أو بالطلاق تربص مدة معينة لا يجوز خلالها للمرأة أن تتزوج، كي يتحقق فراغها من الحمل، منعاً لاختلاط الأنساب وضياعها وهذه المدة تسمى: العدة وهي في حال الطلاق لها ارتباط بالحيض ولا تتجاوز عادة ثلاثة أشهر. وفي حال وفاة الزوج أربعة أشهر وعشرة أيام فإذا ظهر حمل لدى المرأة لا يجوز زواجها إلا بعد أن تضع حملها.

۲ - الولاية والوصاية
١٥/١ - ميّزت الشريعة بين الولاية والوصاية. فالولاية سلطة على شخص القاصر لتنشئته وتعليمه وسائر التصرفات المتعلقة بشخصه. وهذه قررت لها الشريعة نظاماً خاصاً يرجح فيه للولاية الأقرب فالأقرب إلى القاصر بشرط أن يكون الولي ذا أهلية وحَسَنَ التصرف. فالأب مقدم على الجد، والأخ يقدم على العم، وهكذا..
أما الوصاية فهي سلطة إدارية على مال القاصر لحفظه وإدارته
(1) أخرجه أبو داود (۲۱۷۸)، وابن ماجه (۲۰۱۸)، والحاكم ١٩٦/٢، والبيهقي ٣٢٢/٧ من حديث محارب بن دثار عن ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما قال الحافظ ابن حجر في التلخيص ٢٠٥/٣ : ورواه أبو داود والبيهقي مرسلاً ليس فيه ابن عمر، ورجح أبو خاتم والدارقطني في «العلل والبيهقي : المرسل.
Chapter 1: General Overview of Islamic Law
11/1 - Marital prohibitions due to kinship: As for the relatives of the person himself, he is absolutely prohibited from marrying any of his ascendants or descendants or the descendants of his parents (i.e., siblings and their children), and from the first layer of the descendants of his grandparents, so the daughter of an uncle and daughter of a maternal uncle may be married.
As for the relatives of one of the spouses, each is prohibited from marrying any ascendants or descendants of the other.
•
Here comes the issue of polygamy, which is a complex matter of ties and considerations. Islamic law chose a middle position between absolute permission and absolute prohibition, permitting it within limits and conditions, looking at reality, social facts, and anticipated compelling necessities.

Results of marriage:
12/1 - Marriage results, in the ruling of Islamic law, in financial and non-financial obligations: a) From the financial obligation side, the man is charged with providing for the wife and children according to the customary amount at the level of living of his peers. Marriage does not produce any financial obligation on the wife, so that she may devote herself to managing the household, directing internal life, and raising children.
b) From the non-financial side, Islamic law obliges the woman to obey the man within legal limits, and obliges her to follow him and reside with him. Islamic law obliges each to treat the other kindly and with affection. As for the lineage of children, just as it is established from a valid marriage, it is also established from an invalid marriage to protect the rights of children. Islamic law prohibited fictitious lineage through adoption; lineage is restricted to real procreation.

Dissolution of marriage:
13/1 - Islamic law permitted the dissolution of marriage between spouses by divorce to avoid the disadvantages of forcing them to continue marriage when there is discord. But Islamic law surrounded divorce with religious deterrents so that it would not be used except when necessary. The Prophet (peace be upon him) described divorce as 'the most hated lawful thing to Allah' (1).
Islamic law made divorce initially in the hand of the man, because it would be practiced less than if it were in the hand of each, especially since marriage in Islam costs the man money he pays to the woman called a dowry. But Islamic law provided the woman scope to free herself from marriage through the court in many cases, including if the man mistreats her.

Results of dissolution of marriage:
14/1 - Islamic law prescribed upon dissolution of marriage by death or divorce a waiting period of a specified time during which the woman may not marry, to ensure she is free from pregnancy, preventing mixing of lineages and their loss. This period is called 'iddah. In case of divorce, it is connected to menstruation and usually does not exceed three months. In case of the husband's death, it is four months and ten days. If pregnancy appears, she may not marry until she delivers.

2 - Guardianship and executorship
15/1 - Islamic law distinguished between guardianship (wilayah) and executorship (wisayah). Guardianship is authority over the person of a minor to raise him, educate him, and all other acts related to his person. For this, Islamic law established a special system giving precedence to the nearest relative to the minor, provided the guardian is qualified and good in conduct. The father is preferred over the grandfather, the brother over the uncle, and so on.
As for executorship, it is administrative authority over the minor's wealth to preserve and manage it.
(1) Narrated by Abu Dawud (2178), Ibn Majah (2018), al-Hakim 2/196, al-Bayhaqi 7/322 from the hadith of Muharib ibn Dithar from Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with them). Al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar said in al-Talkhis 3/205: Abu Dawud and al-Bayhaqi narrated it as mursal without Ibn Umar, and Abu Hatim and al-Daraqutni in al-'Ilal and al-Bayhaqi preferred the mursal version.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is an introductory overview of Islamic family law from a modern jurisprudential perspective. It covers essential rulings on marriage, divorce, and guardianship while explaining the underlying rationales. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, presents the Hanafi position as part of general Islamic law. The text begins by outlining prohibited marriage relationships based on blood kinship and affinity, noting that polygamy is permitted with conditions. It then describes the financial and non-financial effects of marriage: the husband bears financial responsibility while the wife is not obligated; the wife must obey and live with her husband; mutual kindness is required; lineage is protected even in invalid marriages, and adoption does not create legal parentage. Next, it explains that divorce is permitted but discouraged, with deterrents against its misuse. The husband initially holds the right to divorce, but wives can seek judicial dissolution. Following divorce or death, a waiting period ('iddah) is prescribed to establish non-pregnancy. Finally, the distinction between guardianship (wilayah over the person) and executorship (wisayah over property) is explained, with a priority system for guardians. The hadith at the end shows the evidentiary basis for the dislike of divorce. These rules reflect the maqasid (objectives) of preserving lineage, family stability, and financial justice.

Key Terms
الولاية (al-wilayah): Guardianship authority over the person of a minor for upbringing and education.
الوصاية (al-wisayah): Executorship; administrative authority over a minor's property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The mother.
B) The father.
C) The grandfather.
D) The closest male relative after the father.

Key Takeaway
Wilayah controls the minor's person; wisayah controls property. The father has priority for guardianship.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Wisdom of Divorce and Iddah in Hanafi Law
Explaining the legal wisdom of divorce and iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rationale for placing divorce in the husband’s hand and explain the wisdom of iddah for preserving lineage.

Core Idea
Divorce is halal but detested; iddah protects lineage by ensuring no pregnancy from the prior marriage.

This lesson explores the legal philosophy behind two key family law institutions: talaq (divorce) and iddah (waiting period). You will see why Sharia places the power of unilateral divorce with the husband while still providing judicial remedies for the wife, and understand the essential role of iddah in preventing lineage confusion. This reasoning (ta'lil) helps a mufti appreciate the objectives (maqasid) behind the rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Basis
First, note that the passage explains that divorce is initially placed in the husband’s hand. The basis is a hadith declaring it the most detestable permissible act, plus the financial burden (mahr) he bears.
2
Examine the Wisdom (Hikmah)
Analyze the reasoning: why unilateral power? To minimize occurrence. Sharia also gives the wife judicial recourse, balancing rights. Then, consider iddah: its purpose is to verify freedom from pregnancy, preventing lineage confusion.
3
Apply the Ruling to a Modern Fatwa Context
As a mufti, you may face a case where a divorcée wishes to remarry quickly. Apply the iddah rule: she must wait three tuhrs (or three months if non‑menstruating) or, if pregnant, until delivery. The rationale (maqsad) of lineage preservation governs the fatwa.

الباب الأول: تمهيد وتعريف وتقسيم
بالزواجر الدينية لأجل عدم استعماله إلا عند الضرورة الموجبة. وقد وصف النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الطلاق بأنه «أبغض الحلال إلى الله» (۱) .
وقد جعلت الشريعة الطلاق مبدئياً بيد الرجل لأنه بذلك يكون أقل ممارسة من جعله بيد كل منهما، ولا سيما أنَّ الزواج في الإسلام يكلف الرجل مالاً يدفعه للمرأة يدفعه للمرأة يسمى : مَهْراً. ولكن الشريعة فسحت للمرأة مجالاً للتخلص من الزوجية بطريق القضاء في حالات عديدة، منها ما إذا أساء الرجل معاملتها .
نتائج انحلال الزواج :
١٤/١ - رتبت الشريعة على انحلال الزواج بالموت أو بالطلاق تربص مدة معينة لا يجوز خلالها للمرأة أن تتزوج، كي يتحقق فراغها من الحمل، منعاً لاختلاط الأنساب وضياعها وهذه المدة تسمى: العدة وهي في حال الطلاق لها ارتباط بالحيض ولا تتجاوز عادة ثلاثة أشهر. وفي حال وفاة الزوج أربعة أشهر وعشرة أيام فإذا ظهر حمل لدى المرأة لا يجوز زواجها إلا بعد أن تضع حملها.
Chapter One: Introduction, Definition, and Division
...by religious deterrents so that it is not used except when necessary. The Prophet ﷺ described divorce as 'the most detestable of permissible things to Allah'.
Sharia initially placed divorce in the husband's hand because that leads to less practice than placing it in the hands of both, especially since marriage in Islam costs the husband money he pays to the woman called mahr. But Sharia gave the woman a way to end the marriage through the judiciary in many cases, including when the husband mistreats her.
Consequences of Dissolution of Marriage:
14/1 - Sharia prescribed upon dissolution of marriage by death or divorce a waiting period during which the woman may not marry, to verify her freedom from pregnancy, preventing mixing of lineages. This period is called iddah; in case of divorce it is linked to menstruation and usually does not exceed three months. In case of the husband's death, it is four months and ten days. If the woman is pregnant, she may not marry until she delivers.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical overview of divorce (talaq) and the post-dissolution waiting period (iddah) in Hanafi law. The author begins by emphasizing that while divorce is permissible (halal), it is the most detested permissible act, as reported in the hadith. Sharia places the power of divorce primarily in the husband's hand because making it bilateral would increase its incidence; additionally, the husband bears the financial burden of mahr, which discourages frivolous divorce. However, the wife is not without recourse: she can seek judicial dissolution in cases of harm. The passage then explains the rationale for iddah: to avoid confusion of lineage by ensuring the uterus is free from pregnancy. The duration varies: for a divorced woman, it is three menstrual cycles (or three months for those who do not menstruate); for a widow, four months and ten days; and for a pregnant woman, it lasts until delivery. This ruling is based on Quranic verses (2:228, 2:234). In the Hanafi school, the iddah of a divorced woman is three tuhrs (pure periods), as the Qur'anic 'quru' is interpreted as purity, not menstruation. The passage implicitly adopts the Hanafi view by stating 'her связи with menstruation' and 'usually does not exceed three months', which aligns with the Hanafi position that the iddah is based on menstrual cycles.

Key Terms
الطلاق (al-talaq): Divorce; the dissolution of marriage by the husband's pronouncement.
العدة (al-iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarrying.
المهر (al-mahr): The mandatory bridal gift given by the husband to the wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the Hanafi school interpret the term 'quru' in the Quranic verse on iddah?
A) Menstrual periods
B) Pure periods (tuhr)
C) Both equally
D) Lunar months

Key Takeaway
Divorce and iddah rulings are anchored in the maqsad of lineage preservation, which a mufti must always uphold when issuing fatwas.

Inheritance and Family Law in Islam
Survey of inheritance and family law principles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Islamic inheritance system's rationale for the daughter's half share by linking it to the husband's financial obligations (nafaqa) in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A daughter's half share is not discrimination but a result of the man's duty to support the family.

In this passage, Mustafa al-Zarqa outlines the Islamic law of inheritance and addresses a common misunderstanding about the daughter's share. He emphasizes that when the man's financial burdens are considered, the daughter often benefits more. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and explores the underlying principles of family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the inheritance principle
Read the passage and note how proximity to the deceased determines shares. Note that primogeniture is rejected.
2
State the daughter's share rule
The daughter receives half the share of a son. This is often criticized.
3
Analyze the counter-argument
Link the daughter's smaller share to the man's obligation to provide nafaqa. Consider the net benefit.
4
Acknowledge cross-madhhab differences
The passage notes that different schools have different rulings on divorce and judicial separation, showing the richness of fiqh.

۲ - وزّعت الشريعة الإرثَ بين الأقارب بنظام خاص روعي فيه الأقربية إلى الميت. فتارة يُحجب الأبعد بالأقرب ؛ وتارة يشتركان ويكون للأقرب القسط الأكبر، وذلك بحسب درجة القرب والبعد. ويُحسب للحمل حساب عند وفاة مورثه فيوقف له نصيب يأخذه إن وُلِدَ حيّاً.

٤- للبنت نصف حصة أخيها الذكر. وهذه المسألة كثيراً ما يُساء فهمها وتُظن غبناً للأنثى في الشريعة؛ ولكنها في الحقيقة مرتبطة بنظام التكليف المالي في الأسرة. فالشريعة الإسلامية تكلف الرجل بنفقة زوجته وأولاده فالبنت قبل الزواج تكون نفقتها على الأقرب من رجال أسرتها، وبعد الزواج نفقتها على الزوج، ولا تكلف الزوجة شيئاً من نفقة أولادها، فإذا أخذت في الإرث نصف ما يأخذ أخوها المكلف بنفقة نفسه وزوجته وأولاده وبنفقة أخته أيضاً إذا تأيمت ترجع البنت عندئذ أوفر حظاً من أخيها في الميراث.
ويلحظ في هذا المقام أن تفاصيل أحكام الأحوال الشخصية التي عرضنا هنا أهم النقاط الأساسية منها يوجد في الاجتهادات الإسلامية ومذاهبها اختلافات في تفاريع جوهرية منها. ويوجد في كل من المذاهب الفقهية أحكام ونظريات في بعض التفرعات أفضل من غيره وأصلح نتيجة في التطبيق، كحق إنهاء الزواج قضائياً بطلب المرأة إذا أساء الرجل عشرتها في الاجتهاد المالكي وكون الطلاق في أغلب الأحيان رجعياً قابلاً لأن يرجع فيه الرجل خلال عدة المرأة في الاجتهاد الشافعي.

في الالتزامات :

۱۷/۱ - اعتبرت الشريعة كل فعل ضار بالغير موجباً مسؤولية الفاعل أو المتسبب، والتزامه بالتعويض عن الضَّرر ولو كان عن خطأ. فإن كان عن عمد يوجب أيضاً عقوبة الفاعل. وهذا المبدأ تضمنه الحديث النبوي القائل: (لا ضرر ولا ضرار » (۱) وهذا غير ما توجبه الشريعة من التزامات أخرى مصدرها الإرادة المنفردة كالوقف، أو إرادة الشارع كنفقة الأقارب. وقد اعتبرت جميع الالتزامات مضمونة بتأييد القضاء. فللقاضي سلطة واسعة لا حدود لها في جبر كل إنسان على تنفيذ التزامه ولو كان هو الخليفة وحوادث قضاء القضاة على الخلفاء والملوك معروفة كثيرة في التاريخ الإسلامي.
٢ - في العقود :
أقرت الشريعة الإسلامية في العقود الأسس التالية :
أولاً - العقد المشروع مُلْزِم لعاقده دون غيره كما أن إقرار الشخص لا يسري إلا على نفسه وحقوقه والتزام العاقد ينتقل إلى من يخلفه كالوارث أو الموصى له. وهذا المبدأ تضمنته الآية القرآنية في أول سورة المائدة: يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا أَوْفُوا بِالْعُقُودِ .

(۱) رواه مالك في الموطأ» ۷٤۵/۲ عن عمرو بن يحيى عن أبيه مرسلاً في الأقضية، باب القضاء في المَرْفق، ورواه ابن ماجه من حديث عبادة بن الصامت (۲۳٤۰)، وفيه انقطاع. ومن حديث جابر الجعفي (۲۳٤۱) وهو ضعيف. ورواه الدارقطني والحاكم والبيهقي من حديث أبي سعيد الخدري قال النووي في الأربعين) في الحديث ۳۲: له طرق يقوى بعضها ببعض. وقال ابن الصلاح هذا الحديث أسنده الدارقطني من وجوه ومجموعها يقوّي الحديث ويحسّنه. وقد تقبله جماهير أهل العلم واحتجوا به.

Chapter One: Introduction, Definition, and Classification
2 - The Sharia distributed inheritance among relatives according to a special system in which proximity to the deceased was taken into account. Sometimes the more distant is blocked by the nearer; sometimes they share and the nearer gets the larger portion, according to the degree of closeness or distance. The fetus is taken into account upon the death of its inheritor, so a share is reserved for it, which it takes if born alive.
- The eldest son has no privilege in inheritance or precedence over his younger brother, contrary to some European laws that still today follow in inheritance the rule of primogeniture called: droit d'ainé.
+
4 - The daughter gets half the share of her male brother. This issue is often misunderstood and thought to be a disadvantage for women in the Sharia; but in reality it is linked to the system of financial responsibility in the family. Islamic Sharia obligates the man to support his wife and children. Before marriage, the daughter's maintenance falls on the closest male relatives of her family; after marriage, her maintenance falls on the husband. The wife is not obligated to spend anything on her children. So if she takes in inheritance half of what her brother takes, who is obligated to support himself, his wife, his children, and also his sister if she becomes widowed, then the daughter ends up with a greater fortune than her brother in inheritance.
It is noted in this regard that the details of the rulings on personal status, of which we have presented the most important basic points, there are differences among Islamic juristic efforts and their schools in essential subsidiary matters. In each of the juristic schools there are rulings and theories in some branches that are better than others and more suitable in application, such as the right to judicially terminate marriage at the request of the woman if the man mistreats her in the Maliki school, and that divorce is in most cases revocable, allowing the man to take back the wife during her waiting period in the Shafi'i school.
1
B - In Civil Rights (Transactions)
On Obligations:
:
17/1 - The Sharia considered every harmful act to another as giving rise to the liability of the actor or the cause, and his obligation to compensate for the harm, even if due to negligence. If intentional, it also necessitates punishment of the actor. This principle is contained in the prophetic hadith: 'There is to be no harm and no reciprocating harm.' (1) This is besides other obligations imposed by Sharia whose source is the unilateral will, such as endowment, or the will of the Lawgiver, such as maintenance of relatives. All obligations are considered guaranteed by the backing of the judiciary. The judge has broad, unlimited authority to compel every person to fulfill their obligation, even if he is the caliph. Incidents of judges judging over caliphs and kings are many and well-known in Islamic history.
2 - On Contracts:
Islamic Sharia established the following foundations for contracts:
First: A legitimate contract is binding on its party alone, just as a person's acknowledgment only applies to himself and his rights. The obligation of the contracting party transfers to his successor, such as the heir or legatee. This principle is contained in the Quranic verse at the beginning of Surah al-Ma'idah: 'O you who have believed, fulfill (your) contracts.'
Second: Contractual conditions are free and binding on the contracting parties except what contradicts public order and morals. This principle is contained in the prophetic hadith: 'Muslims are bound by their conditions, except a condition that makes lawful what is unlawful or makes unlawful what is lawful.' (2)
(1) Reported by Malik in al-Muwatta', 2/745 from Amr ibn Yahya from his father as a mursal in the chapter of Judgments, section on Judging about pathways. Also reported by Ibn Majah from the hadith of Ubadah ibn al-Samit (2340), but with a break. And from the hadith of Jabir al-Ju'fi (2341), which is weak. And reported by al-Daraqutni, al-Hakim, and al-Bayhaqi from the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri. Al-Nawawi in al-Arba'in said in hadith 32: It has chains that strengthen each other. Ibn al-Salah said: This hadith was narrated by al-Daraqutni from multiple paths, and the totality strengthens the hadith and makes it good. The majority of scholars have accepted it and used it as evidence.
(2) Reported by al-Tirmidhi (1352) in Judgments, and he said: A hasan sahih hadith. And by Abu Dawud (3594) in Judgments, with a good chain. Both with the wording: 'Muslims are upon their conditions,' except that Abu Dawud's narration ends at 'their conditions.'
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a general overview of key principles in Islamic Sharia, covering inheritance, family law, and civil transactions. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, a modern Hanafi jurist, presents these principles in a comparative and explanatory manner.

**Inheritance:** The Sharia distributes inheritance based on proximity to the deceased. Closer relatives block more distant ones, or they share with the nearer receiving a larger portion. The fetus is considered: a share is reserved and given if born alive. There is no primogeniture; all children are equal, unlike some European systems. The daughter's share is half that of her brother, which is often misunderstood. The author explains that this is tied to the man's financial obligations: he must support his wife, children, and needy female relatives. Thus, when both receive inheritance, the daughter often ends up with more net benefit because she has no such obligations.

**Family Law Differences:** The author notes that among Islamic madhhabs, there are substantive differences in subsidiary rules. For example, the Maliki school allows a woman to seek judicial divorce if her husband treats her badly, while the Shafi'i school often considers divorce revocable (raj'i), allowing the husband to take back his wife during her waiting period. This illustrates the diversity within Islamic jurisprudence.

**Obligations (Liability):** The Sharia holds a person liable for any harmful act, whether direct or indirect, even if unintentional. Intentional harm may also incur punishment. This is based on the hadith 'La darar wa la dirar' (no harm and no reciprocating harm). This principle covers both tort liability and criminal penalties. Obligations arise from various sources: unilateral will (e.g., waqf), divine command (e.g., maintenance of relatives), and are enforceable by the judiciary, even against the caliph.

**Contracts:** Two foundational principles are presented: (1) A legitimate contract is binding only on the parties and is inherited by their heirs or legatees, based on the Quranic verse 'Fulfill your contracts.' (2) Contractual conditions are free and binding except those that violate public order and morals, based on the hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions except a condition that makes lawful what is unlawful or makes unlawful what is lawful.' These principles emphasize the sanctity of contracts and freedom of contracting within Islamic limits.

The passage is a legal philosophy exposition, using scriptural evidence (Quran and hadith) to support the principles.

Key Terms
إرث (irth): The transfer of property from a deceased to his heirs according to Sharia rules.
نفقة (nafaqa): Financial maintenance and support legally required for family members.
عقد (aqd): A legally binding agreement between two or more parties.
شرط (shart): A stipulation or condition made within a contract.
لا ضرر ولا ضرار (la darar wa la dirar): A legal maxim meaning there is no inflicting harm nor responding to harm with harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the daughter receive half the share of her brother in inheritance?
A) Because males are superior.
B) Because the man is obligated to support the family.
C) Because the daughter is not entitled to maintenance.
D) Because of primogeniture.

Key Takeaway
Islamic inheritance law accounts for the overall financial system of the family, making the daughter's half share equitable when men's duties are considered.

Foundations of Obligations and Contracts: the Qawida 'La Darar wa La Dirar' and the Sanctity of Contracts
Foundations of liability and contract law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the legal maxim 'La darar wa la dirar' to determine liability and state the Hanafi principles of contract fulfillment and freedom of conditions.

Core Idea
Harm must be compensated, and contracts are sacred except when they violate the Sharia.

This part of the passage introduces the foundational legal maxim of liability and the Quranic and prophetic bases for contract law. Al-Zarqa explains that every harmful act gives rise to liability, and that contracts are binding with free conditions within Islamic limits. This lesson explores these two pillars of civil transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of liability
Read the hadith 'La darar wa la dirar' and note its meaning and authentication notes.
2
Determine the scope of liability
The passage states that every harmful act, even if due to negligence, leads to liability. Intentional harm also incurs punishment.
3
Apply the qawida to a scenario
Consider a hypothetical: if a person accidentally damages another's property, they must compensate. This is derived from the maxim.
4
State the first contract principle
The Quranic verse 'Fulfill your contracts' establishes that contracts are binding on the parties and their successors.
5
State the second contract principle
The hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions except a condition that makes lawful what is unlawful' establishes freedom of conditions within public order.

The Principle of No Harm (La Darar wa La Dirar)
States the principle of liability for harm with hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'la darar wa la dirar', identify its hadith source, and apply it to determine liability for unintentional harm.

Core Idea
No harm is permitted: causing harm, even unintentionally, creates liability for compensation.

This lesson focuses on a cornerstone of Islamic law of obligations: the maxim 'la darar wa la dirar'. The passage explains that this prophetic hadith establishes liability for harm regardless of intent, and distinguishes such obligations from those arising from unilateral will or divine command. Understanding this maxim is essential for a mufti ruling on torts and damages.

Guided Path
1
State the principle of liability
Identify the core claim: causing harm creates an obligation to compensate, and intentional harm also incurs punishment.
The passage begins with 'or the one who causes...' as a continuation.
2
Locate the hadith evidence
Recognize that the hadith 'la darar wa la dirar' is the basis for this principle.
The hadith is cited in parentheses.
3
Assess the hadith's authority
Understand that the hadith, though transmitted through multiple weak chains, is strengthened to hasan and accepted by the majority of scholars.
The footnote provides details on its authentication.
4
Apply the principle to unintentional harm
Note that compensation is due even without intent, as the maxim prohibits all harm.
The text explicitly says 'even if unintentional'.
5
Distinguish from intentional harm
Intentional harm also incurs punishment in addition to compensation.
This is stated with 'then also punishment of the actor'.

Commentary
This passage begins by stating a fundamental principle of Islamic law of obligations: liability for harm. The author explains that if someone causes harm, even unintentionally, they must compensate. If intentional, also punishment. This is based on the famous hadith 'la darar wa la dirar' which is a major legal maxim. The author then distinguishes these obligations from those arising from unilateral will (like waqf) or the lawgiver's command (like nafaqa). Finally, he emphasizes that all obligations are enforceable by the judiciary, and the judge has vast powers to compel performance, even if the person is the caliph. This illustrates the supremacy of law in Islam.

Key Terms
الضرر (al-darar): Harm or injury, whether physical or financial, that gives rise to liability and compensation.
التعويض (al-ta'wid): Compensation for harm caused, even if unintentional.
القضاء (al-qada'): The judiciary with authority to enforce obligations, including against the caliph.

A) Quranic verse
B) Hadith 'La darar wa la dirar'
C) Consensus
D) Analogy

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'la darar wa la dirar' establishes liability for harm regardless of intent, and is a fundamental pillar of Islamic law of obligations.

Judicial Enforcement of Obligations
Discusses enforceability of obligations through judiciary with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle that all obligations are enforceable by the judiciary, even against the ruler.

Core Idea
Judiciary can compel every person, including the caliph, to fulfill obligations.

This lesson explores the second part of the passage: the enforceability of obligations. The text states that all obligations are guaranteed by judicial authority, and the judge has unlimited power to enforce them, even against the caliph. This underscores the rule of law in Islamic society and is crucial for muftis advising on legal recourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of obligations
Note that obligations arise from harm, unilateral will (waqf), and the will of the Lawgiver (nafaqa).
The passage distinguishes these from the harm-based obligations.
2
Understand the judicial guarantee
All obligations are 'guaranteed by judicial support' meaning they can be enforced in court.
The phrase 'madminah bi ta'yid al-qada' indicates mandatory enforceability.
3
Recognize the judge's authority
The judge has broad unlimited authority to compel performance, including against the caliph.
The text explicitly says 'even if he is the caliph'.
4
Cite historical evidence
The author notes numerous incidents of judges ruling against caliphs and kings.
This confirms that the principle was practiced.

Commentary
This passage begins by stating a fundamental principle of Islamic law of obligations: liability for harm. The author explains that if someone causes harm, even unintentionally, they must compensate. If intentional, also punishment. This is based on the famous hadith 'la darar wa la dirar' which is a major legal maxim. This illustrates the supremacy of law in Islam.

التعويض (al-ta'wid): Compensation for harm caused, even if unintentional.
القضاء (al-qada'): The judiciary with authority to enforce obligations, including against the caliph.

Five Fundamental Principles of Contracts in Islamic Law
Enumerates five key principles governing contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and explain the five fundamental principles of contracts in Islamic law as recognized in the Hanafi school, and identify the textual evidence for each.

Core Idea
Contracts in Islam are consensual, binding, and governed by good faith, with freedom of conditions and custom as interpretive sources.

Islamic law establishes a clear framework for contracts, balancing binding force with flexibility. This lesson unpacks five core principles derived from the Quran and Sunnah, which form the backbone of Hanafi mu'amalat. Understanding these principles is essential for structuring valid transactions and resolving disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first principle and its evidence
Locate the principle that contracts are binding on the contractor and successors. Note the Quranic verse cited (5:1). Understand that obligation transfers to heirs.
This principle ensures contractual stability.
2
Analyze the second principle: freedom of conditions
Identify the rule that contractual conditions are free except those violating public order or morals. Review the hadith on conditions.
The exception protects core Sharia values.
3
Understand consensuality (third principle)
Contrast Islamic consensuality with formalistic systems of Romans and pre-Islamic Arabs. Note that marriage requires witnesses but remains consensual.
Offer and acceptance suffice for most contracts.
4
Grasp the role of good faith (fourth principle)
Identify the requirement of good faith in formation and execution. Note that fraud gives an option to rescind.
This protects the weaker party.
5
Examine the role of custom (fifth principle)
Recognize custom as a source for interpreting silent terms, unless it contradicts a specific text. Apply to lease payment methods.
Custom is widely considered in Hanafi jurisprudence.
