2 - Contracts: Islamic Sharia established the following foundations for contracts: First: A legitimate contract is binding on its contractor alone, just as a person's acknowledgment applies only to himself and his rights, and the obligation of the contractor transfers to his successors such as heir or legatee. This principle is encompassed by the Quranic verse at the beginning of Surat al-Ma'idah: 'O you who believe, fulfill your contracts.' Second: Contractual conditions are free and binding on the contractors except what violates public order and morals. This principle is encompassed by the prophetic hadith: 'Muslims are bound by their conditions, except a condition that permits what is forbidden or forbids what is permissible.' (2) (see: f 2/10 footnote). Third: All contracts are consensual, i.e., they are formed by free mutual agreement merely by agreement between the two parties without requiring any formal rituals. Even the marriage contract it suffices for its formation the mere mutual consent of the man and woman with offer and acceptance in the presence of two witnesses; whereas all contracts among the Romans and some among the Arabs were formal contracts subject to strange rituals and movements that the contractors had to perform, and mere mutual consent was not sufficient. And the effects of formality of contracts continued as an inheritance from the Romans in European laws until the late eighteenth century, when consensuality of contracts was only then established in modern laws. Fourth: Sharia required good faith in the formation and execution of contracts, and made any fraud, deceit, or misrepresentation by one of the contractors grounds for the other's option and justification for him to rescind the contract due to undermining mutual consent. Fifth: Custom and usage were considered a basis for defining the limits of contractual obligations and rights in everything that the text of the contract is silent about: for example, the method of using the leased item and the manner of payment of rent follow the custom of the people. Custom has broad judicial consideration in every place as long as it does not conflict with a specific legal text on the matter, such as the custom of committing to a gambling debt - it is disregarded. (2) Narrated by al-Tirmidhi...
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
Here the author lays out five foundational principles of contracts in Islamic law. First, a contract is binding only on the parties, and obligations transfer to heirs/legatees. The evidence is the Quranic verse 'Awfu bil 'uqud'. Second, freedom of conditions except those violating public order, based on the hadith 'al-muslimoon 'ala shurootihim'. Third, all contracts are consensual; no formalities are required beyond mutual agreement. The author contrasts this with Roman and pre-Islamic Arab formalism, noting that European law only adopted consensuality in the late 18th century. Fourth, good faith and prohibition of fraud, giving the defrauded party an option to rescind. Fifth, custom is a source for interpreting contractual terms, unless it conflicts with a specific textual ruling. These principles are central to Hanafi mu'amalat.

Key Terms
العقود (al-'uqud): Contracts, defined as legally binding agreements between parties.
الرضائية (al-ridaiyya): Consensual nature of contracts meaning formation by mutual agreement without formalities.
العرف (al-'urf): Custom and usage that guide contractual interpretation when the contract is silent, provided it does not contradict a specific legal text.

Islamic contract law rests on five pillars: binding effect, freedom of conditions, consensuality, good faith, and respect for custom — all derived from Quran and Sunnah.

Two Foundations of Islamic Criminal Law
Foundational principles of Islamic criminal law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two foundations of the Islamic penal system and explain the distinction between hudud and tazir crimes.

Core Idea
In Islamic law, every prohibited act is punishable, but most punishments are discretionary (tazir), not fixed.

This passage sets out the overarching principles that govern Islamic criminal law. It emphasises that the Sharia does not limit punitive response to a few named crimes; rather, any forbidden act can be a crime. Crucially, it distinguishes between a small set of crimes with predetermined punishments (hudud) and the vast majority that are left to the discretion of the authority (tazir). Understanding this framework is essential for any mufti weighing the appropriate response to a new case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two foundations
Read the opening sentence: 'أقامت الشريعة نظام العقوبات على أساسين'. This signals that two principles will be listed.
2
Understand the first foundation
The text states that every prohibited act (كل فعل ممنوع) is a crime, and every crime must be punished justly, even if no specific punishment is predetermined. This establishes the principle that all haram acts are subject to punishment.
3
Understand the second foundation
The text states that punishments for most crimes are left undetermined except for five (hudud). The authority (sultan/judge) can determine appropriate punishment based on circumstances.
The five hudud in the Hanafi school are: theft, robbery (hirabah), zina (illicit sexual relations), qadhf (false accusation of zina), and drinking alcohol.
4
Note the implication
The discretion of the authority allows punishments to vary according to the severity of the act and the situation, ensuring justice and social order.
5
Relate to Hanafi fiqh
In Hanafi fiqh, tazir punishments are not fixed and can include imprisonment, flogging, fines, or other corrective measures. The mufti or judge determines what is proportionate.

Most crimes do not have fixed punishments (they are tazir), except five (the hudud). The authority (sultan or judge) has discretion to determine appropriate punishment based on circumstances. This shows the flexibility and justice of Islamic criminal law.

Key Terms
الجرائم (al-jara'im): Crimes, defined as any prohibited act that warrants punishment.
العقوبات (al-'uqubat): Punishments, including both fixed (hudud) and discretionary (tazir).
تأديب (ta'dib): Discipline, the purpose of punishment to reform the perpetrator and maintain social order.

Consensual Contracts, Good Faith, and Custom in Islamic Law
Explaining the consensual nature of contracts and the role of good faith and custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principles of consensual contract, good faith, and custom to identify valid contractual terms and evaluate customary practices against textual rulings.

Core Idea
Islamic contracts are formed by mutual consent alone; fraud voids consent, and custom fills gaps unless it contradicts a clear text.

Islamic contract law is built on the principle of mutual consent without formalities. This lesson explores three foundational principles: the consensual nature of contracts, the requirement of good faith, and the role of custom. You will learn how these principles operate in practice and how they guide muftis in resolving contractual disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract principle
Recognize that consent is the core element; formalities are not required. Even marriage is consensual with offer, acceptance, and witnesses.
Contrast with Roman law which required formal rituals.
2
Distinguish valid from vitiated consent
Good faith requires honesty; fraud, deceit, or misrepresentation gives the deceived party an option to rescind.
This protects the integrity of consent.
3
Apply custom as a gap-filler
Custom determines obligations and rights where the contract is silent, as long as it does not contradict a specific textual ruling.
Example: method of payment follows custom.
4
Integrate all three in a fatwa scenario
Combine the principles to analyze a real-world case: check consent, good faith, and then apply custom if needed.
Ensure custom doesn't override a clear text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثاً - العقود كلها رضائية أي أنها تنعقد بالتراضي الحر بمجرد الاتفاق بين الطرفين دون اشتراط شيء من المراسم الشكلية. حتى أن عقد الزواج يكفي لانعقاده مجرد تراضي الرجل والمرأة بإيجاب وقبول بحضور شاهِدَيْن؛ بينما كانت العقود كافة لدى الرومان وبعضها لدى العرب عقوداً شكلية خاضعة لمراسم وحركات غريبة يجب أن يجريها العاقدان ولا يكفي فيها مجرد التراضي واستمرت آثار شكلية العقود وراثة عن الرومان في الحقوق الأوروبية إلى أواخر القرن الثامن عشر حيث تقررت إذ ذاك فقط رضائية العقود في القوانين الحديثة .
رابعاً - أوجبت الشريعة في إنشاء العقود وتنفيذها حسن النية La bonne Foi وجعلت كل غش أو تدليس أو تغرير من أحد العاقدين موجباً خيار الآخر ومسوّغاً له فسخ العقد لإخلاله بالتراضي.
خامساً - اعتبرت العرف والعادة أساساً لتحديد حدود الالتزامات والحقوق العقدية في كل ما سكت عنه نص العقد: فطريقة استعمال المأجور مثلاً، وكيفية دفع الأجرة يتبع فيه عرف الناس.
وللعرف اعتبار قضائي واسع المدى في كل موضع ما لم يصادم نصاً شرعياً خاصاً في موضوعه كالتعارف على الالتزام بدين القمار فإنه لا عبرة له
Thirdly - All contracts are consensual, meaning they are concluded by free mutual consent upon the mere agreement between the two parties without requiring any formalities. Even the marriage contract is sufficiently concluded by the mere consent of the man and woman with an offer and acceptance in the presence of two witnesses; whereas all contracts among the Romans and some among the Arabs were formal contracts subject to strange rituals and movements that the contracting parties had to perform, and mere consent was not sufficient. The effects of formal contracts continued as an inheritance from the Romans in European legal systems until the late eighteenth century, when only then was the consensual nature of contracts established in modern laws. Fourthly - The Shari'ah required in the formation and execution of contracts good faith (La bonne Foi) and made any fraud, deceit, or misrepresentation by one of the contracting parties grounds for the other to have an option and a justification to rescind the contract due to the breach of consent. Fifthly - It considered custom and usage as a basis for determining the limits of contractual obligations and rights in everything that the text of the contract is silent about: for example, the method of using the leased item and how to pay the rent follow the people's custom. Custom has a broad judicial consideration in every place as long as it does not conflict with a specific legal text in its subject matter; for example, the customary practice of undertaking to pay a gambling debt has no effect.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy overview of the nature of contracts in Islamic law. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, highlights three key principles. First, Islamic law adopts the consensual contract theory: contracts are formed by the mere free consent of the parties without any formalities. This is distinguished from Roman law and early Arab customs, which required elaborate formalities. The author notes that even the marriage contract, which has specific requirements like witnesses, is still fundamentally consensual. Second, the Shari'ah mandates good faith in forming and executing contracts, invalidating fraud or misrepresentation and granting the deceived party the option to rescind. This aligns with the Hanafi emphasis on the integrity of consent. Third, custom plays a crucial role in interpreting and supplementing contractual terms in areas where the contract is silent. However, custom cannot override a specific textual ruling, as exemplified by gambling debts, which are invalid despite any customary agreement. This passage does not provide a detailed fiqh ruling but rather sets forth the philosophical underpinnings recognized by all schools.

Key Terms
عقد رضائي (ʿaqd riḍāʾī): A contract concluded by mere mutual consent without formalities.
حسن النية (ḥusn al-niyya): Good faith required in contract formation and execution.
عرف (ʿurf): Custom or usage that determines contractual obligations in silent areas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A written document signed by the couple
B) Verbal offer and acceptance with two witnesses
C) Formal registration by a religious authority
D) The exchange of a dowry

Key Takeaway
Islamic law prioritizes genuine consent in contracts, requires good faith, and uses custom to fill gaps, forming a flexible and fair contractual framework.

Bases of Punishment: Fixed and Discretionary
Outlining the two foundations of penal system

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fixed punishments (hudud/qiṣāṣ) and discretionary punishments (taʿzīr), and explain the application of taʿzīr when qiṣāṣ is forgiven.

Core Idea
Islamic punishment is mostly discretionary (taʿzīr); fixed punishments apply only to a few crimes.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's legal philosophy outlines the foundational structure of Islamic criminal law. It establishes that every prohibited act is a crime requiring just punishment, but the Shari'ah only prescribes fixed penalties for a very limited set of offenses. The vast majority of crimes are left to the discretion of the state and judiciary, allowing for adaptation to circumstances. The passage also highlights the unique case of intentional murder, where the victim's heirs may forgive the death penalty (qiṣāṣ), but the state still imposes a discretionary taʿzīr punishment to maintain public order.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two foundations
Read the passage and note that the Shari'ah builds the penal system on two bases: every prohibited act is a crime punishable justly, and most crimes do not have predetermined penalties.
2
Distinguish between fixed and discretionary
Understand that only five crimes have fixed penalties (hudud); all others fall under taʿzīr. Fixed penalties are rigid; taʿzīr is flexible.
The five hudud are not listed here but are well-known in fiqh.
3
Analyze the case of qiṣāṣ
The Shari'ah prescribes qiṣāṣ (retaliation) for intentional murder. However, if the victim's heirs forgive, the punishment is not waived entirely but commuted to a taʿzīr penalty.
This preserves the public right in deterrence and discipline.
4
Appreciate the role of authority
The ruling authority and judges are entrusted with estimating taʿzīr punishments based on circumstances. This reflects the principle of justice and flexibility.
5
Synthesize the overall philosophy
Islamic criminal law balances retributive justice (fixed penalties) with rehabilitative and deterrent justice (discretionary taʿzīr).

C - In Criminal Rights (Crimes and Punishments)
18/1 - The Shari'ah established the system of punishments on two foundations:
1 - Every prohibited act is considered a crime, and every crime is subject to punishment in a just manner proportionate to the act and sufficient to achieve internal security and discipline the perpetrator, even if the Shari'ah has not predetermined a specific punishment for that act.
2 - The Shari'ah left the punishments for most crimes - except five of them - unspecified, because the punishments in them must vary according to circumstances. Thus, the ruling authority has the power to codify punishments on this basis, and the judge, in the absence of codification, may estimate the punishment according to temporal, spatial, and personal circumstances. These punishments that the Shari'ah did not specify, but left delegated to the rulers, are called taʿzīr. And the Shari'ah singled out aggression against life with the punishment of equivalence called qiṣāṣ, so it required killing the intentional murderer if the heirs of the victim insist on demanding qiṣāṣ. If they forgive, the qiṣāṣ is commuted to a taʿzīr punishment whose estimation is delegated to the rulers, to preserve the public right in discipline and deterrence.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage explains the structure of Islamic criminal law. The Shari'ah establishes that all prohibited acts are crimes that must be punished, but it does not fix penalties for most of them; instead, it leaves them to the discretion of the state and judges, known as taʿzīr. Only a few crimes (the five hudud, implied) have fixed punishments. This flexibility allows for adaptation to changing circumstances. The text also discusses qiṣāṣ (retaliation) for murder, where the heirs may choose to demand execution or forgive, in which case a taʿzīr punishment is applied to uphold public order. This reflects the Hanafi view that taʿzīr serves the purpose of discipline and deterrence even after forgiveness.

Key Terms
تعزير (taʿzīr): Discretionary punishment left to the judge or ruler for crimes without fixed penalties.
قصاص (qiṣāṣ): Retaliation in kind, specifically the death penalty for intentional murder.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The killer goes unpunished completely
B) The punishment becomes a discretionary taʿzīr penalty
C) The state imposes a fixed fine
D) The killer is still executed

Key Takeaway
Islamic criminal law is built on two principles: all prohibited acts are punishable, but most punishments are discretionary (taʿzīr) to allow flexibility; even qiṣāṣ converts to taʿzīr upon forgiveness to maintain public order.

The Constitutional Principles of Freedom and Equality in Islamic Law
Explaining constitutional principles of freedom and equality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two constitutional principles of freedom and equality as established in the Shari'ah, and identify their textual proofs from the Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
Complete freedom and equality before the law are divinely mandated, limited only by public order and piety.

This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Am examines the constitutional foundations of Islamic public law. The author presents two fundamental principles: complete freedom for individuals, and legal equality regardless of lineage or class. These principles are not merely philosophical; they are rooted in explicit textual evidence from the Quran and the Prophet's Sunnah. Understanding these principles is essential for any mufti addressing modern issues of civil rights and governance.

Guided Path
1
State the first principle
Identify the principle of complete freedom: individual liberty is guaranteed as long as it does not violate public order, morals, or the rights of others.
The Arabic uses 'ḥurriyya tāmma' (complete freedom).
2
State the second principle
Identify the principle of equality: all individuals are equal before the law with no privileges for lineage, race, or class.
The author contrasts this with pre-Islamic Arab, Roman, and Persian practices.
3
Analyse the textual evidence
Read the Quranic verse (49:13) and the hadith. Explain how each supports the principle of equality and the basis of superiority (taqwā).
The hadith explicitly negates ethnic and racial superiority.
4
Examine the historical example
Describe the incident with Caliph 'Umar punishing the son of the governor. How does this illustrate the practical application of equality before the law?
This shows that even the son of a conqueror is not above the law.

Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage discusses the constitutional principles of Islamic law, specifically in the area of public rights. The author lists two (of three) basic principles: complete freedom (subject to public order and the rights of others) and equality before the law without any privilege based on lineage or class. This directly contrasts with the pre-Islamic Arab, Roman, and Persian systems. The Quranic verse (49:13) and the well-known hadith about piety being the only criterion for superiority are cited as evidence. The author also gives an example from the time of Umar ibn al-Khattab, who punished the son of Amr ibn al-As (the governor of Egypt) for a transgression, showcasing that no one is above the law. This reflects the Hanafi commitment to the application of justice equally, as the school gives weight to public interest and the rule of law.

Key Terms
حرية (ḥurriyya): Complete freedom for individuals, limited only by public order, morals, and others' rights.
مساواة (musāwāt): Equality before the law without discrimination based on lineage, race, or class.
تقوى (taqwā): Piety and God-consciousness, the only basis for superiority in Islam.

Ta'zir and Qisas: Fixed and Discretionary Penalties
Explaining fixed and discretionary penalties in Sharia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qisas (fixed retaliation) and ta'zir (discretionary punishment) in Hanafi law, and explain why forgiveness of qisas converts the case to ta'zir.

Core Idea
Qisas is a fixed right of the victim's heirs; if forgiven, the judge imposes ta'zir to uphold public order.

This passage opens by explaining how Islamic Sharia balances fixed penalties with judicial discretion. While qisas is a prescribed penalty for intentional murder, it becomes a ta'zir matter if the heirs forgive. This demonstrates the Sharia's concern for both the victim's family and the public interest in deterrence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of penalties
Recognize qisas as a fixed penalty for murder and ta'zir as a discretionary penalty delegated to the judge.
Qisas is a hadd-like penalty, but it is a right of the heirs.
2
Locate the condition for qisas
The text states qisas is obligatory only if the victim's heirs demand it. Otherwise, it is converted.
3
Trace the transition from qisas to ta'zir
When heirs forgive, the penalty becomes ta'zir, estimated by the judge according to circumstances.
This reflects the Hanafi view that the state retains the right to punish for public order.
4
Understand the rationale
The preservation of public order (hifz al-nizam) justifies ta'zir even after private forgiveness.
The 'public right' (al-haqq al-'amm) in deterrence is maintained.

Chapter (1) General Overview of Islamic Sharia. The governing codification of penalties on this basis, and for the judge when there is no codification, he may estimate the penalty according to temporal, spatial, and personal circumstances. These penalties that the Sharia did not specify, but left delegated to rulers, are called: Ta'zir. And the Sharia specifically designated aggression against life with the penalty of equivalence called Qisas, so it obligated killing the intentional murderer if the heirs of the victim insist on demanding Qisas. If they forgive, Qisas is converted into a Ta'zir penalty whose estimation is delegated to the rulers, preserving the public right of discipline and deterrence. Second Section: Division of Public Rights. A - In Internal Rights. 1 - In the Constitutional Aspect: 19/1 - The Sharia established here three basic principles: (First Principle) - Complete freedom for people without violating public order and public morals, and without transgressing the limits of others' freedom. (Second Principle) - Equality before the law in all rights, no privilege for lineage or class of people, contrary to the condition of the Arabs, Romans, and Persians. It came in the Noble Quran: 'Indeed, the most noble of you in the sight of Allah is the most righteous of you' (49:13), meaning the best in deed and compliance with the orders of Sharia. And it came in the Prophetic hadith: 'No superiority for an Arab over a non-Arab, nor for a red over a black, except through piety.' (Reported by Ahmad...). Among the applications of these two principles in early Islamic history is the incident of the second Caliph Umar ibn al-Khattab, who severely punished the son of the commander and conqueror of Egypt because he transgressed against the freedom of an Egyptian Christian (Copt) in a horse race when the Copt surpassed him, so the commander's son struck him saying, 'You should not surpass the son of the most honorable.' A famous incident. The Caliph Umar said to the commander and his son after the punishment his famous words: 'Since when have you enslaved people when their mothers bore them free?' Here comes the issue of slavery. Islam improved the condition of slaves, organized legal rights for them guaranteed by the judiciary, advised treating them well, considered them (according to Islamic creed) as 'brothers' to their masters, considered manumission of slaves as one of the greatest acts of worship in reward with Allah, and obligated this manumission obligatorily in many situations and incidents. It abolished all familiar sources of enslavement at that time among Arabs, Romans, and others except only legitimate war. But it did not completely abolish slavery at that time because the nations surrounding Muslims all adhered to it and enslaved those who fell into their hands from the Muslims. So Islam kept slavery temporarily as a result of legitimate war on the method of reciprocity, a principle established to this day in international law, but the Sharia paved the way for absolute abolition by making it delegated to the Imam (the supreme ruling authority) when nations become ready to accept its abolition, so there remains no need for reciprocity. Note that all other laws, religious or secular, not one of them came with a call to abolish slavery or to improve the condition of slaves, nor did they pave for its abolition as Islam established. Rather, slavery continued in all non-Islamic world in its cruelty until the nineteenth century CE. Third Principle - Governance in the Islamic state must be based on consultation (Shura), a principle contained in the Quran and applied by the Prophet throughout his life and his companions. Hence, governance cannot be despotic. But Islam did not specify the method of implementing Shura because that varies according to temporal and spatial possibilities and whatever new beneficial methods arise. Thus, the method of governance can be established according to considerations of public interest either on a constitutional monarchy system, a republican system, or a system...
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a modern legal-philosophical introduction by the Hanafi jurist Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explaining the broad principles of Islamic Sharia as a legal system. The author first distinguishes between fixed penalties (qisas) and discretionary punishments (ta'zir), emphasizing the judge's flexibility when laws are not codified. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on judicial discretion (ijtihad) within the boundaries of Sharia. He then outlines three constitutional principles: freedom, equality, and consultation (shura). The principle of equality is supported by Quranic verses and hadith, notably the famous hadith 'No superiority of Arab over non-Arab except by piety,' which al-Zarqa interprets as a legal equality regardless of race or lineage. The story of Caliph Umar illustrates the practical application of this principle, as he punished a governor's son for striking a Copt. This incident is frequently cited in Hanafi works on siyar (international law) to demonstrate the state's accountability. Regarding slavery, al-Zarqa presents the Hanafi historical view: Islam did not abolish slavery outright due to prevailing international circumstances, but it restricted sources of enslavement to legitimate war, improved slaves' rights, and encouraged manumission as an act of worship. The ultimate abolition is left to the ruler's discretion when no longer needed for reciprocity. This aligns with the Hanafi principle of istihsan (juristic preference) in considering changing circumstances. The third principle of shura is presented as a flexible constitutional framework, allowing various forms of government (constitutional monarchy, republic) as long as they embody consultation. This reflects al-Zarqa's modernist Hanafi approach, advocating for compatibility between Islamic principles and modern governance.

Key Terms
التعزير (at-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment whose type and measure are left to the judge's estimation when the Sharia has not specified a fixed penalty.
القصاص (al-qisas): The penalty of equivalence for homicide or bodily harm, specifically requiring the death of the intentional murderer if the victim's heirs demand it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to the penalty of qisas if the victim's heirs forgive the murderer?
A) The murderer is automatically released.
B) The qisas penalty is replaced by a discretionary ta'zir penalty determined by the judge.
C) The murderer must still pay diyah (blood money) without exception.
D) The state imposes a fixed hadd penalty instead.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, forgiveness of qisas does not eliminate all punishment; the judge retains discretion to impose ta'zir for public deterrence.

Constitutional Principles: Freedom, Equality, and Shura in Islamic Sharia
Explaining constitutional principles and the nature of slavery in Islamic law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three constitutional principles (freedom, equality, shura) and evaluate the Hanafi approach to slavery as a temporary institution subject to abolition by the ruler.

Core Idea
Islamic Sharia guarantees freedom and equality, and slavery is only a temporary exception due to international circumstances, to be abolished when possible.

This passage outlines the fundamental constitutional principles of an Islamic state: freedom within limits, equality without racial privilege, and governance by consultation. It then tackles the sensitive issue of slavery, presenting Islam's gradual reform as a model for legal change in accordance with changing circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three constitutional principles
Extract freedom (without violating public order), equality (no privilege for lineage), and shura (consultation-based governance).
These are derived from the Quran and Sunnah.
2
Analyze the evidence for equality
The Quranic verse 49:13 and the hadith 'No superiority of Arab over non-Arab except by piety' establish legal equality.
The hadith is narrated by Ahmad and others.
3
Examine the story of Umar
Caliph Umar punished the son of a governor for striking a Copt, embodying equality before the law.
This is a famous example of public accountability.
4
Understand the slavery discussion
Islam did not abolish slavery outright due to reciprocity in war; it improved conditions and made abolition possible when circumstances permit.
The ruler (Imam) has authority to abolish slavery.
5

Governance must be consultative; the method is flexible and can adapt to modern systems like constitutional monarchy or republic.
Shura is an obligation but its implementation is not fixed.

الفصل (۱) نظرة عامة إلى الشريعة الإسلامية
الحاكمة تقنين العقوبات على هذا الأساس وللقاضي عند عدم التقنين أن يقدر العقوبة بحسب الظروف الزمانية والمكانية والشخصية. وهذه العقوبات التي لم تحددها الشريعة، بل تركتها مفوَّضة للحكام تسمى : التعزير . وقد خصَّت الشريعة العدوان على النفس بعقوبة المماثلة المسماة بالقصاص، فأوجبت قتل القاتل المتعمد إذا أصر خلفاء المقتول على طلب القصاص. فإذا عَفَوْا تبدل القصاص إلى عقوبة تعزير يفوّض تقديرها إلى الحكام، حفظاً للحق العام في التأديب والزجر.
المطلب الثاني
قسم الحقوق العامة

١ - في الناحية الدستورية :
۱۹/۱ - أقرَّت الشريعة هنا ثلاثة مبادىء أساسية :
(المبدأ الأول) - الحرية التامة للناس دون إخلال بالنظام العام والآداب العامة، ودون تجاوز على حدود حرية الغير.
(المبدأ الثاني) - المساواة أمام القانون في جميع الحقوق، فلا امتياز لنسب أو لطبقة من الناس، خلافاً لما كانت عليه حال العرب والرومان والفرس. وقد جاء في القرآن الكريم إِنَّ أَكْرَمَكُمْ عِندَ الله أنقنكُمْ ﴾ [١٤٩ ۱۳] أي أحسنكم عملاً وامتثالاً لأوامر الشريعة وجاء في الحديث النبوي: لا فضل لعربي على أعجمي ولا لأحمر على أسود إلا بالتقوى»(۱). رواه أحمد ٤١١/٥ عن أبي نضرة ممن سمع خطبة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أيام التشريق، ورواه الطبراني والبزار عن أبي سعيد، ورجال البزار رجال الصحيح كما في المجمع » ٨٤/٨. ومن تطبيقات هذين المبدأين في التاريخ الإسلامي الأول حادثة الخليفة الثاني عمر بن الخطاب الذي عاقب ابن أمير مصر وفاتحها عقوبة شديدة لأنه تجاوز على حرية نصراني مصري (قبطي) في سباق الخيل لما سبقه القبطي فضربه ابن الأمير قائلاً ما كان لك أن تسبق ابن الأكرمين. حادثة معروفة . وقد قال الخليفة عمر للأمير وابنه بعد العقوبة كلمته المشهورة منذ كم استعبدتم الناس وقد ولدتهم أمهاتهم أحراراً؟!». وهنا تأتي قضية الرق. فالإسلام أتى بتحسين حال الأرقاء ونظم لهم حقوقاً قانونية مضمونة بالقضاء، وأوصى بهم واعتبرهم بمقتضى العقيدة الإسلامية (إخواناً) لمواليهم واعتبر إعتاق الرقيق عبادة من أعظم العبادات ثواباً عند الله، وأوجب هذا الإعتاق إيجاباً في كثير من الأحوال والحوادث. وألغى جميع منابع الاسترقاق المألوفة إذ ذاك عند العرب والرومان وغيرهم سوى الحرب المشروعة فقط. ولكنه لم يلغ الاسترقاق بتاتاً إذ ذاك لأنَّ الأمم المحيطة بالمسلمين كلها متمسكة به وتسترق من يقع في أيديها من المسلمين. فأبقى الإسلام على الاسترقاق موقتاً بنتيجة الحرب المشروعة على طريقة المقابلة بالمثل، وهو مبدأ مقرر إلى اليوم في الحقوق الدولية ولكن الشريعة مهدت للإلغاء المطلق بجعله مفوّضاً إلى الإمام السلطة الحاكمة العليا عندما تستعد الأمم لقبول إلغائه فلا تبقى حاجة للمقابلة بالمثل.
مع هذا ملاحظة أن جميع الشرائع الأخرى من دينية أو وضعية لم تأت منها واحدة بالدعوة إلى إلغاء الرق ولا إلى تحسين وضع الأرقاء، بل ولا مهدت لإلغائه ذلك التمهيد الذي أسسه الإسلام، بل استمر الرق في جميع العالم غير الإسلامي على قَسْوَته حتى القرن التاسع عشر الميلادي .
المبدأ الثالث - الحكم في دولة الإسلام يجب أن يقوم على أساس الشورى، وهذا مبدأ تضمنه القرآن وطبقه الرسول طوال حياته أصحابه ، فلا يجوز أن يكون الحكم استبدادياً. ولكن الإسلام لم يحدد طريقة تحقيق الشورى لأن ذلك يختلف بحسب الإمكانيات الزمانية والمكانية وما يحدث من أساليب جديدة مفيدة. فيمكن تأسيس طريقة الحكم بحسب دواعي المصلحة إما على نظام الملكية الدستورية أو النظام الجمهوري أو النظام
Chapter (1) General Overview of Islamic Sharia. The governing codification of penalties on this basis, and for the judge when there is no codification, he may estimate the penalty according to temporal, spatial, and personal circumstances. These penalties that the Sharia did not specify, but left delegated to rulers, are called: Ta'zir. And the Sharia specifically designated aggression against life with the penalty of equivalence called Qisas, so it obligated killing the intentional murderer if the heirs of the victim insist on demanding Qisas. If they forgive, Qisas is converted into a Ta'zir penalty whose estimation is delegated to the rulers, preserving the public right of discipline and deterrence. Second Section: Division of Public Rights. A - In Internal Rights. 1 - In the Constitutional Aspect: 19/1 - The Sharia established here three basic principles: (First Principle) - Complete freedom for people without violating public order and public morals, and without transgressing the limits of others' freedom. (Second Principle) - Equality before the law in all rights, no privilege for lineage or class of people, contrary to the condition of the Arabs, Romans, and Persians. It came in the Noble Quran: 'Indeed, the most noble of you in the sight of Allah is the most righteous of you' (49:13), meaning the best in deed and compliance with the orders of Sharia. And it came in the Prophetic hadith: 'No superiority for an Arab over a non-Arab, nor for a red over a black, except through piety.' (Reported by Ahmad...). Among the applications of these two principles in early Islamic history is the incident of the second Caliph Umar ibn al-Khattab, who severely punished the son of the commander and conqueror of Egypt because he transgressed against the freedom of an Egyptian Christian (Copt) in a horse race when the Copt surpassed him, so the commander's son struck him saying, 'You should not surpass the son of the most honorable.' A famous incident. The Caliph Umar said to the commander and his son after the punishment his famous words: 'Since when have you enslaved people when their mothers bore them free?' Here comes the issue of slavery. Islam improved the condition of slaves, organized legal rights for them guaranteed by the judiciary, advised treating them well, considered them (according to Islamic creed) as 'brothers' to their masters, considered manumission of slaves as one of the greatest acts of worship in reward with Allah, and obligated this manumission obligatorily in many situations and incidents. It abolished all familiar sources of enslavement at that time among Arabs, Romans, and others except only legitimate war. But it did not completely abolish slavery at that time because the nations surrounding Muslims all adhered to it and enslaved those who fell into their hands from the Muslims. So Islam kept slavery temporarily as a result of legitimate war on the method of reciprocity, a principle established to this day in international law, but the Sharia paved the way for absolute abolition by making it delegated to the Imam (the supreme ruling authority) when nations become ready to accept its abolition, so there remains no need for reciprocity. Note that all other laws, religious or secular, not one of them came with a call to abolish slavery or to improve the condition of slaves, nor did they pave for its abolition as Islam established. Rather, slavery continued in all non-Islamic world in its cruelty until the nineteenth century CE. Third Principle - Governance in the Islamic state must be based on consultation (Shura), a principle contained in the Quran and applied by the Prophet throughout his life and his companions. Hence, governance cannot be despotic. But Islam did not specify the method of implementing Shura because that varies according to temporal and spatial possibilities and whatever new beneficial methods arise. Thus, the method of governance can be established according to considerations of public interest either on a constitutional monarchy system, a republican system, or a system...
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a modern legal-philosophical introduction by the Hanafi jurist Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explaining the broad principles of Islamic Sharia as a legal system. The author first distinguishes between fixed penalties (qisas) and discretionary punishments (ta'zir), emphasizing the judge's flexibility when laws are not codified. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on judicial discretion (ijtihad) within the boundaries of Sharia. He then outlines three constitutional principles: freedom, equality, and consultation (shura). The principle of equality is supported by Quranic verses and hadith, notably the famous hadith 'No superiority of Arab over non-Arab except by piety,' which al-Zarqa interprets as a legal equality regardless of race or lineage. The story of Caliph Umar illustrates the practical application of this principle, as he punished a governor's son for striking a Copt. This incident is frequently cited in Hanafi works on siyar (international law) to demonstrate the state's accountability. Regarding slavery, al-Zarqa presents the Hanafi historical view: Islam did not abolish slavery outright due to prevailing international circumstances, but it restricted sources of enslavement to legitimate war, improved slaves' rights, and encouraged manumission as an act of worship. The ultimate abolition is left to the ruler's discretion when no longer needed for reciprocity. This aligns with the Hanafi principle of istihsan (juristic preference) in considering changing circumstances. The third principle of shura is presented as a flexible constitutional framework, allowing various forms of government (constitutional monarchy, republic) as long as they embody consultation. This reflects al-Zarqa's modernist Hanafi approach, advocating for compatibility between Islamic principles and modern governance.

Key Terms
الشورى (ash-shura): Consultation as a constitutional principle for governance, requiring rulers to seek counsel from qualified individuals.
الرق (ar-riqq): Slavery as a legal status, which Islam did not abolish outright but regulated, limited to prisoners of war, and encouraged manumission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who has the authority to completely abolish slavery in an Islamic system?
A) The judge (qadi) in each case.
B) The victim's heirs or the private individuals.
C) The ruler (Imam) when international conditions permit.
D) The consensus of all scholars.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats slavery as a temporary institution allowed only due to reciprocity in war; the ruler can abolish it when conditions allow.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Three General Principles of Islamic Law: Slavery, Shura, and Executive Authority
Explaining three general principles of Islamic public law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi stance on slavery as a temporary institution, state the principle of shura and its flexible implementation, and identify the scope of the Imam's regulatory authority within Shariah.

Core Idea
Islamic law regulates slavery to phase it out, mandates consultation in governance, and empowers the ruler to issue regulations within Shariah.

This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm presents three foundational principles that shape Islamic public law. Understanding these principles is essential for a mufti navigating issues of governance, human rights, and public policy. We will examine each principle's basis, its development, and how it informs fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three principles
From the passage, list the three general principles: slavery regulation, shura-based governance, and executive authority of the Imam. Note the Shariah basis for each.
These are not isolated but interrelated components of the Islamic legal system.
2
Analyze the slavery principle: temporary retention with abolition mechanism
Understand that Islam improved the slaves' condition, limited enslavement to legitimate warfare, and delegated complete abolition to the Imam when reciprocity is no longer needed. Note the principle of reciprocity.
This shows flexibility within fixed texts, a key maqasid approach.
3
Analyze the shura principle: fixed requirement, flexible method
Recognize that consultation (shura) is an obligatory Quranic principle, but its implementation method is left open to circumstances. Any system that ensures the will of the nation and prevents despotism is valid.
Hereditary rule is explicitly rejected as corrupting.
4
Analyze the executive authority principle: broad powers constrained by Shariah
The Imam (head of state) has comprehensive executive powers, including issuing temporal regulations. However, these must be applications of Shariah texts and maqasid, not arbitrary legislation.
This principle aligns with the Hanafi concept of siyasa shar'iyya.

الباب الأول: تمهيد وتعريف وتقسيم
شديدة لأنه تجاوز على حرية نصراني مصري (قبطي) في سباق الخيل لما سبقه القبطي فضربه ابن الأمير :قائلاً ما كان لك أن تسبق ابن الأكرمين. حادثة معروفة . وقد قال الخليفة عمر للأمير وابنه بعد العقوبة كلمته وهي المشهورة منذ كم استعبدتم الناس وقد ولدتهم أمهاتهم أحراراً؟!». وهنا تأتي قضية الرق. فالإسلام أتى بتحسين حال الأرقاء ونظم لهم حقوقاً قانونية مضمونة بالقضاء، وأوصى بهم واعتبرهم بمقتضى العقيدة الإسلامية (إخواناً) لمواليهم واعتبر إعتاق الرقيق عبادة من أعظم العبادات ثواباً عند الله، وأوجب هذا الإعتاق إيجاباً في كثير من الأحوال والحوادث. وألغى جميع منابع الاسترقاق المألوفة إذ ذاك عند العرب والرومان وغيرهم سوى الحرب المشروعة فقط. ولكنه لم يلغ الاسترقاق بتاتاً إذ ذاك لأنَّ الأمم المحيطة بالمسلمين كلها متمسكة به وتسترق من يقع في أيديها من المسلمين. فأبقى الإسلام على الاسترقاق موقتاً بنتيجة الحرب المشروعة على طريقة المقابلة بالمثل، وهو مبدأ مقرر إلى اليوم في الحقوق الدولية ولكن الشريعة مهدت للإلغاء المطلق بجعله مفوّضاً إلى الإمام السلطة الحاكمة العليا عندما تستعد الأمم لقبول إلغائه فلا تبقى حاجة للمقابلة بالمثل.
مع هذا ملاحظة أن جميع الشرائع الأخرى من دينية أو وضعية لم تأت منها واحدة بالدعوة إلى إلغاء الرق ولا إلى تحسين وضع الأرقاء، بل ولا مهدت لإلغائه ذلك التمهيد الذي أسسه الإسلام، بل استمر الرق في جميع العالم غير الإسلامي على قَسْوَته حتى القرن التاسع عشر الميلادي .
المبدأ الثالث - الحكم في دولة الإسلام يجب أن يقوم على أساس الشورى، وهذا مبدأ تضمنه القرآن وطبقه الرسول طوال حياته أصحابه ، فلا يجوز أن يكون الحكم استبدادياً. ولكن الإسلام لم يحدد طريقة تحقيق الشورى لأن ذلك يختلف بحسب الإمكانيات الزمانية والمكانية وما يحدث من أساليب جديدة مفيدة. فيمكن تأسيس طريقة الحكم بحسب دواعي المصلحة إما على نظام الملكية الدستورية أو النظام الجمهوري أو النظام البرلماني أو الرئاسي، أو غير ذلك من الأساليب ضمن حدود المبدأ العام، وهو إرادة الأمة والشورى. ومن يُنتَخَب لممارسة السلطة العليا يُعتبر ممثلاً للدولة الإسلامية في كل شيء باعتبار أن الدولة بمجموعها شخص اعتباري Personnalité morale وتصرفه نافذ عليها في حدود الشريعة.
والشريعة تتنافى تماماً مبدأ وراثة الحكم، بل على الأمة أن تختار دائماً الأكثر كفاءة للسلطة العليا. وقد بيّن الرسول لا لا لأصحابه أن فساد نظام الحكم في الدولة الإسلامية يكون عندما يتحوّل عن طريقة الخلافة والشورى فيصبح ملكاً عَضُوضاً» (١) ، أي يعض على الأمة عضا ويُفرض عليها فرضاً. ومثل ذلك كل نظام حكم استبدادي يفرض على الأمة بقوة السلطة والنفوذ تحت أي اسم كان ولو سمي جمهورياً.
جميع السلطات
۲ - في الناحية الإدارية : ۲٠/١ - قرّرت الشريعة لممثل السلطة العليا (الإمام) في الدولة صلاحيات إدارية تنفيذية غير محدودة. ففي يده تتمركز التنفيذية، ومن جملتها سلطة إصدار الأوامر الزمنية (الأنظمة) التي لها اليوم في عرف الدول صفة تشريعية. ولكنَّ الشريعة تمنح الإمام هذه السلطة التنظيمية باعتبار أنها في الحقيقة تطبيق للنصوص الشرعية العامة المقررة الأمة سابقاً في الشريعة، وتنفيذ لمقاصدها العامة في تنظيم مصالح مقتضيات الأحوال.
بحسب
(۱) رواه أحمد في مسنده ٢٧٣/٤ من حديث حذيفة رضي الله عنه : تكون النبوة فيكم ما شاء الله أن تكون، ثم يرفعها إذا شاء أن يرفعها ثم تكون خلافة على منهاج النبوة، فتكون ما شاء الله أن تكون ثم يرفعها إذا شاء الله أن يرفعها، ثم تكون ملكاً عاضاً فيكون ما شاء الله أن يكون ثم يرفعها إذا شاء أن يرفعها ثم تكون ملكاً جبرية فتكون ما شاء الله أن تكون ثم يرفعها إذا شاء أن يرفعها ثم تكون خلافة على منهاج النبوة . قال الهيثمي في مجمع الزوائد /۱۸۹/۵ : رواه أحمد في ترجمة النعمان - بن بشير -، والبزار أتم منه، والطبراني ببعضه في الأوسط، ورجاله ثقات.
Chapter One: Introduction, Definition, and Division
... severe because he transgressed against the freedom of an Egyptian Christian (Copt) in a horse race when the Copt overtook him, so the emir's son hit him saying: 'You should not have overtaken the son of the most noble.' A well-known incident. And Caliph Umar said to the emir and his son after the punishment his famous words: 'Since when have you enslaved people while their mothers bore them free?!' Then comes the issue of slavery. Islam improved the condition of slaves, organized legal rights guaranteed by the judiciary, commended kind treatment, considered them brothers to their masters according to Islamic creed, considered freeing slaves an act of worship with great reward from Allah, and made such freeing obligatory in many situations and events. It abolished all familiar sources of enslavement then existing among Arabs, Romans, and others except only legitimate warfare. But it did not abolish slavery outright at that time because the nations surrounding the Muslims all adhered to it and enslaved any Muslims falling into their hands. So Islam retained slavery temporarily as a result of legitimate warfare on the principle of reciprocity, a principle established to this day in international law, but the Shariah paved the way for absolute abolition by entrusting it to the Imam, the supreme ruling authority, when nations become ready to accept its abolition so there is no more need for reciprocity.
With this, note that all other religious or positive laws – not one of them called for the abolition of slavery or improvement of the slaves' condition, nor did they pave the way for its abolition as Islam did. Rather, slavery continued in the entire non-Muslim world in its cruelty until the nineteenth century.
The third principle – Governance in the Islamic state must be based on consultation (shura). This is a principle contained in the Quran and applied by the Messenger throughout his life and his Companions. Thus, government cannot be despotic. But Islam did not specify a method for realizing shura because that varies according to temporal and spatial possibilities and emerging useful new methods. So the method of governance can be established based on considerations of benefit, whether a constitutional monarchy, republican system, parliamentary or presidential system, or other methods within the limits of the general principle, which is the will of the nation and shura. Whoever is elected to exercise supreme authority is considered representative of the Islamic state in everything, considering that the state as a whole is a legal personality (Personnalité morale) and his actions are binding upon it within the limits of Shariah.
Shariah completely opposes the principle of hereditary rule; rather, the nation must always choose the most competent for supreme authority. And the Messenger [صلى الله عليه وسلم] explained to his Companions that corruption of the governance system in the Islamic state occurs when it deviates from the way of the caliphate and consultation and becomes 'oppressive kingship' (mulkan 'adhudan), i.e., it bites the nation and is imposed forcibly. And similar is every despotic system imposed on the nation by force of authority and influence under any name, even if called republican.
All powers
2 – In the administrative aspect: 20/1 – Shariah granted the representative of supreme authority (the Imam) in the state unlimited executive administrative powers. In his hand are concentrated all executive powers, including the authority to issue temporal orders (regulations) which today in the usage of states have a legislative character. But Shariah grants the Imam this regulatory authority on the basis that it is in reality an application of the general Shariah texts previously established by the nation in Shariah, and implementation of its general purposes in organizing interests according to the requirements of circumstances.
(1) Narrated by Ahmad in his Musnad 4/273 from Hadith of Hudhayfah (may Allah be pleased with him): 'Prophethood will remain among you as long as Allah wills, then He will remove it when He wills to remove it; then there will be a caliphate on the prophetic model, and it will remain as long as Allah wills, then He will remove it when He wills; then there will be oppressive kingship, and it will remain as long as Allah wills, then He will remove it when He wills; then there will be tyrannical kingship, and it will remain as long as Allah wills, then He will remove it when He wills; then there will be a caliphate on the prophetic model.' Al-Haythami said in Majma' al-Zawa'id 5/189: Ahmad narrated it in the biography of al-Nu'man bin Bashir, and al-Bazzar narrated a more complete version, and al-Tabarani narrated part of it in al-Awsat, and its narrators are trustworthy.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' presents a legal-philosophical discussion of three foundational principles of Islamic law. The author first addresses the issue of slavery, explaining that while Islam did not abolish it outright at first, it radically transformed the institution by mandating humane treatment, making emancipation a meritorious act, and limiting the sources of enslavement to legitimate warfare only. Crucially, al-Zarqa notes that Islamic law made absolute abolition a delegated power of the Imam (supreme ruler), to be exercised when international circumstances allow reciprocity to cease. This reflects a dynamic understanding of Shariah's goals (maqasid).

The second principle discussed is shura (consultation) as the basis of governance. Al-Zarqa clarifies that Islam requires consultation but does not prescribe a specific political system; rather, any system that realizes the will of the nation and prevents despotism is permissible. He explicitly rejects hereditary rule and warns against oppressive kingship. This portion cites a hadith about the eventual return to caliphate on the prophetic model.

Finally, the author discusses the administrative powers of the Imam, stating that he possesses unlimited executive authority, including the power to issue regulations that today would be considered legislative. However, this authority is not arbitrary; it is a means to apply the general texts and maqasid of Shariah to changing circumstances.

For the Hanafi mufti, these principles inform fatwa in areas of governance and public law, emphasizing flexibility within fixed principles and the importance of the ruler's role in enacting maqasid-oriented policies.

Key Terms
الرق (al-riqq): الاسترقاق الذي أباحه الإسلام مؤقتاً بنتيجة الحرب المشروعة فقط، مع تمهيد للإلغاء المطلق بتفويضه للإمام.
الشورى (al-shura): مبدأ حكم يقوم على التشاور بين الأمة ومنتخبيها، وهو أصل قرآني يمنع الاستبداد، مع ترك طريقة تحقيقه للظروف الزمانية والمكانية.
الإمام (al-imam): ممثل السلطة العليا في الدولة الإسلامية (الخليفة أو الرئيس)، وله صلاحيات تنفيذية واسعة تشمل إصدار الأنظمة الزمنية ضمن حدود الشريعة.

Key Takeaway
Islamic law combines fixed principles (shura, limitation of slavery, executive authority) with flexible implementation, empowering the ruler to adapt to changing circumstances while remaining within Shariah limits.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
The Principle of Public Interest (Maslaha) in Islamic Governance
Legal philosophy exposition on constitutional principles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim of maslaha to evaluate the permissibility of different forms of government and regulatory delegation.

Core Idea
Sharia's basis is public interest; governance forms may adapt as long as maqasid are served.

This lesson explores the foundational principle that the Sharia legal system revolves around the public interest (al-maslahah al-‘ammah). The passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa explains that the form of government is not fixed; rather, it must adhere to the general principles of consultation (shura) and the will of the nation. By understanding this qawida, you will see how classical Hanafi legal philosophy provides flexibility for modern constitutional and administrative arrangements, including the delegation of regulatory power to parliaments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core qawida
Locate the statement: 'الأصل في النظام القانوني من الشريعة هو رعاية المصلحة العامة، والتحول معها.' This is the key maxim governing the entire discussion.
The qawida is explicitly stated in the text.
2
Understand its scope and limits
The qawida operates in constitutional and administrative law. It allows for adaptation but is restricted by explicit Sharia texts and the principle 'no obedience to creation in sin.'
The hadith 'لا طاعة لمخلوق في معصية الله' serves as a limiting principle.
3
Apply the qawida to delegation of regulatory authority
The text states that the Imam's regulatory power is derived from the need to apply general Sharia texts; if public interest requires, this power can be delegated to an elected parliament.
This is a concrete furu‘ from the qawida.
4
Relate to the duties of ruler and ruled
The ruler is like a shepherd, responsible for the subjects' welfare, while subjects must obey but also monitor and correct rulers when they deviate.
Abu Bakr's statement exemplifies this balance.
5
Test the qawida with a modern scenario
Consider a Muslim-majority country adopting a presidential system. Evaluate its permissibility using the qawida: does it serve public interest? Is shura maintained? Are any explicit prohibitions violated?
This step prepares you for the practice tasks.

And whoever is elected to exercise supreme authority is considered a representative of the Islamic state in everything, considering that the state as a whole is a legal personality (Personnalité morale) and his action is binding on it within the limits of Sharia.
And Sharia completely contradicts the principle of hereditary rule; rather, the nation must always choose the most competent for supreme authority. And the Messenger (peace be upon him) explained to his companions that corruption of the system of government in the Islamic state occurs when it deviates from the method of caliphate and consultation and becomes 'oppressive kingship' (mulkan 'adudan), i.e., it bites the nation harshly and is imposed upon it forcibly. And similar to that is every tyrannical system of government imposed on the nation by force of authority and influence under any name, even if called republican.

And Sharia obligated everyone who assumes governance to base his conduct towards the subjects on observing what is most beneficial for them as a result of the obligatory principle of consultation. And the Messenger (peace be upon him) established in his authentic hadiths that the Imam (ruler) is like a shepherd, and he is responsible before Allah and the nation for the measures most beneficial for the subjects, and the subjects are similarly responsible for obedience. This is the foundation in all matters of administration and governance in imposing and collecting taxes, establishing various types of public services, compulsory acquisition for public benefit, and other things.

So the Messenger (peace be upon him) stated: 'No obedience to a creation in disobedience of Allah.' Based on this principle and the Sharia texts concerning it, the first Caliph Abu Bakr said in his first sermon: 'If I do well, support me; and if I do wrong, correct me.'

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy exposition on the principles of Islamic governance. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, presents a modern articulation of Sharia's constitutional framework. He begins by affirming that the form of government (parliamentary, presidential, etc.) is not fixed, as long as it operates under the principle of the nation's will and consultation (shura). The ruler is a representative of the state, which is a legal personality; his authority is binding but limited by Sharia. Hereditary rule is categorically rejected; the nation must elect the most competent. The author cites the famous hadith about the stages of Islamic governance, emphasizing that deviation from caliphate and consultation leads to oppressive monarchy. Over the next paragraphs, he discusses administrative powers: the Imam has broad executive authority, including issuing regulations, but this is merely implementation of general Sharia texts and maqasid (objectives). He then introduces the possibility of separation of powers and delegation to parliaments if it serves public interest (maslaha). The obligation of consultation is tied to the principle of the ruler as a shepherd responsible for the flock, and the subjects have a duty of obedience but also a duty to monitor and correct rulers, as per the hadith 'no obedience in sin' and Abu Bakr's inaugural speech. Finally, he moves to public finance, stating that Sharia transformed the prevalent norms. This passage is typical of modern legal philosophy works that rearticulate classical principles in contemporary language, but it remains faithful to Hanafi and broader Sunni constitutional theory.

Key Terms
الشورى (al-shura): The obligatory principle of consultation in governance.
المصلحة العامة (al-maslahah al-'ammah): Public interest, the overriding consideration in legal systems.
شخص اعتباري (shakhs i'tibari): Legal person (the state), capable of rights and obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The Imam derives authority from hereditary right.

Key Takeaway
The Sharia's foundational principle in governance is the observance of public interest, allowing for adaptation in forms of government and administration as long as maqasid are fulfilled.

Sharia Principles of Governance, Public Finance, and International Law
Exposition of legal philosophy on governance, finance, and international law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principles that the ruler is a trustee, the public treasury belongs to the nation, and international relations are based on justice and human dignity, and apply these principles to modern governance scenarios.

Core Idea
The ruler is a shepherd, not an owner; the treasury belongs to the nation; obedience is limited by divine law.

This lesson explores foundational principles of Islamic governance and international law as presented by Mustafa al-Zarqa. We will examine the concept of public interest (maslaha), the ruler's role as trustee, and the limits of obedience, drawing on authentic hadith and the practice of the early caliphs. Understanding these principles is essential for a mufti to issue fatwas aligned with the higher objectives of Sharia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational principle of governance
Read the passage to determine what principle forms the basis of the legal system. Notice the emphasis on 'al-maslahah al-'ammah' (public interest) and how it allows for institutions like parliaments.
Maslaha is a key concept in Hanafi usul, recognized as a source when not conflicting with text.
2
Analyze the hadith of the shepherd
Examine the hadith 'All of you are shepherds...' and understand that the ruler is accountable to God and the community for his actions. This establishes a relationship of trust and responsibility.
Also consider the hadith 'No obedience to a creature in sin' which limits the ruler's authority.
3
Understand public finance principles
Focus on the section about Bayt al-Mal (state treasury). The passage asserts that the treasury belongs to the nation, not the ruler. The ruler is a trustee with a fixed salary.
The story of Caliph Umar holding a governor accountable illustrates this principle in action.
4
Examine international legal principles
Review the passage on external rights. Note the affirmed principles: equality of peoples, justice in peace and war, and the protection of non-combatants and property.
The basis is Quran 17:70 on human dignity, which grounds all rules of war.
5
Apply the principles to a modern scenario
Consider how these principles would guide a mufti advising on a contemporary issue, such as a government's compulsory acquisition of land for a public project.
The principles of maslaha, trustee accountability, and limits on obedience are all relevant.

Chapter One: Introduction, Definition, and Division. This does not preclude the possibility of separation of powers and placing the right to issue temporary regulations in the hands of a special elected authority like parliaments today, if the nation sees its interest in that, for the principle in the legal system from Sharia is the observance of public interest and adaptation with it. Sharia has obligated every ruler to act towards the subjects based on what is most beneficial for them, resulting from the principle of obligatory consultation; and the Prophet established in his authentic hadiths (1) that the Imam (ruler) is like a shepherd, and he is responsible before God and the nation for the measures most beneficial for the subjects, and the subjects are also responsible for obedience. This is the foundation in all administrative and governmental affairs: imposing and collecting taxes, establishing public services of various types, compulsory acquisition for public benefit, and others. (2) Just as Sharia obligated obedience upon the subjects, it also awakened in them, alongside it, a sense of responsibility for the deviation of rulers and the necessity of monitoring them. Thus the Prophet (peace be upon him) decreed: 'No obedience to a creature in disobeying God.' Based on this principle and the religious texts about it, the first Caliph Abu Bakr said in his first sermon: 'If I do well, support me; if I do ill, correct me.' 3. In the area of public finance: 21/1 - Sharia came, contrary to what was prevalent at its emergence, with the principle that the Bayt al-Mal (state treasury) is superior to the property of the supreme ruler. The wealth of the public treasury belongs to the nation, is reserved for its interests, and the Imam is a trustee responsible for the method of its collection, expenditure on those public interests, and its administration. He has no right in it except his stipulated salary for his living in a moderate manner. The second Caliph ‘Umar called one of the governors to account when he saw signs of new wealth, and ordered him to confiscate the excess over what he had at the time of his appointment for which he could not prove a new legitimate source. When the governor said, 'I traded and profited,' ‘Umar replied, 'By God, we did not send you to trade.' B. In external rights (public international law): 22/1 - Sharia affirmed in this area the following principles: 1. All peoples are equal in human rights (contrary to the theory of the chosen people found in some religions and nations). 2. Relations between the Islamic state and others must be based on foundations of justice in peace and war. In peace, all acquired rights of states and their subjects are respected. In war, it is not permissible to exceed the limit that repels the evil of the enemy. Thus, it is not permissible to mutilate the dead (i.e., disfigure them), nor to torture prisoners, nor to kill gradually by cutting limbs, nor to destroy fruit-bearing trees except for military necessity, nor to kill animals except for food or military necessity, nor to harm monks (devotees) of religion in temples and hermitages, nor to harm those incapable of bearing arms: women, children, elderly old men, and the sick. The basis of all that is the saying of God Almighty: 'And We have certainly honored the children of Adam and carried them on the land and sea and provided them with good things and preferred them over much of what We have created with [great] preference.' [Quran 17:70]. So in the theory of Islam, man is honored for his humanity, and war is permitted to repel...
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of legal philosophy, outlining fundamental Sharia principles from an introductory chapter. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, discusses three key areas: governance, public finance, and international relations. The passage first establishes that Sharia permits separation of powers and representative bodies if they serve public interest (maslaha). The principle 'al-maslaha al-‘amma' (public interest) is the foundation of legal systems in Sharia. Then it addresses the ruler-subject relationship: the ruler is a shepherd responsible for the flock, and subjects have a duty of obedience but also a duty to monitor and advise (as exemplified by Abu Bakr's speech). The hadith 'no obedience to a creature in sin' limits obedience. In public finance, the Bayt al-Mal belongs to the nation, not the ruler; the ruler is a trustee with a limited salary. The story of ‘Umar and the governor illustrates accountability. In international law, Sharia establishes human dignity (based on Quran 17:70), equality of peoples, and just conduct in war and peace, prohibiting mutilation, torture, destruction, and harming non-combatants. This is a legal philosophy passage, not a specific ruling or maxim, so no analytical toolbox is provided.

Key Terms
المصلحة العامة (al-maslahah al-'ammah): Public interest; the general welfare of the community that informs legal rulings and policy.
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): The state treasury; public funds belonging to the Muslim community, managed by the ruler as a trustee.
الشورى (al-shura): Consultation; the obligatory principle of seeking counsel from qualified individuals in governance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The ruler's personal judgment
B) The principle of public interest (maslaha) and the hadith 'no obedience to a creature in sin'
C) The wealth of the ruler
D) International treaties

Key Takeaway
Islamic governance is founded on the principle that the ruler is a trustee over public affairs and wealth, accountable to God and the community, with obedience limited by divine law.

Principle of Public Treasury (Bayt al-Mal)
Defining the nature of public treasury as belonging to the nation, not the ruler.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Islamic legal principle regarding the public treasury and distinguish between the ruler's role as trustee versus owner.

Core Idea
Bayt al-Mal belongs to the nation, not the ruler; the ruler is a trustee, not an owner.

This lesson introduces a foundational principle of Islamic governance: the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal) is not the personal property of the ruler but a trust for the nation. The ruler is a trustee (amin) with strict fiduciary duties. The historical example of Caliph Umar holding a governor accountable illustrates this principle in action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Read the passage to determine who owns Bayt al-Mal. The text states it belongs to the nation, not the ruler.
2
Define the ruler's role
Explain that the Imam (ruler) is a trustee (amin) responsible for collection and expenditure for public interests, with no personal right except a fixed moderate salary.
3
Recognize limits on personal use
The ruler cannot use public funds for personal purposes beyond his salary. Any unexplained wealth is subject to confiscation.
4
Analyze the historical example
Study Caliph Umar's action against a governor who had new wealth. Umar confiscated the excess because the governor failed to prove a legitimate source, rejecting the claim that trade profits explained the wealth.
This shows strict accountability.
5
Apply to modern contexts
Consider how this principle applies to contemporary public officials: they are trustees of state funds, not owners, and must account for all public resources.

Chapter (1) General Overview of Islamic Shariah: On the principle of the superiority of Bayt al-Mal (public treasury) as the property of the state, not the possession of the supreme ruler. The wealth of the public treasury belongs to the nation, and is dedicated to it for its interests. Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical statement about the nature of public property in Islam. The author begins by establishing that Bayt al-Mal (the public treasury) is the property of the state, not the personal property of the ruler. The ruler (Imam) is only a trustee, responsible for collection and expenditure for public interests. His personal right is limited to a modest salary. The historical example of Caliph Umar illustrates this principle: he confiscated a governor's unexplained wealth even though the governor claimed it was from trade, because the governor's primary duty was governance, not commerce. This shows the strict accountability of public officials. The Hanafi school upholds this principle, and similar rules apply to judges and other trustees of public funds.

Key Terms
بيت المال (Bayt al-Maal): The public treasury of the Islamic state, belonging to the nation, managed by the ruler as a trustee.
الإمام (al-Imam): The supreme ruler/caliph, entrusted with managing public affairs and Bayt al-Mal.

Five Principles of Islamic International Law
Listing five foundational principles of Islamic international law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five foundational principles of Islamic international law as outlined by al-Zarqa, and identify the Quranic and historical evidence for each.

Core Idea
Islamic law establishes justice in war and peace, with necessity limiting harm and treaties binding.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's introduction to Islamic jurisprudence presents the core principles that govern relations between the Islamic state and other political entities. These principles, drawn from the Quran and early practice, form the basis of Islamic international law (siyar). Understanding them is essential for any mufti dealing with modern geopolitical issues within a Hanafi framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five principles
List the five numbered principles from the passage: equality of peoples, justice in peace/war (with specific war prohibitions), sanctity of treaties, necessity of prior warning before war, and reciprocity limited by Shariah.
The fifth principle on reciprocity is connected to the hostage ruling of al-Awza'i.
2
Locate the Quranic proof
The passage cites Quran 17:70 ('We have honored the children of Adam') as the ultimate basis for human dignity and protection in war. Understand how this verse grounds all the prohibitions against harming non-combatants.
This is a dalil naqli (textual evidence) that anchors the principles.
3
Understand the role of necessity (darurah)
The principle of necessity ('necessity is measured by its extent') allows things like destroying fruit trees or killing animals only when militarily necessary. This is a key legal maxim that restricts otherwise prohibited acts.
The qawida 'al-darurah tuqaddar bi qadariha' is central to Hanafi reasoning in siyar.
4
Analyze the hostage controversy
Imam al-Awza'i forbade killing hostages even after treaty violation, based on the Quranic principle 'no bearer of burdens bears another's burden.' Note that this view represents a legitimate ikhtilaf; some Hanafis allowed execution in retribution.
This shows that even within the broad principles, there is room for juristic difference.
5
Apply the principles to a modern scenario
Consider a modern war where an enemy uses civilian shields. Use the qawida of necessity to evaluate permissible military action, ensuring that harm does not exceed what is needed to repel aggression.
This connects the classical principles to contemporary fatwa work.

(انظر تاريخ البلاذري في بحث فتح بسمرقند). ه - المعاملة بالمثل جائزة إلا فيما يخالف المبادىء الإسلامية. وعلى هذا الأساس أفتى الإمام الأوزاعي أحد كبار فقهاء الشريعة في القرن الثاني للهجرة بعدم جواز قتل الرهائن المأخوذة من رجال الروم ضماناً لتنفيذ معاهدة بينهم وبين المسلمين عندما غَدَر الروم وخرقوا المعاهدة المعقودة. ومستند الأوزاعي في فتواه هذه هي الآية القرآنية التي تمنع مؤاخذة شخص بجريمة غيره ولا تزر وازرة وزر أخرى . وقد نشأ حول تلك الأسس في قسم الحقوق الخارجية من الشريعة الإسلامية فقه تفصيلي واسع مبسوط في أبوابه الخاصة من كتب الفقه
B. On External Rights (Public International Law). 1/22. The Shariah has affirmed the following principles in this area: 1- All peoples are equal in human rights (contrary to the theory of the chosen people found in some religions and nations). 2- Treatment between the Islamic state and others must be based on foundations of justice in peace and war. In peace, all acquired rights of states and their subjects are respected. In war, it is not permissible to exceed the limit that repels the evil of the enemy. Thus, it is not permissible to mutilate the dead, torture prisoners, kill gradually by cutting limbs, destroy fruit-bearing trees except for military necessity, kill animals except for food or military necessity, harm monks in monasteries and hermitages, or harm those unable to bear arms: women, children, elderly, and sick. The basis of all this is the saying of Allah Most High: 'And indeed We have honored the children of Adam...' [Al-Isra: 70]. So the human being in Islamic theory is respected for his humanity, and war is permissible to repel harm, not for harm, according to the rule of necessity, and necessity is measured by its extent. The first Caliph Abu Bakr's instructions in equipping the army of Usama contained great comprehensive matters on this subject. 3- Treaties are respected between Islamic and non-Islamic states and are binding like contracts between individuals, and must be fulfilled and executed in good faith. The Prophet and his caliphs conducted many treaties that were executed with utmost good faith on the Islamic side, except when the other side violated them. 4- Warfare is not permissible without prior warning. The incident of the Umayyad Caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz with the delegation from Samarqand is well-known: the delegation complained against the Muslim army commander, so the Caliph appointed a judge for them who ruled against the commander, and the result was the withdrawal of the Muslim army from Samarqand. (See al-Baladhuri's history on the conquest of Samarqand). 5- Reciprocity is permissible except when it contradicts Islamic principles. On this basis, Imam al-Awza'i, one of the great jurists of the second century AH, issued a fatwa that it is not permissible to kill hostages taken from the Romans as a guarantee for the implementation of a treaty between them and the Muslims when the Romans betrayed and violated the concluded treaty. Al-Awza'i's basis for this fatwa is the Quranic verse that forbids holding a person responsible for another's crime: 'No bearer of burdens shall bear the burden of another.' Extensive detailed jurisprudence on these foundations in the section on external rights of Islamic Shariah is elaborated in its own chapters in fiqh books.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents five foundational principles of Islamic international law. The author, a Hanafi jurist, emphasizes equality of all peoples, justice in war and peace, respect for treaties, necessity of prior warning before war, and limited reciprocity. Each principle is supported by Quranic verses and historical precedents. The Hanafi school incorporates these principles in the chapter of Siyar (international law). Key points: war must be defensive and limited by necessity; non-combatants are protected; treaties are binding; and retaliation (reciprocity) is allowed only within Shariah boundaries. The ruling of Imam al-Awza'i against killing hostages is noted, though some schools allowed it in retribution. The final sentence indicates that detailed fiqh of international law exists in classical works.

Key Terms
الضرورة (al-darurah): Necessity that permits normally prohibited actions, but only to the extent needed to remove harm.
الرهائن (al-rahain): Hostages taken from enemy as guarantee for treaty compliance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The Quranic principle that no one bears another's burden.
C) The necessity rule.
D) The treaty itself.

Key Takeaway
Islamic international law is founded on justice, necessity, and respect for treaties, with strict limits even in war.
