Foundational Principles of Islamic International Law (Siyar)
Listing foundational principles of Islamic international relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five principles of Islamic international law and apply the maxim 'necessity is measured by its extent' to a new case.

Core Idea
Islamic international law rests on treaties binding in good faith, necessity limited by its extent, and reciprocity within Islamic bounds.

This passage presents key axioms from the Hanafi tradition of siyar (Islamic international law). It begins with the maxim of necessity, then outlines principles governing treaties, warfare, and reciprocity. These axioms are not merely theoretical; they shaped historical decisions by caliphs and jurists like Abu Bakr, Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, and al-Awza'i. Understanding them is essential for a mufti addressing modern international relations.

Guided Path
1
Identify each principle
Read the passage and list the principles: (1) harm not for harm, necessity measured; (2) treaties binding in good faith; (3) warfare requires warning; (4) reciprocity allowed except against Islamic principles; (5) individual responsibility (from al-Awza'i's fatwa).
The fifth principle is implicit in the hostage example.
2
Link principles to examples
For each principle, note the historical example given: Abu Bakr's army (necessity), Prophet's treaties (treaties), Samarkand incident (warning), al-Awza'i's fatwa (reciprocity limited).
These examples show how the principles were applied.
3
Extract and analyze the maxim 'al-darura tuqaddar bi qadariha'
The maxim 'necessity is measured by its extent' appears at the start. It qualifies the first principle: you may not harm to remove harm beyond what is necessary.
This is a well-known Hanafi qa'ida.
4
Understand the exception to reciprocity
Reciprocity is allowed unless it contradicts Islamic principles. Al-Awza'i's fatwa shows that killing hostages is forbidden because it violates individual responsibility (Quran).
This exception is crucial for fatwa.
5
Reflect on authority and derivation
These principles come from Quran, Sunnah, and early practice. They are not mere opinions but have strong backing.
For a mufti, they constitute established usul.

معاهدة (mu'ahada): A treaty between Islamic and non-Islamic states, binding like contracts between individuals.

Key Takeaway
The five principles—harm limitation, necessity proportionality, treaty sanctity, warning before war, and bounded reciprocity—form the foundation of Islamic international law.

The Two Meanings of Fiqh in Islamic Law
Defining fiqh as knowledge and as the body of rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two meanings of fiqh (knowledge vs. body of rulings) and explain why the restriction 'practical' excludes creedal matters.

Core Idea
Fiqh is both the knowledge of divine rulings and the body of those rulings themselves.

Many students think of fiqh only as the set of rules (halal/haram). But classical scholars defined it first as a science—knowledge with detailed evidence. This lesson clarifies that dual meaning and the scope of 'practical' rulings, which deliberately excludes beliefs. Understanding this distinction is essential for usul al-fiqh and for a mufti's work.

Guided Path
1
Recognize two distinct meanings
Read the passage carefully: the author states that fiqh is used in two senses. Identify both in the Arabic text.
The first meaning relates to a person's knowledge; the second to the rulings as an objective body.
2
Analyze the first meaning
The first meaning defines fiqh as 'knowledge of practical shar'i rulings with their detailed evidence.' Note the key components: knowledge (not mere opinion), practical (not creedal), and detailed evidence (not general).
A faqih is someone who possesses this knowledge, not just anyone who memorizes rulings.
3
Analyze the second meaning
The second meaning treats fiqh as the rulings themselves: 'the collection of practical rulings legislated in Islam.' This is the sense used in 'I studied fiqh.'
This meaning focuses on the content, not the process of knowing.
4
Understand the scope: 'practical'
The term 'practical' (amaliyya) separates fiqh from creedal matters (i'tiqadiyya). Fiqh deals with actions (prayer, contracts) not beliefs (oneness of God, prophethood).
Creed is studied in 'ilm al-kalam, not fiqh.
5
Identify the sources of fiqh
The passage lists four sources: Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and ijtihad from the principles of Sharia. A mufti must derive rulings from these, not personal opinion.
This connects the definition of fiqh to the methodology of usul al-fiqh.

Source Text

Chapter Two: Meaning of Fiqh and Divisions of Its Rulings
1/2 - Meaning of Islamic Fiqh: Islamic fiqh has two meanings:
The first meaning of fiqh - divisions of its rulings
The second meaning of fiqh - Islamic fiqh as a spiritual and civil system - the idea of halal and haram in it - ruling of judiciary and ruling of religion
A - Fiqh in the first meaning is: knowledge of practical shar'i rulings with their detailed evidence, i.e., a person's detailed knowledge of them derived from their evidence, so fiqh becomes a scientific attribute of a person by which he is considered a faqih.
And by (rulings) is meant everything that the Lawgiver issues to people of commands and practical systems that organize their social life and their relations with each other, and determine the outcomes of their actions and transactions. Such as the usurper being liable for the usurped object if it perishes, and the trustee - like the depositary for example - not being liable unless he transgresses against the trust or is negligent in its preservation.
And by (shar'i) is meant that which is derived from the command of the Lawgiver explicitly or implicitly. And the restriction with the description (practical) is to exclude matters of belief from the principles of faith and their branches. For they are the subject of another science. B - And just as fiqh was first applied to knowledge of shar'i rulings, it was also subsequently applied to those rulings themselves. And this is what is meant in expressions like: I studied Islamic fiqh, and this is the second meaning of fiqh.
And on this basis, fiqh is defined as: the collection of practical rulings legislated in Islam.
And knowledge of their legality is through explicit text in the Quran, or the statement of the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم and his Sunnah, or through the consensus of Muslim scholars, or through the deduction of past and present mujtahid jurists from the evidences of Quranic texts and Prophetic Sunnah, and the principles and objectives of Sharia.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage introduces the foundational definition of fiqh in Islamic legal theory. The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, clarifies that fiqh has two distinct meanings. The first meaning is the science (knowledge) of practical shar'i rulings with their detailed evidence. This is the technical classical definition: a faqih is one who possesses this comprehensive, detailed knowledge derived from the sources. The second meaning is the body of rulings itself, i.e., the positive law of Islam comprising all practical legislated provisions. This is the meaning when one says 'I studied Islamic fiqh.' The passage also defines the scope: 'shar'i' rulings come from divine command, and 'practical' excludes creedal matters (aqida), which are the domain of theology (kalam). The author emphasizes that knowledge of the legality of these rulings comes from four primary sources: Quran, Sunnah, consensus (ijma), and ijtihad based on the evidence and principles of Sharia. This dual definition is essential for understanding later discussions in usul al-fiqh and the classification of rulings.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): First meaning: knowledge of practical shar'i rulings with their detailed evidence. Second meaning: the collection of practical rulings legislated in Islam.
الأحكام الشرعية العملية (al-ahkam al-shari'iyya al-'amaliyya): Practical shar'i rulings, as opposed to creedal matters. They regulate actions and transactions.
الشارع (al-shari'): The Lawgiver, i.e., Allah and His Messenger as the source of legislation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Matters of belief (i'tiqadiyya)
C) Matters of transactions
D) Matters of family law

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is both a science (knowledge with detailed evidence) and a body of rulings (the positive law of Islam), and it covers only practical actions, not creed.

The Five Categories of Fiqh Rulings
Enumerating the first five categories of fiqh rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five main categories of fiqh rulings and map them to their modern legal equivalents.

Core Idea
Fiqh rulings cover every aspect of life, from worship to constitutional law.

Fiqh is often misunderstood as merely a set of ritual and penal rules. In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa presents a systematic classification that reveals the full scope of Islamic law. By understanding these categories, you will see how fiqh integrates spiritual, familial, economic, political, and penal dimensions, and how classical categories map onto modern legal fields.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main classification principle
The author divides fiqh rulings into seven groups; this lesson focuses on the first five. Note that the classification is based on subject matter, not on the nature of the ruling (e.g., obligation vs. prohibition).
The remaining two categories (likely judicial procedure and international law) are not in this window.
2
Examine each category sequentially
For each of the five categories, observe the label (e.g., 'ibadat) and the brief description. The author gives examples for each: prayer and fasting for ibadat; marriage, divorce, inheritance for personal status; contracts and disputes for mu'amalat; ruler-subject relations for sultaniyya; punishments for 'uqubat.
Personal status is a modern term; classical fiqh usually subsumes these under nikah, talaq, etc.
3
Map classical categories to modern legal terminology
The author explicitly links personal status and mu'amalat to form 'civil law' in modern usage. Sultaniyya is split into administrative and constitutional law. Recognize that these mappings help modern jurists (especially in Muslim-majority countries) integrate fiqh into contemporary legal systems.
The mapping is not one-to-one; fiqh categories are broader in scope.
4
Recognize the comprehensive scope
Note that fiqh covers not only individual acts (ibadat) but also societal structures (mu'amalat, sultaniyya, 'uqubat). This refutes the narrow view that fiqh is only about rituals and criminal punishment.
The classification demonstrates Islam's holistic worldview (din wa dawla).
5
Anticipate the missing two categories
The author mentions seven groups but lists only five. Based on typical classification in Hanafi usul, the remaining two are often 'qada' (judiciary) and 'siyar' (international law). Consider how these fit into the overall scheme.
Al-Zarqa later introduces these in the full text.

2/2 - Divisions of Fiqh Rulings:
And these fiqh rulings are divided into seven groups:
1 - Rulings related to worship of Allah Almighty, such as prayer, fasting, and others; these are called: ibadat (worship).
2 - Rulings related to the family, such as marriage, divorce, lineage, maintenance, bequests, and inheritance, etc.; and in modern language they are called: personal status (ahwal shakhsiyya).
3 - Rulings related to people's acquisitive activity and their dealings with each other in matters of wealth and rights, and their transactions through contracts and otherwise, and settling their disputes through the judiciary; these are called: mu'amalat (transactions). And from these two groups (2) and (3) is composed what in modern legal terminology is called: civil law.
4 - Rulings related to the authority of the ruler over the subjects, and the mutual rights and duties between them; some jurists call these: sultaniyya rulings, and they are of the type called: siyasa shar'iyya (Sharia policy). And this group comprises two distinct types of rights in modern legal terminology: administrative rights and constitutional rights.
5 - Rulings related to punishment of criminals and maintaining internal order among people; these are called: 'uqubat (penalties).
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents a systematic classification of Islamic legal rulings (ahkam fiqhiyya) into seven broad categories. Although the text in this window only lists the first five, the author mentions seven groups, likely including two more such as judicial procedure (qada') and international law (siyar) or other categories that appear in subsequent pages. The first category is 'ibadat (worship), covering all devotional acts like prayer, fasting, zakat, hajj, etc. The second is personal status law (ahwal shakhsiyya), modern terminology for family law including marriage, divorce, inheritance, etc. The third is mu'amalat (transactions), which together with personal status forms civil law in modern terms. The fourth is sultaniyya rulings, which encompass administrative and constitutional law, i.e., the relationship between the ruler and the governed. The fifth is criminal law ('uqubat) concerning punishments and maintaining order. This classification demonstrates the comprehensive scope of Islamic fiqh, covering all aspects of life—spiritual, familial, economic, political, and penal. The author draws parallels with modern legal categories to make the subject accessible to contemporary readers.

Key Terms
العبادات (al-‘ibādāt): Rulings related to acts of worship of Allah, such as prayer, fasting, etc.
الأحوال الشخصية (al-aḥwāl al-shakhṣiyya): Modern term for family law, covering marriage, divorce, lineage, maintenance, bequests, inheritance.
المعاملات (al-mu‘āmalāt): Rulings related to financial transactions, contracts, property, and dispute resolution.
الأحكام السلطانية (al-aḥkām al-sulṭāniyya): Rulings concerning the ruler's authority over the subjects and their reciprocal rights and duties.
العقوبات (al-‘uqūbāt): Rulings related to punishment of criminals and maintenance of internal order.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Personal status and Sultaniyya
D) Mu'amalat and 'Uqubat

Key Takeaway
Fiqh rulings are comprehensively categorized into seven groups covering worship, family, transactions, governance, and penal law, reflecting Islam's holistic approach to life.

Definition of Fiqh and Its Seven Divisions
Definition and classification of fiqh rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fiqh and list its seven divisions, identifying which category a given ruling belongs to.

Core Idea
Fiqh governs every aspect of life, from ritual worship to international relations, in seven distinct categories.

This lesson unpacks the foundational definition of fiqh as the set of practical legislated rulings. You will learn its sources—Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, and ijtihad—and the sevenfold classification that covers all human activity, from ibadat to adab. Understanding these categories is essential for any mufti to navigate the vast landscape of Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of fiqh
Extract the author's definition: 'مجموعة الأحكام العملية المشروعة في الإسلام'. Note the key elements: practical rulings, legislated in Islam.
This is the broad meaning of fiqh, not the narrower 'knowledge of detailed rulings'.
2
Recognize the sources of fiqh
The text mentions explicit Qur'anic text, Prophetic Sunnah, consensus of scholars, and deduction by mujtahids from textual evidences, principles, and objectives.
These are the four main sources of Islamic law in general.
3
List the seven divisions
Enumerate: 1) Ibadat, 2) Personal status (ahwal shakhsiyyah), 3) Mu'amalat (transactions), 4) Sultanic rulings (siyasa shar'iyya), 5) Penal laws (uqubat), 6) Siyar (international law), 7) Adab (ethics).
The author notes that civil law in modern terms comprises groups 2 and 3.
4
Note the contemporary equivalents
Map the classical divisions to modern legal categories: personal status and mu'amalat form civil law; sultanic rulings form administrative and constitutional law; siyar forms public international law.
This mapping helps modern application.
5
Relate to the Majalla
The author points out that the Ottoman Majalla only covered mu'amalat, not personal status. This is a practical limitation.
Knowing this helps avoid assuming the Majalla covers all civil matters.

Chapter One: Introduction, Definition, and Classification
We studied Islamic fiqh, and this is the second meaning of fiqh.
According to this, fiqh is defined as: the set of practical rulings legislated in Islam.
And their legality is known through explicit text in the Quran, or the statement of the Messenger (peace be upon him) and his Sunnah, or through the consensus of Muslim scholars, or through the deduction of mujtahid jurists, past and future, from the evidences of the Quranic texts and Prophetic Sunnah, and the principles and objectives of Sharia.
2/2 - Divisions of Fiqh Rulings:
These fiqh rulings are divided into seven groups:
1 - Rulings related to worship of Allah Almighty, such as prayer, fasting, and others, called: acts of worship (ibadat).
2 - Rulings related to the family, such as marriage, divorce, lineage, maintenance, bequests, inheritance, etc., called in today's language: personal status (ahwal shakhsiyyah).
3 - Rulings related to people's economic activity and dealings with each other in wealth and rights, and their transactions through contracts and other things, and the resolution of their disputes through judiciary, called: transactions (mu'amalat). From these two groups (2) and (3) is composed what is called in modern legal terminology: civil law.
4 - Rulings related to the authority of the ruler over the subjects, and the mutual rights and duties between them. Some jurists call them: sultanic rulings (ahkam sultaniyyah). They are of the type called: siyasa shar'iyya (Sharia-oriented policy). This group forms two distinct types of rights in modern legal terminology: administrative rights and constitutional rights.
5 - Rulings related to the punishment of criminals and maintaining internal order among people, called: penal laws (uqubat).
6 - Rulings that regulate the relationship of the Islamic state with other states, forming the system of peace and war, called: siyar (plural of sirah), and in modern terminology: public international law.
7 - Rulings related to ethics, decency, virtues and vices, called: etiquettes (adab).
Civil law usually consists of the rulings of the second and third groups, but our Majallat al-Ahkam al-Shar'iyya was limited to the third group, i.e., the transactions section only, excluding personal status.
3/2 - From the above, it is clear that Islamic fiqh is both a spiritual and civil system, because Islamic law came to regulate matters of religion and worldly life.
Hence, Islamic fiqh, even in its civil part (which is transactions), differs from positive civil laws (i.e., those that have no religious character, but are established by nations for themselves).
In those positive laws, there is no place for the concept of halal and haram, nor consideration of inner realities; rather, consideration is given to outward appearances and forms. Whatever can be enforced by law and decided by rulings is a valid right, and whatever cannot be enforced is not a right.
As for Islamic fiqh, due to the religious consideration in its structure, the concept of halal and haram is an inner companion that accompanies a person and calls to it in every action. Consideration in the attachment of rights is for realities, even though the judiciary necessarily proceeds on outward appearances.
Thus, if a person is granted a right based on an apparent cause while in reality he is not entitled, as if the witnesses were false, or the person awarded relies on a document whose amount he had actually received or the debtor had been discharged from, or he was awarded dismissal of his opponent's claim...
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is an introductory definition and classification of fiqh from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's work. The author begins by defining fiqh in its broad sense as the set of practical rulings legislated in Islam, drawing from the Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and ijtihad based on Sharia principles and objectives. He then provides a sevenfold division of these rulings: (1) ibadat (ritual worship), (2) personal status (family law), (3) mu'amalat (transactions and civil dealings), (4) sultanic rulings (public law), (5) penal laws, (6) siyar (international law), and (7) adab (ethical norms). Notably, he notes that civil law in modern terminology comprises groups 2 and 3, but the Ottoman Majalla only covered group 3. The author then contrasts Islamic law with positive secular law: positive law is purely formal and only concerned with external enforcement, whereas Islamic law incorporates the concepts of halal/haram and internal realities, maintaining a spiritual dimension even in civil matters. This distinction is crucial for understanding the unique nature of Islamic jurisprudence as both spiritual and civic. The author uses an example to illustrate that Islamic law, while judging based on external evidence, remains aware of internal truths; a judgment based on false testimony does not change the underlying reality.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): مجموعة الأحكام العملية المشروعة في الإسلام (the set of practical rulings legislated in Islam)
الأحوال الشخصية (al-ahwal al-shakhsiyyah): الأحكام المتعلقة بالأسرة من نكاح وطلاق ونسب ونفقة ووصايا وميراث (family law rulings: marriage, divorce, lineage, maintenance, bequests, inheritance)
السياسة الشرعية (al-siyasa al-shar'iyya): الأحكام المتعلقة بسلطان الحاكم على الرعية وبالحقوق والواجبات المتبادلة (rulings on ruler-subject relations and mutual rights/duties)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Into how many groups does the author divide the rulings of fiqh?
A) Five
B) Six
C) Seven
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is the comprehensive set of practical Islamic rulings, divided into seven categories covering all aspects of life.

Islamic Law vs Positive Law: The Role of Halal and Haram
Contrast between Islamic law and positive law, emphasizing halal/haram and inner realities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the fundamental difference between Islamic law and positive secular law regarding the concepts of halal/haram and internal realities.

Core Idea
Positive law judges only appearances; Islamic law judges both appearance and inner reality, guided by halal and haram.

In this lesson, we examine the sharp contrast drawn by the author between Islamic fiqh and man-made laws. You will understand why Islamic law cannot be reduced to mere enforceability and how the conscience of halal/haram permeates every ruling, even civil transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim about positive law
The author states that in positive law, there is no place for halal/haram or inner realities; only outward appearances and enforceability matter.
Positive law is purely formal.
2
Contrast with Islamic law's spiritual dimension
Islamic law incorporates the concept of halal/haram as an inner regulator, accompanying a person in every action. Rights are attached to realities, not just appearances.
This makes Islamic law a spiritual-civil system.
3
Analyze the example of false testimony
If a judgment is based on false witnesses, the outward ruling is valid, but the underlying reality (the wrong person gains a right) is not accepted by Islamic law.
The judiciary goes by the apparent, but the truth remains.
4
Derive the implication for fatwa and judiciary
A mufti must consider both the apparent and the real, advising people to adhere to the inner truth even if the court ruled otherwise.
This dual aspect is crucial for ethical guidance.

الباب الأول : تمهيد وتعريف وتقسيم
درست الفقه الإسلامي، وهذا هو المعنى الثاني للفقه.
وعلى هذا يعرف الفقه بأنه : مجموعة الأحكام العملية المشروعة في الإسلام.
وتعلم مشروعيتها بطريق النص الصريح في القرآن، أو بيان الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وسنته، أو بطريق إجماع علماء المسلمين، أو باستنباط الفقهاء المجتهدين سابقاً ولاحقاً من دلائل نصوص القرآن والسنة النبوية، وقواعد الشريعة ومقاصدها .
٢/٢ - أقسام الأحكام الفقهية :
وهذه الأحكام الفقهية تنقسم إلى سبع زمر :
١ - الأحكام المتعلقة بعبادة الله تعالى من صلاة وصيام وغيرهما، وتسمى : العبادات .
٢ - الأحكام المتعلقة بالأسرة من نكاح وطلاق ونسب ونفقة ووصايا وميراث إلخ، وتسمى بلغة اليوم : الأحوال الشخصية.
٣ - الأحكام المتعلقة بنشاط الناس الاكتسابي وتعاملهم بعضهم مع بعض في الأموال والحقوق وتصرفهم بالتعاقد، وغيره، وفصل منازعاتهم بالقضاء، وتسمى المعاملات ومن هاتين الزمرتين (٢) و (٣) يتألف ما يسمى في الاصطلاح القانوني الحديث : القانون المدني.
٤ - الأحكام المتعلقة بسلطان الحاكم على الرعية، وبالحقوق والواجبات المتقابلة بينهما، ويسميها بعض الفقهاء: الأحكام السلطانية، وهي من قبيل ما يسمى : السياسة الشرعية.
وهذه الزمرة تؤلف نوعين متميزين من الحقوق في الاصطلاح القانوني الحديث : الحقوق الإدارية والحقوق الدستورية.
٥ - الأحكام المتعلقة بعقاب المجرمين وضبط النظام الداخلي بين الناس، وتسمى : العقوبات.
٦ - الأحكام التي تنظم علاقة الدولة الإسلامية بالدول الأخرى، وتؤلف نظام السلم والحرب، وتسمى السير بكسر ففتح) جمع سيرة، وتسمى في الاصطلاح القانوني: الحقوق الدولية العامة
٧ - الأحكام المتعلقة بالأخلاق والحشمة والمحاسن والمساوىء، وتسمى : الآداب.
والقانون المدني يتكون عادة من أحكام الزمرتين الثانية والثالثة ولكن مجلة الأحكام الشرعية لدينا جاءت مقصورة على الزمرة الثالثة أي قسم المعاملات فقط، دون الأحوال الشخصية.
٣/٢ - مما تقدم يتضح أنّ الفقه الإسلامي هو نظام روحي ومدني معاً، لأنَّ الشرع الإسلامي جاء ناظماً لأمور الدين والدنيا.
ومن هنا افترق الفقه الإسلامي حتى في القسم المدني منه ـ وهو المعاملات - عن القوانين المدنية الوضعية أي) التي ليس لها صفة دينية، بل هي من وضع الأمم لنفسها).
ففي تلك القوانين الوضعية لا محل لفكرة الحلال والحرام ولا عبرة لبواطن الأمور، بل العبرة للظواهر والصور ; فما أمكن منه القانون وقضت به الأحكام كان حقاً سائغاً، وما لم يمكن منه فليس بحق.
أما الفقه الإسلامي فللاعتبار الديني في مبناه كانت فكرة الحلال والحرام فيه رقيباً باطنياً ترافق الإنسان وتنادي به في كل عمل. والعبرة في تعلق الحقوق للحقائق وإن كان القضاء يجري ضرورة على الظاهر.
فإذا قضي لإنسان بحق بناء على سبب ظاهر وكان في الواقع غير :محق كما لو كان الشهود كذبة أو كان المقضي له يستند إلى وثيقة قد قبض في الواقع مبلغها أو أبرأ المدين منه، أو قضي له برد دعوی خصمه
Chapter One: Introduction, Definition, and Classification
We studied Islamic fiqh, and this is the second meaning of fiqh.
According to this, fiqh is defined as: the set of practical rulings legislated in Islam.
And their legality is known through explicit text in the Quran, or the statement of the Messenger (peace be upon him) and his Sunnah, or through the consensus of Muslim scholars, or through the deduction of mujtahid jurists, past and future, from the evidences of the Quranic texts and Prophetic Sunnah, and the principles and objectives of Sharia.
2/2 - Divisions of Fiqh Rulings:
These fiqh rulings are divided into seven groups:
1 - Rulings related to worship of Allah Almighty, such as prayer, fasting, and others, called: acts of worship (ibadat).
2 - Rulings related to the family, such as marriage, divorce, lineage, maintenance, bequests, inheritance, etc., called in today's language: personal status (ahwal shakhsiyyah).
3 - Rulings related to people's economic activity and dealings with each other in wealth and rights, and their transactions through contracts and other things, and the resolution of their disputes through judiciary, called: transactions (mu'amalat). From these two groups (2) and (3) is composed what is called in modern legal terminology: civil law.
4 - Rulings related to the authority of the ruler over the subjects, and the mutual rights and duties between them. Some jurists call them: sultanic rulings (ahkam sultaniyyah). They are of the type called: siyasa shar'iyya (Sharia-oriented policy). This group forms two distinct types of rights in modern legal terminology: administrative rights and constitutional rights.
5 - Rulings related to the punishment of criminals and maintaining internal order among people, called: penal laws (uqubat).
6 - Rulings that regulate the relationship of the Islamic state with other states, forming the system of peace and war, called: siyar (plural of sirah), and in modern terminology: public international law.
7 - Rulings related to ethics, decency, virtues and vices, called: etiquettes (adab).
Civil law usually consists of the rulings of the second and third groups, but our Majallat al-Ahkam al-Shar'iyya was limited to the third group, i.e., the transactions section only, excluding personal status.
3/2 - From the above, it is clear that Islamic fiqh is both a spiritual and civil system, because Islamic law came to regulate matters of religion and worldly life.
Hence, Islamic fiqh, even in its civil part (which is transactions), differs from positive civil laws (i.e., those that have no religious character, but are established by nations for themselves).
In those positive laws, there is no place for the concept of halal and haram, nor consideration of inner realities; rather, consideration is given to outward appearances and forms. Whatever can be enforced by law and decided by rulings is a valid right, and whatever cannot be enforced is not a right.
As for Islamic fiqh, due to the religious consideration in its structure, the concept of halal and haram is an inner companion that accompanies a person and calls to it in every action. Consideration in the attachment of rights is for realities, even though the judiciary necessarily proceeds on outward appearances.
Thus, if a person is granted a right based on an apparent cause while in reality he is not entitled, as if the witnesses were false, or the person awarded relies on a document whose amount he had actually received or the debtor had been discharged from, or he was awarded dismissal of his opponent's claim...
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is an introductory definition and classification of fiqh from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's work. The author begins by defining fiqh in its broad sense as the set of practical rulings legislated in Islam, drawing from the Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and ijtihad based on Sharia principles and objectives. He then provides a sevenfold division of these rulings: (1) ibadat (ritual worship), (2) personal status (family law), (3) mu'amalat (transactions and civil dealings), (4) sultanic rulings (public law), (5) penal laws, (6) siyar (international law), and (7) adab (ethical norms). Notably, he notes that civil law in modern terminology comprises groups 2 and 3, but the Ottoman Majalla only covered group 3. The author then contrasts Islamic law with positive secular law: positive law is purely formal and only concerned with external enforcement, whereas Islamic law incorporates the concepts of halal/haram and internal realities, maintaining a spiritual dimension even in civil matters. This distinction is crucial for understanding the unique nature of Islamic jurisprudence as both spiritual and civic. The author uses an example to illustrate that Islamic law, while judging based on external evidence, remains aware of internal truths; a judgment based on false testimony does not change the underlying reality.

Key Terms
القانون الوضعي (al-qanun al-wad'i): Positive law: law enacted by human authority without religious character.
الحلال والحرام (al-halal wa al-haram): The lawful and the forbidden in Islam; central concepts in fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between Islamic law and positive law according to the passage?
A) Positive law is more just.
B) Positive law considers inner realities; Islamic law only cares about appearances.
C) Islamic law incorporates halal/haram and inner realities; positive law only cares about outward enforceability.
D) Both are the same.

Key Takeaway
Islamic law incorporates both the external judgment of courts and the internal reality of halal and haram, making it a complete spiritual-civil system.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
The Dual Nature of Islamic Rulings: Judicial and Religious
Explains the distinction between judicial and religious aspects of fiqh rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the judicial ruling (qada') and the religious ruling (diyanah) and apply this distinction to a fatwa scenario.

Core Idea
A court judgment binds in this world but does not change what is halal or haram in Allah's sight.

Islamic fiqh integrates spiritual and civil dimensions, leading to a unique dual perspective on rulings. While courts must judge by outward appearances, the true religious status of an action depends on reality and intention. This distinction is essential for a mufti, who issues fatwas based on diyanah, not merely on judicial decisions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two spheres
Recognise that fiqh rulings have two considerations: qada' (judicial) operating on outward evidence, and diyanah (religious) based on inner reality.
The same act may have different rulings in each sphere.
2
Examine the Hadith evidence
Understand the Prophet's hadith: 'I judge by what I hear; if I give someone something of his brother's right, it is a piece of the Fire.' This shows that judicial decisions do not alter the actual halal or haram.
The hadith is the basis for the distinction.
3
Apply to a concrete case
Consider a mistaken divorce: the word of divorce is uttered unintentionally. The judge rules it effective (qada'), but religiously (diyanatan) it does not occur because the intention was absent.
The mufti may advice the couple to remain married in the sight of Allah.
4
Understand the mufti's role
The mufti's fatwa is based on diyanah, not necessarily on the court's ruling. He must consider the real circumstances and intention.
This allows the mufti to guide people according to truth, even if it conflicts with a judicial outcome.

الفصل (۲) معنى الفقه وأقسام أحكامه
٦ - الأحكام التي تنظم علاقة الدولة الإسلامية بالدول الأخرى، وتؤلف نظام السلم والحرب، وتسمى السير بكسر ففتح) جمع سيرة، وتسمى في الاصطلاح القانوني: الحقوق الدولية العامة
۷ - الأحكام المتعلقة بالأخلاق والحشمة والمحاسن والمساوىء، وتسمى : الآداب.
والقانون المدني يتكون عادة من أحكام الزمرتين الثانية والثالثة ولكن مجلة الأحكام الشرعية لدينا جاءت مقصورة على الزمرة الثالثة أي قسم المعاملات فقط، دون الأحوال الشخصية.
٣/٢ - مما تقدم يتضح أنّ الفقه الإسلامي هو نظام روحي ومدني معاً، لأنَّ الشرع الإسلامي جاء ناظماً لأمور الدين والدنيا.
ومن هنا افترق الفقه الإسلامي حتى في القسم المدني منه ـ وهو المعاملات - عن القوانين المدنية الوضعية أي) التي ليس لها صفة دينية، بل هي من وضع الأمم لنفسها).
ففي تلك القوانين الوضعية لا محل لفكرة الحلال والحرام ولا عبرة لبواطن الأمور، بل العبرة للظواهر والصور ؛ فما أمكن منه القانون وقضت به الأحكام كان حقاً سائغاً، وما لم يمكن منه فليس بحق.
أما الفقه الإسلامي فللاعتبار الديني في مبناه كانت فكرة الحلال والحرام فيه رقيباً باطنياً ترافق الإنسان وتنادي به في كل عمل. والعبرة في تعلق الحقوق للحقائق وإن كان القضاء يجري ضرورة على الظاهر.
فإذا قضي لإنسان بحق بناء على سبب ظاهر وكان في الواقع غير :محق كما لو كان الشهود كذبة أو كان المقضي له يستند إلى وثيقة قد قبض في الواقع مبلغها أو أبرأ المدين منه، أو قضي له برد دعوی خصمه
بسبب التقادم أي مرور الزمان على الحق المدعى به، وكان الحق لا يزال - في ذمته، فإنَّ القضاء في أمثال ذلك - وإن اعتبر نافذاً من الوجهة المدنية عملاً بالظاهر ضرورة ـ لا يحل حراماً ولا يحرم حلالاً، لأنَّ الحِل والحرمة أن يكونا مستندين إلى سبب صحيح في نظر الشرع وفقهه. والأصل في ذلك قول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام: «إنما أنا بشر . وإنكم لتختصمون إليَّ، وعسى أن يكون بعضكم ألحن بحجته من الآخر، فأقضي له على نحو مما أسمع ؛ فمن قضيتُ له بشيء من حق أخيه فإنما أقطع له قطعة من النار فليأخذها أو ليتركها»(۱).
فالقضاء في مثل هذا متى استوفى شرائطه وبذل الحاكم جهده هو قضاء حق، لكن المقضي به ليس بحق وتبعته الدينية على المقضي له المبطل :
٤/٢ - حكم القضاء وحكم الديانة :
وعن هذا كانت أحكام المعاملات في الفقه الإسلامي ذات اعتبارين:
اعتبار قضائي، واعتبار دياني.
فالقضاء يحاكم العمل أو الحق بحسب الظاهر أما الديانة فإنما تحكم بحسب الحقيقة والواقع . فالأمر أو العمل الواحد قد يختلف حكمه في القضاء عنه في الديانة .
فمن طلق زوجته مخطئاً بأن جرى على لسانه لفظ الطلاق غير قاصد إليه، بل إلى لفظ آخر، يعتبر الطلاق منه واقعاً قضاء، أي يقضي القاضي بوقوعه عملاً بالظاهر، ولكنه لا يقع ديانة، فيفتيه المفتي بجواز بقائه مع امرأته فتوى معلقة على ذمته في زعم الخطأ
(1) أخرجه البخاري كتاب الحيل (٦٩٦٧) وانظر منه (٢٤٥٨) ، ومسلم : كتاب الأقضية ۳ ۳۳۷ (٤۱)، ومعنى الحنُ بحجته قال في «النهاية » ٤ : ٢٤١ ... أراد أن بعضكم يكون أعرف بالحجة وأفطن لها من غيره.
Chapter (2): The Meaning of Fiqh and the Divisions of Its Rulings. 6 - The rulings that organize the relationship of the Islamic state with other states, and form the system of peace and war, called 'siyar' (with kasra then fatha, plural of sira), and in legal terminology: public international law. 7 - The rulings concerning morals, modesty, virtues and vices, called: adab (etiquette). The civil law usually consists of the rulings of the second and third groups, but our book of Islamic legal rulings (al-Majallah) came limited to the third group, i.e., the section of transactions only, without personal status. It becomes clear from the above that Islamic fiqh is a spiritual and civil system together, because the Islamic Sharia came regulating matters of religion and worldly life. And from here Islamic fiqh differed even in its civil part — which is transactions — from man-made civil laws (i.e., those that have no religious character, but are set by nations for themselves). For in those man-made laws there is no place for the idea of halal and haram, and no consideration of inner matters, rather the consideration is for appearances and forms; so whatever the law enables and the rulings decide is a permissible right, and what it does not enable is not a right. As for Islamic fiqh, due to the religious consideration in its structure, the idea of halal and haram in it is an inner monitor accompanying a person and calling out to him in every action. And the consideration for the attachment of rights is to realities, even though the judiciary necessarily proceeds on the apparent. So if a right is adjudged for a person based on an apparent cause while in reality he is not entitled, such as if the witnesses were liars, or the person adjudged for relies on a document while he actually received its amount or released the debtor from it, or his opponent's claim was dismissed due to prescription (statute of limitations) while the right still existed in the debtor's liability, then the judgment in such cases — although it is considered effective from the civil point of view, acting on the apparent as a necessity — does not make lawful what is unlawful nor unlawful what is lawful, because halal and haram must be based on a valid cause in the view of Sharia and its fiqh. The basis for this is the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'I am only a human, and you bring disputes to me. Perhaps some of you are more eloquent in argument than others, so I judge in their favor according to what I hear. Whoever I give something of his brother's right, I am only giving him a piece of the Fire, so let him take it or leave it.' So the judgment in such cases, when it fulfills its conditions and the ruler exerts his effort, is a true judgment, but what is adjudged is not a right, and its religious burden is on the wrong party. Second/quarter: The Ruling of the Judiciary and the Ruling of Religion (Diyanah). Hence, the rulings of transactions in Islamic fiqh have two considerations: a judicial consideration and a religious (diyani) consideration. For the judiciary judges actions or rights according to the apparent, while religion (diyanah) judges according to reality and fact. So one action or matter may have a different ruling in the judiciary than in diyanah. For example, if a man divorces his wife mistakenly, such that the word of divorce escaped his tongue unintentionally, meaning he intended another word, then the divorce is considered effective judicially, i.e., the judge rules its occurrence acting on the apparent, but it does not occur religiously (diyanatan), so the mufti gives him a fatwa allowing him to remain with his wife, a fatwa tied to his conscience in the assumption of error. (Footnote 1: Narrated by al-Bukhari, Kitab al-Hiyal (6967) and see also (2458), and Muslim in Kitab al-Aqdiyah 3/1337 (41). The meaning of 'alhan bi-hujjatihi' is as in al-Nihayah 4:241... meaning some of you are more knowledgeable in argument and more astute than others.)
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy explanation of the dual nature of Islamic fiqh rulings. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by listing the categories of fiqh rulings: (6) international law (siyar) and (7) ethics (adab), then clarifies that the Majallah (Ottoman civil code) covers only transactions, not personal status. He then highlights a key difference between Islamic fiqh and man-made law: Islamic fiqh integrates a spiritual dimension with the civil dimension, so the concepts of halal and haram act as an inner monitor, and rights are based on reality (haqiqah), even though courts must judge by appearances (zahir). This leads to the core distinction: the same act can have a judicial ruling (qada') and a religious ruling (diyanah). For example, if a man utters a divorce word unintentionally, a judge rules it effective because the apparent evidence (the utterance) indicates divorce, but religiously it is not effective because no divorce was intended; thus the mufti can advise the couple that they remain married in the sight of Allah. The hadith of the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) is cited: judges must judge by apparent evidence, but the ultimate right is determined by reality. This is a foundational principle in Hanafi fiqh, especially in fatwa (iftaa), as it allows the mufti to give rulings based on actual intention and reality, separate from the formal court judgment.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qada'): The judicial ruling based on apparent evidence presented in court.
الديانة (ad-diyanah): The religious/conscientious ruling based on reality and intention, known only to Allah.
الحلال والحرام (al-halal wa al-haram): The categories of lawful and unlawful determined by Sharia, tied to inner reality not just external form.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man mistakenly utters the word of divorce intending another word, what is the ruling in qada' and diyanah?
A) It occurs in both qada' and diyanah.
B) It occurs in qada' but not in diyanah.
C) It does not occur in either.
D) It occurs in diyanah but not in qada'.

Key Takeaway
The judicial ruling (qada') is based on apparent evidence, while the religious ruling (diyanah) is based on reality; a mufti must distinguish between them.

Judicial Rulings and Religious Rulings: Qaḍā' and Diyāna
Distinction between qaḍā' and diyāna

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the judicial ruling (qaḍā') and the religious ruling (diyāna) and apply this distinction to scenarios involving contracts and family law.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling binds outwardly; the religious truth binds before God.

In Islamic law, not every legally enforceable ruling reflects the true religious reality. This lesson explores the Hanafi distinction between the judicial perspective (qaḍā')—which relies on apparent evidence—and the religious perspective (diyāna)—which considers the actual truth before God. Understanding this distinction is crucial for a mufti, who must look beyond the external to give fatwa that aligns with the reality.

Guided Path
1
Define the two concepts
Clearly articulate the definitions of qaḍā' (judicial ruling based on apparent evidence) and diyāna (religious ruling based on inner reality).
The Arabic terms are key.
2
Identify the primary proof
Read the hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ) cited in the text and explain how it supports the distinction.
The hadith warns that a judge may mistakenly give away someone's right.
3
Apply to the divorce example
Trace the textual example: a man mistakenly utters a divorce word. The judge rules divorce (qaḍā'), but the mufti says it did not occur (diyāna).
This shows the practical divergence.
4
Apply to the debt example
Follow the example of a creditor who was discharged but later sues and wins. The judge allows collection, but it is unlawful in conscience.
This illustrates the same principle in financial transactions.
5
Contrast the roles of judge and mufti
Identify that the judge only considers the apparent, while the mufti investigates reality and weighs both aspects.
This is the takeaway for iftaa methodology.

وكان الحق لا يزال - في ذمته، فإنَّ القضاء في أمثال ذلك - وإن اعتبر نافذاً من الوجهة المدنية عملاً بالظاهر ضرورة ـ لا يحل حراماً ولا يحرم حلالاً، لأنَّ الحِل والحرمة أن يكونا مستندين إلى سبب صحيح في نظر الشرع وفقهه. والأصل في ذلك قول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام: «إنما أنا بشر . وإنكم لتختصمون إليَّ، وعسى أن يكون بعضكم ألحن بحجته من الآخر، فأقضي له على نحو مما أسمع ؛ فمن قضيتُ له بشيء من حق أخيه فإنما أقطع له قطعة من النار فليأخذها أو ليتركها»(۱).
فالقضاء في مثل هذا متى استوفى شرائطه وبذل الحاكم جهده هو قضاء حق، لكن المقضي به ليس بحق وتبعته الدينية على المقضي له المبطل :
٤/٢ - حكم القضاء وحكم الديانة :
وعن هذا كانت أحكام المعاملات في الفقه الإسلامي ذات اعتبارين: اعتبار قضائي، واعتبار دياني. فالقضاء يحاكم العمل أو الحق بحسب الظاهر أما الديانة فإنما تحكم بحسب الحقيقة والواقع . فالأمر أو العمل الواحد قد يختلف حكمه في القضاء عنه في الديانة . فمن طلق زوجته مخطئاً بأن جرى على لسانه لفظ الطلاق غير قاصد إليه، بل إلى لفظ آخر، يعتبر الطلاق منه واقعاً قضاء، أي يقضي القاضي بوقوعه عملاً بالظاهر، ولكنه لا يقع ديانة، فيفتيه المفتي بجواز بقائه مع امرأته فتوى معلقة على ذمته في زعم الخطأ . وكذلك لو أبرأ أحد مدينه ولم يخبره، ثم ادعى عليه بالدين وكتم إبراءه وقضي له به فإنَّ له التنفيذ والاستيفاء قضاء لا ديانة.
وبناء على ذلك اختلفت في الأوضاع والترتيبات الشرعية مهمة القضاء عن مهمة الإفتاء، أي وظيفة القاضي عن وظيفة المفتي: فالقاضي يجري على الاعتبار القضائي للأعمال والأحكام، ولا ينظر إلى الاعتبار الدياني. أما المفتي فيبحث عن الواقع وينظر إلى الاعتبارين، فإن اختلف اتجاههما أفتى الإنسان بالاعتبار الدياني. ومن ثم يذكر الفقهاء في كثير من المسائل التي يصورونها أن الحكم فيها قضاءً كذا وديانةً بعكسه؛ كمن كان له دَيْن جحده المدين وعجز الدائن عن إثباته أمام القضاء، ثم ظفر بمال للمدين: فإنَّ الديانة تقر للدائن أن يأخذ منه قدر حقه دون إذن المدين أو علمه ولكن لو وصل الأمر إلى القضاء لا يقرُّ له هذا الأخذ لعدم إثبات حقه.
And the right still remains in his liability. Indeed, the judicial ruling in such cases—even if considered enforceable from a civil perspective, acting on the apparent [evidence] as a necessity—does not render lawful what is unlawful nor unlawful what is lawful, because lawfulness and unlawfulness must be based on a valid cause in the view of the Sharia and its fiqh. The basis for this is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'I am only a human being. You bring disputes to me, and perhaps one of you is more eloquent in his argument than the other, so I judge in his favor based on what I hear. Whoever I give something that is his brother's right, I only give him a piece of Hellfire; let him take it or leave it.' So the judgment in such a case, when its conditions are fulfilled and the ruler exerts his effort, is a valid judgment, but the thing adjudged is not a right, and its religious consequence lies on the wrongdoer who obtained it. 4/2 - The Ruling of the Judiciary and the Ruling of Religion: Because of this, the rulings of transactions in Islamic jurisprudence have two aspects: a judicial aspect and a religious aspect. The judiciary judges an action or a right based on the apparent [evidence], while the religious aspect judges based on the reality and the true state. Thus, a single matter or action may have a different ruling in the judiciary than in religion. For example, if a man divorces his wife mistakenly—i.e., the word of divorce fell from his tongue without intending it, but rather some other word—the divorce is considered to have occurred judicially, meaning the judge rules it occurred based on the apparent [evidence], but it does not occur religiously. The mufti gives him a fatwa that he may remain with his wife, a fatwa tied to his conscience based on the assumption of the mistake. Similarly, if someone discharges his debtor's debt without informing him, then later claims the debt and conceals the discharge, and judgment is given in his favor, then he may enforce and collect judicially but not religiously. Based on that, the functions of the judiciary and the function of issuing fatwas differ in the Sharia arrangements: the judge proceeds on the judicial consideration of actions and rulings and does not look at the religious consideration. As for the mufti, he investigates the reality and looks at both considerations; if their directions differ, he gives the person a fatwa based on the religious consideration. Hence, jurists often mention in many issues they describe that the ruling therein is such-and-such judicially and the opposite religiously; e.g., someone has a debt that the debtor denies, and the creditor cannot prove it in court, then later obtains some of the debtor's property: religiously, the creditor is allowed to take an amount equal to his right without the debtor's permission or knowledge, but if the matter reaches the judiciary, this taking is not upheld due to lack of proof of his right.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm explains a foundational principle in Hanafi legal theory: the distinction between the judicial ruling (qaḍā') and the ruling in conscience or before God (diyāna). The author establishes that a judge's ruling based on apparent evidence is valid and enforceable in this world, but it does not change the actual legal and moral status of the act in the sight of Allah. The proof is the hadith where the Prophet (ﷺ) warns that if he mistakenly gives someone a right belonging to another, it is like a piece of Hellfire. This means the judge's decision is binding outwardly, but the true entitlement remains with the rightful owner. This leads to the principle that every transaction has two aspects: judicial and religious. Examples are given: a man who mistakenly utters a divorce word—the judge rules divorce occurred, but the mufti tells him it did not happen religiously, so he may stay with his wife. Similarly, a creditor who was discharged but later sues and wins—he may take the money legally, but it is unlawful for him in conscience. The passage then contrasts the roles: the judge only looks at external evidence; the mufti investigates inner reality and gives fatwa based on religious truth. The example of a creditor taking from a debtor's property without court order is given: religiously permissible, but judicially not upheld if brought to court. This distinction is crucial for understanding Hanafi fatwa methodology and the limits of judicial authority.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qaḍā'): الحكم الذي يصدره القاضي بناءً على الظاهر من الأدلة والبينات
الديانة (al-diyāna): ما يعتقده المكلف في نفسه ويعامل الله به على حسب الواقع والحقيقة
المفتي (al-muftī): الذي يبين الحكم الشرعي بناءً على الواقع والاعتبار الدياني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if a person mistakenly utters a divorce word without intending divorce, what is the ruling?
A) The divorce occurs both judicially and religiously.
B) The divorce does not occur at all.
C) The divorce occurs judicially but not religiously.
D) The divorce occurs religiously but not judicially.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the judicial ruling (qaḍā') is based on apparent evidence and is binding in this world, but it does not determine the religious ruling (diyāna), which is based on inner reality and governs one's standing before God.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
beginner

The Quran as the Primary Source of Islamic Jurisprudence
Introducing the four sources and explaining the Quran's role as the primary source

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Quran is considered the primary source in Hanafi usul and state the relationship between its general texts and the Sunnah's detailing role.

Core Idea
The Quran provides general principles; the Sunnah supplies details, enabling Sharia's flexibility across time.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational role of the Quran in Islamic jurisprudence. The passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa outlines the four primary sources and then focuses on the Quran, describing it as the 'constitution' of Islam. You will learn how the Quran's general (ijmali) style is intentional and how it works together with the Sunnah to produce a living, adaptable legal system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Sources
Recall from the passage that the scholars enumerate four primary sources of fiqh: the Book (Quran), the Sunnah, Consensus (Ijma), and Analogy (Qiyas). These are the main springs from which legal rulings are derived.
2
Recognize the Quran's Constitutional Role
The Quran is described as the 'foundation' and 'constitution' of Islamic legislation. It lays down the basic principles and beliefs in detail, but for acts of worship and transactions, it provides general commands without specifics.
This is why the Sunnah is indispensable.
3
Understand the Quran's General Style (Ijmali)
The Quran generally does not go into detailed particulars except in a few areas like inheritance and li'an. This general style serves other Qur'anic purposes such as eloquence and allows the text to remain concise.
The term 'ijmali' here means general, summary statements.
4
Note Where the Quran Provides Detail
The passage notes exceptions: inheritance (mawarith), li'an, some hudud penalties, and mahram women in marriage. These are detailed directly in the Quran.
These exceptions are rare and deliberate.
5
Grasp the Advantage of Generality for Adaptability
The Quran's general texts allow for multiple interpretations and applications, making Sharia capable of keeping pace with changing circumstances without violating its core principles. Examples include the flexible form of shura (consultation) and the judiciary system.
This is a key feature for a 'living Sharia'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث
المصادر الأساسية للفقه
الكتاب - السنة - الإجماع - القياس

رأينا في الفصل الثاني السابق ف (١/٢) أن الفقه الإسلامي تكون أحكام مسائله مستندة إما إلى النص الصريح في القرآن أو في السنة النبوية؛ وإما إلى إجماع العلماء، وإما إلى استنباط المجتهدين من دلائل النصوص وقواعد الشريعة.
هنا عد العلماء مصادر الفقه أي منابعه التي يستقي منها، أربعة وهي الكتاب والسنة والإجماع والقياس.

المبحث الأول: الكتاب

فأما الكتاب وهو القرآن فإنه هو الأصل في التشريع الإسلامي؛ فقد بينت فيه أسس الشريعة وأوضحت معالمها في العقائد تفصيلاً، وفي العبادات والحقوق إجمالاً.
وهو في الشريعة الإسلامية كالدستور في الشرائع الوضعية لدى الأمم؛ وهو القدوة للنبي نفسه صلى الله عليه وسلم فمن بعده، ولذا كان هو المصدر التشريعي الأصلي.
غير أن الكتاب بصفته الدستورية إنما يتناول بيان الأحكام بالنص الإجمالي ولا يتصدّى للجزئيات وتفصيل الكيفيات إلا قليلاً، لأن هذا التفصيل يطول به ويخرجه عن أغراضه القرآنية الأخرى من البلاغة وغيرها.
فقد ورد فيه الأمر مثلاً بالصلاة والزكاة مجملاً، ولم يفصل فيهما كيفية ولا مقادير بل فصلتهما السنة بقول الرسول عليه السلام وفعله. وكذلك أمر القرآن بالوفاء بالعقود، ونصّ على حل البيع وحرمة الربا إجمالاً، ولكن لم يبين ما هي العقود والعهود الصحيحة المحللة التي يجب الوفاء بها، وما الباطلة أو الفاسدة التي ليست محلاً للوفاء؟ فتكفلت السنة أيضاً ببيان أسس هذا التمييز.
على أن القرآن قد تناول تفصيل جزئيات الأحكام في بعض المواضع أو المواضيع كما في المواريث وكيفية اللعان بين الزوجين، وبعض الحدود العقابية والنساء المحارم في النكاح.
ولهذا الإجمال في نصوص القرآن مَزيَّة هامة أخرى بالنسبة إلى أحكام المعاملات المدنية والنظم السياسية والاجتماعية فإنه يساعد على فهم تلك النصوص المجملة وتطبيقها بصور مختلفة يحتملها النص، فيكون باتساعه قابلاً لمجاراة المصالح الزمنية وتنزيل حكمه على مقتضياتها بما لا يخرج عن أسس الشريعة ومقاصدها.
وذلك كما ورد في القرآن من النص على الشورى السياسية دون تعيين شكل خاص لها فكانت شاملة لكل نظام حكومي يجتنب فيه الاستبداد، ويتحقق فيه تشاور واحترام صحيح لرأي أولي الرأي والعلم في الأمة، سواء أكان نظاماً جمهورياً، أو نظام خلافة دستورية، أو غير ذلك مما لا استثثار فيه لفرد أو لفئة، بحسب ما تمليه المصلحة العامة.
وكذلك أوجب القرآن إقامة العدل وإحقاق الحق بين الناس بنصوص عامة كثيرة، ولم يحدد طريقة القضاء الذي هو الأداة لذلك. فمن حيث العدد يمكن أن تكون المحاكم محاكم فرد أو محاكم جماعة، ومن حيث الدرجة يمكن أن يكون القضاء على درجة واحدة أو على عدة درجات (ر: ف ١١/٥).
Chapter Three: The Primary Sources of Fiqh: The Book, the Sunnah, Consensus, and Analogy. We saw in the previous second chapter (1/2) that the rulings of Islamic fiqh are based either on explicit text in the Quran or the Prophetic Sunnah, or on consensus of scholars, or on the deduction of mujtahids from the evidences of texts and the principles of Sharia. Here the scholars counted the sources of fiqh, i.e., its springs from which it derives, as four: the Book, the Sunnah, Consensus, and Analogy. The First Section: The Book. As for the Book, which is the Quran, it is the foundation of Islamic legislation; the foundations of Sharia have been explained in it and its features clarified in beliefs in detail, and in acts of worship and rights in general. It is in Islamic Sharia like the constitution in positive laws of nations; it is the model for the Prophet himself, peace be upon him, and those after him, and therefore it is the original legislative source. However, the Book, in its constitutional capacity, only deals with the explanation of rulings by general text and does not address particulars and details of modalities except rarely, because this detail would lengthen it and take it out of its other Qur'anic purposes such as eloquence and others. For example, the command for prayer and zakat came in it in general terms, and it did not detail how or amounts, but the Sunnah detailed them by the saying and action of the Prophet, peace be upon him. Likewise, the Quran commanded fulfillment of contracts, and stated the permissibility of sale and the prohibition of usury in general, but it did not explain what are the valid, permissible contracts and covenants that must be fulfilled, and what are the invalid or corrupt ones that are not subject to fulfillment? So the Sunnah also undertook to explain the bases of this distinction. However, the Quran has detailed some particular rulings in some places or topics, such as in inheritance, the method of li'an between spouses, some punitive hudud, and women who are mahram in marriage. This generality in the texts of the Quran has another important advantage regarding the rulings of civil transactions and political and social systems: it helps to understand those general texts and apply them in different forms that the text can bear, so that with its breadth it becomes capable of keeping pace with temporal interests and applying its ruling to their requirements without departing from the foundations of Sharia and its objectives. For example, what came in the Quran of the text on political consultation (shura) without specifying a particular form for it, so it included every government system that avoids despotism and in which consultation and genuine respect for the opinion of the people of opinion and knowledge in the nation are realized, whether it is a republican system, or a constitutional caliphate system, or other than that in which there is no exploitation by an individual or a group, according to what the public interest dictates. Likewise, the Quran has obligated the establishment of justice and the realization of right among people with many general texts, and it did not specify the method of judiciary which is the tool for that. So in terms of number, courts can be single-judge or multi-judge courts, and in terms of level, the judiciary can be single-level or multiple levels (see: 11/5).
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is an introductory explanation of the primary sources of Islamic jurisprudence and a detailed discussion of the Quran as the first and foundational source. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by recapping the four sources: Quran, Sunnah, Ijma (consensus), and Qiyas (analogy). He then focuses on the Quran, describing it as the 'constitution' of Islam, providing the broad principles and foundations. The author highlights a key characteristic: the Quran generally does not go into detailed particulars, especially in acts of worship and transactions. This generality is intentional, serving other Qur'anic purposes such as eloquence and, importantly, allowing flexibility for changing circumstances. The Sunnah complements the Quran by providing the necessary details, as seen in the rulings of prayer and zakat. The author also notes that despite this general tendency, the Quran does detail certain areas like inheritance and li'an. Finally, he discusses the advantage of this generality: it allows the Sharia to adapt to new situations while remaining within its principles, giving examples of political consultation (shura) and the judiciary system. This passage is typical of legal philosophy, establishing the epistemological weight of the Quran as the primary source (Zahir al-Riwayah by implication, as it's basic agreed-upon principle) and its relationship with the Sunnah.

Key Terms
الكتاب (al-Kitab): The Quran, the primary source of Islamic legislation.
السنة (al-Sunnah): The sayings, actions, and approvals of the Prophet Muhammad, serving as the second source and the explainer of the Quran.
الإجمالي (al-ijmali): General, summary statement leaving details unspecified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the Quran generally not provide detailed rulings for acts of worship and transactions?
A) Because the Prophet disliked lengthy laws.
B) To keep the Quran concise and serve other Qur'anic purposes like eloquence.
C) Because Muslims were expected to deduce everything through analogy.
D) Because the Quran was revealed gradually and details were added later.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the Quran is the primary source, providing foundational principles in general terms, while the Sunnah elaborates and specifies, allowing the Sharia to adapt to new circumstances.

The Necessity of the Sunnah and Its Definition
Explains need for Sunnah and defines it as transmission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the Quran requires the Sunnah for practical application, cite Quran 59:7 as evidence, and define Sunnah as the Prophet's sayings, actions, and approvals.

Core Idea
The Sunnah is the key to the Book — the Quran itself commands following the Prophet.

This passage from Al-Mudkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aam by Mustafa al-Zarqa addresses a fundamental question: How can the concise Quranic texts be applied in practice? The answer is the Prophetic Sunnah. Al-Zarqa explains that the Quran itself points to the Sunnah for details, then defines what Sunnah technically means. This is a cornerstone of Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem — Why the Quran needs the Sunnah
Recognize that Quranic texts are concise; they require clarification for modalities (kayfiyyat), quantities (kamiyyat), and limits (hudud) to be applied to specific cases.
The Quran gives general principles; the Sunnah provides the specifics.
2
Locate the Quranic evidence for the Sunnah's authority
Find the verse cited: Quran 59:7, 'And whatever the Messenger gives you, take it; and whatever he forbids you, abstain from it.' This is the primary textual proof for the binding nature of the Sunnah.
This verse is a general command to follow the Prophet.
3
Define Sunnah technically
State the three components: qawl (saying), fi'l (action), and taqrir (tacit approval). In this context, it is synonymous with hadith.
Taqrir is the Prophet's silence indicating approval.
4
Understand taqrir (approval)
Explain that taqrir occurs when an action or saying takes place in the Prophet's presence or he is informed of it, and he does not object. This implies permissibility or approval.
Hanafis consider taqrir as a type of Sunnah that can indicate various rulings depending on context.
5
Equate Sunnah with hadith in this passage
Note that al-Zarqa states that 'Sunnah' in this sense is synonymous with 'hadith', meaning all transmitted reports from the Prophet.
In later usul, 'hadith' often includes the chain of narration, but here the focus is on its content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (۳) المصادر الأساسية للفقه
وعلى كل بهذا الإجمال في نصوص الكتاب كانت هذه النصوص محتاجة إلى البيان بالسنة النبوية ليمكن تطبيقها في الكيفيات والكميات ولتعرف حدودها في الشمول والاقتصار وتنزل عليها جزئيات الحوادث والأعمال . لذلك جاء في القرآن إحالة عامة على السنة النبوية في هذه التفصيلات بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا آتَاكُمُ الرَّسُولُ فَخُذُوهُ وَمَا نَهَاكُمْ عَنْهُ فَانتَهُوا﴾ [٧/٥٩]. ومن ثم كانت السنة مفتاح الكتاب .

المبحث الثاني : السنة
۳/۳ - يطلق لفظ (السنة) على ما جاء منقولاً عن الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم من (1) قول أو فعل أو تقرير وهي بهذا المعنى مرادفة للفظ «الحديث».

(1) المراد من التقرير أن يصدر أمام النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فعل أو قول من إنسان، أو يُخبر به، فيعلمه الرسول ، ويقرّ الفاعل أو القائل أو الناقل على ذلك. ولا ينهاه ولا ينكر عليه، ولا يشير إلى أنه خلاف الأولى (ر: (الموقظة) للذهبي والتتمة الأولى في بيان السنة التقريرية ص ۹۸ - ۱۰۲ للشيخ عبد الفتاح أبو غدة).
Chapter (3): The Basic Sources of Fiqh. In summary, despite the conciseness of the Quranic texts, these texts were in need of clarification by the Prophetic Sunnah so that they can be applied in modalities and quantities, and so that their limits in inclusiveness and restrictiveness can be known, and so that the particulars of events and actions can be subsumed under them. Therefore, the Quran made a general referral to the Prophetic Sunnah regarding these details in His statement: 'And whatever the Messenger gives you, take it, and whatever he forbids you, abstain from it' [59:7]. Hence, the Sunnah is the key to the Book. Section 2: The Sunnah. 3/3 - The term 'Sunnah' is applied to what has been transmitted from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) of (1) a saying, action, or approval, and in this sense it is synonymous with the term 'hadith.' (1) The intended meaning of 'approval' is that an action or saying occurs before the Prophet (peace be upon him) from a person, or he is informed of it, and the Prophet knows of it and approves of the doer, speaker, or reporter thereof. He does not forbid it, nor reproach it, nor indicate that it is contrary to what is preferable (reference: al-Mawqidha by al-Dhahabi and the first supplement on explanatory Sunnah pp. 98-102 by Shaykh Abd al-Fattah Abu Ghuddah).
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is from Al-Mudkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aam by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, a modern work on the introduction to Islamic jurisprudence. The author begins by explaining why the Quran requires the Sunnah for its application. The Quranic texts are concise and general; they need the Sunnah to specify how to pray, how much to give in zakat, and what limits apply. He cites Quran 59:7 as the primary evidence for the authority of the Sunnah, calling it the 'key to the Book.' Then he defines 'Sunnah' technically as what is transmitted from the Prophet in terms of his sayings, actions, and tacit approvals (taqrir). This is equivalent to 'hadith.' The footnote clarifies that taqrir means the Prophet's silent approval of something said or done in his presence without objection. This is a standard Hanafi definition emphasizing that Sunnah is a source of legislation alongside the Quran.

Key Terms
السنة (as-sunnah): What is transmitted from the Prophet of saying, action, or approval; synonymous with hadith.
التقرير (at-taqrir): The Prophet's silent approval of an action or saying done in his presence without objection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Sunnah is indispensable for understanding and applying the Quran, and it consists of the Prophet's sayings, actions, and tacit approvals.

Two Meanings of Sunnah and Its Legislative Role
Distinguishes two meanings of Sunnah and explains conflict resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two meanings of Sunnah (transmitted report vs. actual practice) and explain how Hanafi scholars resolve contradictions between them.

Core Idea
Sunnah has two meanings: transmitted reports and actual practice; when they conflict, reconciliation is the first step.

In this passage, Dr. al-Zarqa deepens our understanding of the term 'Sunnah' by revealing that it can refer not only to verbally transmitted hadiths but also to the actual living practice that prevailed during the Prophet's era and the time of the Rightly Guided Caliphs. This distinction is crucial for Hanafi usul because it provides a mechanism for handling apparent conflicts between what is narrated and what was historically practiced.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of Sunnah
From the passage, extract the two distinct usages: (1) Sunnah as transmitted verbal reports (hadith) and (2) Sunnah as the actual practice (amal) of the prophetic era and the period of the Rightly Guided Caliphs.
The footnote illustrates this: Ibn Mahdi considered Malik an imam in both, but Sufyan only in hadith, al-Awza'i only in practice.
2
Explain the consequence of conflict
Understand what happens when a verbal hadith contradicts the established practice. The passage states that if both are authentically established, scholars first attempt reconciliation (tawfiq); if impossible, they use preference (tarjih) or abrogation (naskh).
This approach mirrors the handling of conflicting hadiths, but here the conflict is between a hadith and a practice.
3
Describe the legislative role of the Sunnah
The Sunnah is the second source after the Quran. It explains the Quran's summary (mujmal), clarifies ambiguous points, restricts absolute terms, and supplies rulings not mentioned in the Quran (e.g., grandmother's inheritance).
The Sunnah is independent yet subordinate: it derives from Quranic principles.
4
Recognize conditions for acceptance
Note that after the prophetic era, only hadiths meeting strict authenticity conditions are accepted for legislation. Scholars classified hadiths by authenticity.
This contrasts with the earlier reliance on continuous practice which does not require the same chain of narration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تُطلق على معنى الواقع العملي في تطبيقات الشريعة في عصر النبوة، أي الحالة التي جَرَى عليها التعامل الإسلامي في ذلك العصر الأول (۲). والفرق بين المعنيين في النتيجة أنه قد ينقل عن الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام حديث لفظي، ولكن يثبت للعلماء تاريخياً أن عمل الناس في عصر النبوة أو في امتداد زمن الخلفاء الراشدين كان جارياً على خلاف مدلول ذلك الحديث المنقول ؛ فيقال عندئذ جاء في الحديث (كذا) ولكن السنة على (كذا). وفي هذه الحال إذا كان كل من الحديث والسنة صحيح الثبوت يلجأ العلماء إلى التوفيق بين الحديث والسنة إن أمكن، أو إلى ترجيح أحدهما بأسباب مُرَجُحة إن لم يمكن التوفيق، كما إذا تعارض حديثان من قول الرسول صحيحا الثبوت فإنهما يعتبر أحدهما ناسخاً للآخر، فيبحث عن الناسخ والمنسوخ منهما. والسنة تلي الكتاب رتبة في مصدريَّة التشريع، من حيث إنَّ بها بيان مجمله، وإيضاح مشكله وتقييد مطلقه وتدارك ما لم يذكر فيه. فالسنة مصدر تشريعي مستقل من جهة، لأنها قد يَرِدُ فيها من الأحكام ما لم يرد في القرآن كميراث الجدة، فقد ثبت أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حكم بتوريث جدة المتوفى سدس المال ولكن السنة من جهة أخرى يلحظ فيها معنى التبعية للقرآن لأنها، علاوة على كونها بياناً وإيضاحاً له، تخرج عن مبادئه وقواعده العامة حتى فيما تقرره من الأحكام التي لم يرد ذكرها في القرآن فمرجع السنة في الحقيقة إلى نصوص القرآن وقواعده العامة . والسنة بصورة عامة ضرورية لفهم الكتاب لا يمكن أن يستغنى عنها في فهمه وتطبيقه وإن كان فيها ما لا يتوقف عليه فهم الكتاب هذا التوقف . هذا وبعد أن أصبحت السنة تنقل نقلاً بالرواية، لانقضاء عصر الرسالة، وانقطاع مشافهة الرسول بوفاته عليه السلام، لا يقبل منها في تشريع الأحكام الفقهية إلا ما كان صحيح الثبوت بشرائط معينة شديدة. وقد تكفل علماء السنة بتمييز مراتب الأحاديث النبوية في الثبوت.

(۲) وعلى هذا المعنى يقع مثل قول عبد الرحمن بن مَهْدي وقد سُئِل عن سفيان الثوري والأوزاعي ومالك : سفيان إمام في الحديث وليس بإمام في السنة، والأوزاعي إمام السنة وليس بإمام في الحديث ومالك إمام فيهما جميعاً» (ر: شرح موطأ مالك للزرقاني، ومقدمة تنوير الحوالك شرح الموطأ أيضاً للسيوطي، وكتاب «نظرة عامة في تاريخ الفقه الإسلامي للأستاذ علي حسن عبد القادر ص ١١٦).
And it [the term Sunnah] is sometimes applied to the meaning of the actual practice in the applications of Shariah in the era of prophethood, i.e., the state upon which Islamic dealings proceeded in that first era (2). The difference between the two meanings in result is that a verbal hadith may be transmitted from the Messenger (peace be upon him), but historically it is established for scholars that the practice of the people in the era of prophethood or extending to the time of the Rightly Guided Caliphs proceeded contrary to the implication of that transmitted hadith; then it is said: 'It has come in the hadith such-and-such, but the Sunnah is such-and-such.' In this case, if both the hadith and the Sunnah [practice] are authentically established, scholars resort to reconciling between the hadith and the Sunnah if possible, or to preferring one over the other with preponderating factors if reconciliation is not possible, just as if two hadiths from the Prophet's sayings, both authentically established, conflict, then one is considered abrogating the other, so they search for the abrogating and abrogated among them. And the Sunnah follows the Book in rank as a source of legislation, in that by it its [the Quran's] summary is explained, its problematic points are clarified, its absolute is restricted, and what is not mentioned in it is supplied. So the Sunnah is an independent legislative source on one hand, because rulings may come in it that have not come in the Quran, like the inheritance of the grandmother – it is established that the Prophet (peace be upon him) ruled that the grandmother of the deceased inherits one-sixth of the estate – but on the other hand, the Sunnah is seen to have a sense of subordination to the Quran, because, besides being an explanation and clarification of it, it emerges from the Quran's general principles and rules, even in the rulings it establishes that are not mentioned in the Quran; for the reference of the Sunnah in reality is to the Quranic texts and its general rules. And the Sunnah in general is necessary for understanding the Book; it cannot be dispensed with in understanding and applying it, although there is in it what understanding the Book does not absolutely depend on. Now, after the Sunnah came to be transmitted by narration, due to the end of the era of prophethood and the cessation of direct communication from the Prophet by his death (peace be upon him), nothing is accepted from it for legislating fiqh rulings except what is authentically established according to certain strict conditions. And the scholars of hadith have undertaken to distinguish the ranks of Prophetic hadith in authenticity. (2) On this meaning falls the saying of Abd al-Rahman ibn Mahdi, when asked about Sufyan al-Thawri, al-Awza'i, and Malik: 'Sufyan is an imam in hadith but not an imam in Sunnah; al-Awza'i is an imam in Sunnah but not an imam in hadith; and Malik is an imam in both.' (Reference: Sharh Muwatta' Malik by al-Zurqani, introduction of Tanwir al-Hawalik also by al-Suyuti, and the book 'Nazrah 'Ammah fi Tarikh al-Fiqh al-Islami' by Professor Ali Hasan Abd al-Qadir, p. 116).
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the term 'Sunnah' by introducing a second meaning: the actual practice (amal) that was prevalent during the prophetic era and the time of the Rightly Guided Caliphs. This is a crucial distinction for Hanafi usul, as it acknowledges that an established practice may override a verbally transmitted hadith if the practice is authentically attributed to the Prophet's era. The author explains that when a hadith contradicts the established practice, scholars first try to reconcile them; if not, they apply principles of preference (tarjih) or abrogation (naskh). Then he discusses the legislative role of the Sunnah: it is subordinate to the Quran yet independent in providing rulings not found in the Quran, such as the grandmother's inheritance. He emphasizes that the Sunnah is essential for understanding the Quran, though some parts of it are not strictly necessary for that purpose. Finally, he notes that only authentically transmitted hadiths are acceptable for legislation, and scholars have graded hadiths accordingly. The footnote gives an example from early scholars distinguishing between expertise in hadith transmission and expertise in the actual sunnah (practice).

Key Terms
السنة العملية (as-sunnah al-‘amaliyyah): The actual practice of Shariah in the prophetic era and the period of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, which may differ from a transmitted hadith.
التوفيق (at-tawfiq): Reconciliation between a hadith and established practice or between two conflicting hadiths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Try to reconcile between them if possible

Key Takeaway
The Sunnah has two meanings: transmitted Sunnah and actual practice; when they conflict, reconciliation is prioritized.
